Instances vs. Concepts in Unity and Multiplicity
Commentator distinguishes real instances from concepts in unity-multiplicity discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the crucial distinction between conceptual opposition and accidental opposition in the context of unity and multiplicity in kalam.

Core Idea
Scholars meant real instances, not concepts, when discussing unity and multiplicity.

In this brief but important clarification, the commentator addresses a potential misunderstanding in earlier theological discussions. He explains that when scholars spoke of 'the many and the one,' they were referring to the actual instances (what the concepts apply to) rather than the abstract concepts themselves. This distinction resolves why the opposition is considered accidental, not essential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the key phrases: 'لا مفهوم الواحد والكثير' and 'بالعرض'.
Pay attention to how the commentator explains the intent of earlier scholars.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The passage contrasts two ways of understanding 'the one and the many': as concepts vs. as real instances. Write down what each would imply for the claim about opposition.
Concepts would be essentially opposed; instances are accidentally opposed.
3
Understand the analogy
The commentator uses the analogy of 'measure and measured' to explain accidental opposition. Reflect on how a measure and what it measures are related accidentally, not essentially.
This analogy clarifies the accidental nature of the opposition.
4
Connect to the broader discussion
Ask yourself why this distinction matters. How does it resolve apparent contradictions in the original text? The commentary shows that the earlier scholars' statement is consistent when understood correctly.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال : ( ولا يبعد أنهم أرادوا الكثير والواحد منه، لا مفهوم الواحد والكثير يعني أنه لا يبعد أن يكون مرادهم بقولهم : لا تقابل بين الوحدة والكثرة بالذات، أنه لا تقابل بين الكثرة والوحدة التي هي جزؤها إلا بالعرض، من حيث المكيالية والمكيلية كما تقرر لا أنه تقابل بالذات بين مفهومي الوحدة والكثرة.
Then he said: 'And it is not unlikely that they intended the many and the one from it, not the concept of the one and the many.' Meaning: It is not unlikely that their intent by their statement 'There is no essential opposition between unity and multiplicity' is that there is no opposition between multiplicity and the unity that is a part of it except accidentally, in terms of the measure and the measured as established, not that there is essential opposition between the concepts of unity and multiplicity.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the author's earlier statement. The commentator explains that when the author says 'they intended the many and the one', 'they' refers to earlier scholars, and 'the many and the one' means the real instances (what the concepts apply to), not the concepts themselves. Thus, the claim 'there is no essential opposition between unity and multiplicity' means that between multiplicity and the unity that is a part of it (i.e., the unity inherent in the many), the opposition is accidental, similar to the relationship between a measure and what is measured. It is not an essential opposition between the concepts of unity and multiplicity. This distinction resolves apparent contradictions in the discussion.

Key Terms
الكثير
الواحد
المفهوم
ما صدق
بالعرض
بالمكيالية والمكيلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that when scholars deny essential opposition between unity and multiplicity, they are talking about the concepts.
Correction: The commentator clarifies they meant real instances, not concepts. The opposition in reality is accidental, not essential.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what did the earlier scholars intend by 'the many and the one'?
A) The concepts of unity and multiplicity.
B) The real instances of unity and multiplicity.
C) The numerical values one and many.
D) The categories of substance and accident.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between concepts and real instances is crucial: the claim of no essential opposition refers to accidental opposition between instances, not essential opposition between concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold strongly. Are you clinging to a concept of that belief, or to the reality it points to? How might confusing the two lead you to oppose something that is only accidentally different? Commit to examining your beliefs for this confusion.
?
The commentator uses the analogy of measure and measured. Consider a disagreement you have with someone else—is it possible that you are both right in essence but only opposed accidentally, in terms of different perspectives? How would viewing it as accidental change your response? Resolve to test this in a current conflict.
?
The lesson distinguishes between essential and accidental opposition. Honestly, which dominates your inner life: a focus on essential unity with God (tawḥīd) or accidental differences in matters of practice? Where do you need to reorient yourself today toward the essential?

Three Types of Opposition Between Unity and Multiplicity
Classification of three types of opposition between unity and multiplicity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of opposition between unity and multiplicity—essential, accidental, and by contradiction—and how the perspective (concept vs. instance) determines the type.

Core Idea
Opposition between unity and multiplicity depends on whether we consider concepts or real subjects.

This lesson presents a nuanced classification from the author. It shows that the relationship between unity and multiplicity is not simple—it changes based on whether we consider their meanings or their actual manifestations in the world. The passage provides a threefold distinction that clarifies seemingly contradictory statements about these fundamental concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: first a general condition, then two subcases within the instance-based consideration.
The passage is a quotation from the author, so the commentator is presenting the author's own words.
2
Identify the three types of opposition
The passage lists three types: essential (by negation and affirmation), accidental (when unity is a part of multiplicity), and by contradiction (when one annuls the other in a subject). Mark each type with its distinguishing condition.
Essential opposition deals with concepts; the other two deal with instances (what they apply to).
3
Understand the water examples
The examples illustrate opposition by contradiction: multiple waters poured into one jar (multiplicity annuls unity) and one water poured into multiple vessels (unity annuls multiplicity). Reflect on how these show the property of contraries.
The key is that the subject (the water mass) is the same; the opposite state arises and negates the previous state.
4
Connect to the broader kalam context
Consider why this classification matters: it prevents confusion when discussing unity and multiplicity in arguments about the divine attributes or creation.
This passage refines the language of opposition, showing that not all opposition is essential.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد نقل عنه أنه قال : إن اعتبر التقابل بين مفهوميهما فهو تقابل ذاتي بالسلب والإيجاب والوحدة كما ذكر في الكتاب. وإن اعتبر بين ما صدقتا عليه، فإما أن يعتبر بين الكثرة والوحدة التي هي جزؤها، فهو تقابل بالعرض، كما هو المشهور. وإن اعتبر بين الكثرة والوحدة التي تطرأ على موضوع الكثرة فتبطلها وتنفيها، كالمياه المتعددة إذا صبت في جرة أو بين الوحدة والكثرة الطارئة على موضوع الوحدة النافية إياها، كماء واحد صب في أوان متعددة، فهو تقابل بالتضاد؛ لأن شأن الضد إذا ورد على محل الآخر أن يبطله وينفيه وشأن الوحدة والكثرة
And it is narrated from him that he said: If the opposition is considered between their two concepts, then it is an essential opposition by negation and affirmation, and unity as mentioned in the book. And if it is considered between what they apply to, then either it is considered between multiplicity and the unity that is a part of it, then it is an accidental opposition, as is well-known. And if it is considered between multiplicity and the unity that supervenes upon the subject of multiplicity and annuls it and negates it, like multiple waters when poured into a jar, or between unity and the multiplicity that supervenes upon the subject of unity negating it, like one water poured into multiple vessels, then it is an opposition by contradiction; because the property of a contrary is that when it comes upon the locus of the other, it annuls it and negates it, and the property of unity and multiplicity
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage is a quotation from the author, presented by the commentator. It provides a threefold classification of the opposition between unity and multiplicity. First, if we consider the concepts (māfūhīm) of unity and multiplicity, the opposition is essential (dhātī) by affirmation and negation (salb wa ījāb). Second, if we consider the real instances (mā ṣadaqat ʿalayhi), there are two subcases: (a) when we consider multiplicity and the unity that is a part of it (i.e., the unity that each individual member has), the opposition is accidental (bil-ʿaraḍ), as is well-known. (b) When we consider multiplicity that supervenes on a subject of unity and annuls it (e.g., multiple waters poured into a single jar) or unity that supervenes on a subject of multiplicity and annuls it (e.g., one water poured into multiple vessels), then the opposition is by contradiction (taḍādd). This is because a contrary (ḍidd) annuls its opposite when it arises in the same subject. The passage ends by beginning to explain why this is the case.

Key Terms
تقابل
ذاتي
بالسلب والإيجاب
بالعرض
بالتضاد
موضوع
ضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All opposition between unity and multiplicity is essential.
Correction: The passage shows that the type of opposition depends on whether we consider concepts or real subjects. When considering instances, the opposition can be accidental (if unity is part of multiplicity) or by contradiction (if one annuls the other in the same subject).

Checkpoint
When is the opposition between unity and multiplicity considered to be by contradiction (taḍādd)?
A) When considering the concepts alone.
B) When considering multiplicity and the unity that is its part.
C) When unity supervenes on a subject of multiplicity and annuls it.
D) When the instances are considered.

Key Takeaway
The opposition between unity and multiplicity is not monolithic; it must be classified as essential, accidental, or by contradiction depending on whether we consider concepts or real subjects and how they relate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the oneness of Allah (tawḥīd), do you ever confuse accidental opposition with essential opposition in your understanding of His attributes? How can this classification help you avoid thinking of God's attributes as contradictory when they are only accidentally opposed?
?
Consider a time when you saw two seemingly conflicting truths in your faith (e.g., divine mercy and justice). Using the categories from this lesson, is that opposition essential, accidental, or by contradiction? What does that mean for how you hold both truths together?
?
The passage gives an example of multiple waters becoming one in a jar. In your own life, where do you try to unify competing desires or beliefs? Are you aware of the type of opposition between them, and does that change how you approach resolution?

The Part-Whole Identity: Essential Annihilation of Multiplicity
The commentator defends the annihilation of parts as identical to annihilation of whole, resolving an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why removing the part is identical to removing the whole in the context of unity and multiplicity, and how this resolves an objection about accidental annihilation.

Core Idea
Removing the part is removing the whole itself — not just the necessary concomitant.

This passage addresses a subtle objection: when unity supervenes on multiplicity, does it annihilate multiplicity essentially or only accidentally? The objection argues that unity only annuls constituent unities (parts), so multiplicity is destroyed only accidentally. The commentator responds by clarifying the relationship between part and whole: annulling the parts is identical to annulling the whole, not like removing a necessary concomitant.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the passage and find the objection that unity only annuls parts, not the whole essentially.
The objection claims that annihilation of multiplicity is accidental.
2
Understand the rebuttal
The commentator says 'إبطال الوحدات المقومة عين إبطال الكثرة'. Reflect on why annulling parts is identical to annulling the whole.
This is the core argument.
3
Contrast with necessary concomitant
Notice the distinction between removing a part (which eliminates the whole) and removing a necessary concomitant (like heat from fire) which does not eliminate the subject.
The passage uses the terms اللازم and الملزوم.
4
Conclude the essential annihilation
Understand that because part and whole are identical in removal, the annihilation of multiplicity is essential, not accidental.
This resolves the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواردتين على محل واحد كذلك لا يقال : الوحدة إذا طرأت على محل لا تفني الكثرة بالذات بل تبطل الوحدات المقومة لها ثم يلزم من إبطالها إبطال الكثرة بالعرض، ومن شأن الضد أن يبطل ضده بالذات لا بالعرض؛ لأنا نقول: إبطال الوحدات المقومة عين إبطال الكثرة ؛ لأن رفع الجزء هو رفع الكل بعينه، بخلاف رفع الكل اللازم فإنه مستلزم لرفع الملزوم ، ولذلك أمكن أن يتصور رفع اللازم مع الملزوم، وإن كان المتصور محالاً. ولم يمكن أن يتصور رفع الجزء مع بقاء الكل، فإن التصور هاهنا محال، كالمتصور. بقي هاهنا بحث وهو أن طريان الوحدة على :
And they occur upon one subject. Likewise, it is not said: Unity, when it supervenes on a subject, does not annihilate multiplicity essentially but rather annuls the constituent unities, then from its annulling necessarily follows the annihilation of multiplicity accidentally, and the property of a contrary is to annihilate its opposite essentially not accidentally; for we say: The annulling of the constituent unities is identical to the annulling of multiplicity, because removing the part is removing the whole itself, unlike removing the necessary whole (lāzim), for then it necessitates removing the necessitator (malzūm). Therefore it is possible to conceive the removal of the necessary concomitant along with the necessitator, even if what is conceived is impossible. But it is not possible to conceive the removal of the part while the whole remains, for the conception here is impossible, like the conceived. There remains here a discussion: that the supervention of unity upon...
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a response to a potential objection against the claim that when unity supervenes on a subject of multiplicity, it annihilates multiplicity essentially. The objection states that unity only annuls the constituent unities (parts), and thus multiplicity is annihilated accidentally, not essentially, but a contrary (like unity) should annihilate its opposite essentially. The commentator answers that annulling the constituent unities is identical to annulling multiplicity because removing the part is the same as removing the whole (the whole is nothing more than the sum of its parts). This is different from removing a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of something, which only necessitates the removal of the necessitator (malzūm), but they are distinct. For example, fire's heat is necessary but removing heat does not remove fire necessarily. Here, removing a part necessarily removes the whole. Thus the annihilation is essential, not accidental. The passage concludes by noting another discussion: the supervention of unity on something.

Key Terms
إبطال
الوحدات المقومة
رفع الجزء
رفع الكل
اللازم
الملزوم
الكثرة
الوحدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Removing a part is only partial removal, not total; thus multiplicity is only accidentally annihilated.
Correction: Removing a part is identical to removing the whole because the whole is constituted by its parts; therefore annihilation is essential.

Checkpoint
What is the commentator's main argument when responding to the objection that unity annihilates multiplicity only accidentally?
A) Multiplicity is not affected by the annihilation of parts.
B) Annulling the constituent unities is identical to annulling multiplicity.
C) Unity cannot annihilate anything.
D) The objection is based on a false definition of unity.

Key Takeaway
Removing the part is removing the whole — thus unity annihilates multiplicity essentially, not accidentally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider a sin in your life, do you treat it as a removable part that leaves 'you' intact, or do you recognize that removing it changes your very identity before Allah? How would your repentance differ if you truly believed it was the whole you—not just a part—that needed transformation?
?
The passage distinguishes between a necessary consequence (like heat from fire) and a constitutive part (like a piece of the whole). In your worship, are your good deeds merely consequences of your faith, or are they constitutive of it? If you neglect a duty, does that weaken your faith as a removal of a part, or do you treat it as separate? Examine yourself honestly.

Why Quantitative Parts Are Specified
Explains the restriction to quantitative parts using water as example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author restricts parts to quantitative when discussing the one by continuity, and how water serves as an example despite containing heterogeneous essential parts.

Core Idea
Quantitative parts ensure homogeneous division like water despite heterogeneous essence.

This lesson examines a key restriction in the commentary: why the author specifies 'quantitative parts' when analyzing the one by continuity. The commentator clarifies that this allows water to serve as an example, even though water contains different essential parts like hyle and form. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the concept of continuity in Islamic philosophical theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the gist of the restriction.
Notice the author's precise wording: 'to quantitative parts' (ila ajza' miqdariyyah).
2
Identify the restriction and reason
The author limits parts to quantitative; the commentator explains why: to make clear the example of water, which divides into similar parts despite having different essential parts.
Water's essential parts (hyle and form) are heterogeneous, but its quantitative parts are similar.
3
Connect to 'at all'
The commentator extends the restriction to a previous statement: 'if it does not accept division into parts at all' refers specifically to quantitative parts.
Thus, non-division into quantitative parts is the criterion, which includes point, unity, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( إلى أجزاء مقدارية قيد الأجزاء بالمقدارية ليتضح تمثيل المنقسم إلى الأجزاء المتشابهة بالماء مع اشتماله على أجزاء الوجود المتخالفة في الحقيقة، أعني الهيولى والصورة وفيه إشارة إلى أن المراد بالأجزاء في قوله فإن لم يقبل القسمة إلى الأجزاء أصلا هو الأجزاء المقدارية أيضاً، فمعنى أصلاً أن لا يقبل القسمة إلى تلك الأجزاء لا حساً ولا حقيقة فلا يقدح في شموله للنقطة والوحدة والمفارق الشخصيات تركبها من الأجزاء المحمولة أعني الجنس والفصل، ولا كون التشخص جزءاً للأشخاص على تقدير القول بهذين التركيبين، لكن تفسيره الواحد الحقيقي فيما سيأتي بما لا يقبل الانقسام، لا بحسب الأجزاء المقدارية ولا بحسب غيرها ينافيه. اللهم إلا أن يقال : الواحد الحقيقي يطلق على معنيين ويؤيده ما سيذكره هناك، وبقي فيه شيء آخر وهو أن تقييد الأجزاء بالمقدارية يختل بالقياس إلى الواحد بالاجتماع، فإن مثل وحدة العشرة الجزئية ليست وحدة اتصالية بل اجتماعية على ما قيل، مع أنها غير منقسمة إلى أجزاء مقدارية غير متشابهة . اللهم إلا أن يقال هي منقسمة إليها نظراً إلى ظاهر انقسامها إلى الأربعة والستة مثلاً، وإن كانت غير منقسمة باعتبار الوحدات وهذا الانقسام الظاهري يكفي هاهنا، كما كفى اتصال الماء حساً على رأي مثبتي الجزء في الوحدة الاتصالية، أو يمنع كون العشرة من الواحد بالاجتماع.
His saying: (into quantitative parts). He restricts parts by 'quantitative' to make clear the example of what divides into similar parts, like water, despite its containing parts of existence that are different in reality, namely hyle and form. In it is an indication that what is meant by 'parts' in his saying 'if it does not accept division into parts at all' is also the quantitative parts. So the meaning of 'at all' is that it does not accept division into those parts, neither sensibly nor in reality. Thus it does not detract from its inclusion of the point, unity, and the separate individuals that they are composed of predicative parts, i.e., genus and differentia; nor does the fact that individuation is a part for individuals, assuming these two compositions. But his later interpretation of the true one as that which does not accept division, neither with respect to quantitative parts nor with respect to others, contradicts it. However, it could be said that the true one is applied to two meanings, and this is supported by what he will mention there. And there remains another point: that the restriction of parts to quantitative is problematic with respect to the one by aggregation (al-wahid bi al-ijtima'), for the unity of ten as a part is not a continuity unity but an aggregation unity, as has been said, even though it is not divided into quantitative parts that are dissimilar. However, it could be said that it is divided into them by virtue of its apparent division into four and six, for example, even if it is not divided in terms of the units, and this apparent division is sufficient here, just as the sensible continuity of water sufficed according to the opinion of the affirmers of the part in the continuity unity; or one could deny that ten is of the one by aggregation.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage comments on the phrase 'into quantitative parts' (ila ajza' miqdariyyah). It explains that the author restricted parts to quantitative to clarify the example of water, which divides into similar parts despite containing different essential parts (hyle and form). The commentary also notes that when the author said 'if it does not accept division at all,' he meant quantitative parts; so non-division into those parts is the criterion. It then addresses a potential conflict: later the author interprets the true one (al-wahid al-haqiqi) as not accepting division into any parts, which would contradict this restriction. The commentator suggests that 'true one' may have two meanings, and the author will clarify later. Another issue is that restricting to quantitative parts creates a problem for the one by aggregation (al-wahid bi al-ijtima') like ten, which is not divisible into quantitative parts. The commentator proposes that ten can be considered divisible into smaller numbers like four and six in an apparent sense, which is sufficient for the classification, just as sensible continuity sufficed for water according to the affirmers of the part. Alternatively, ten might not be considered a true one by aggregation.

Key Terms
أجزاء مقدارية
الهيولى
الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The one by continuity requires its essential parts to be homogeneous.
Correction: No, continuity depends on quantitative parts being similar, not on essential parts. Water is an example where essential parts (hyle and form) are different but quantitative parts are similar.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator restrict parts to quantitative in the division of the one by continuity?
A) To exclude the point and separate individuals
B) To allow water as an example despite its heterogeneous essential parts
C) To match the later definition of the true one
D) To include the one by aggregation

Key Takeaway
The one by continuity is defined by the similarity of its quantitative parts, not the uniformity of its essential parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that water is considered one by continuity because its quantitative parts are similar, even though its essential parts are different. In your own life, you may feel that your outward actions (like prayer and charity) are consistent, but your inner state (like doubts or desires) seems conflicted. Does this passage give you hope that Allah still sees your faith as fundamentally unified? What would it take for you to become truly 'one by continuity' in both action and intention? Are you willing to work on the parts that are out of alignment?
?
The passage distinguishes between quantitative parts and essential parts. Is your identity as a Muslim based more on outward continuity (consistency in rituals and behavior) or on the inner essential reality of your faith? Which one do you think Allah values more? What steps will you take today to ensure your 'essence' matches your 'appearance'?
?
The commentator says that the point, unity, and separate individuals are still true ones despite being composed of predicative parts. Reflect: Do you ever feel that your own complexity—your multiple roles, thoughts, and struggles—makes you less of a true believer in Allah's sight? How does this lesson challenge that feeling? What would it mean to accept yourself as a unified servant of Allah despite your internal diversity?

Resolving Contradictions with the True One and the One by Aggregation
Addresses two challenges: contradiction with later definition and problem with one by aggregation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator addresses a potential contradiction with the later definition of the true one and how he resolves a problem with the one by aggregation (such as ten) regarding division into quantitative parts.

Core Idea
The true one has two meanings; ten can be seen as quantitatively divisible in an apparent sense.

This lesson tackles two difficulties arising from the restriction to quantitative parts: first, it seems to conflict with a later statement about the true one being indivisible into any parts; second, it appears problematic for the one by aggregation, like ten, which is not divisible into quantitative parts. The commentator offers resolutions for both, showing the depth of this classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the two issues and their resolutions.
Pay attention to the structure: first a contradiction, then a problem.
2
Analyze the first issue
The commentator notes that the later definition of the true one (indivisible into any parts) contradicts the earlier restriction. The resolution is that 'true one' has two meanings.
This is a common technique in Islamic theology to resolve apparent contradictions.
3
Analyze the second issue
The one by aggregation, like the number ten, is not divisible into quantitative parts. The commentator offers two possible solutions: either consider an apparent division (into four and six) or deny that ten is truly a one by aggregation.
This shows the flexibility of the classification system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( إلى أجزاء مقدارية قيد الأجزاء بالمقدارية ليتضح تمثيل المنقسم إلى الأجزاء المتشابهة بالماء مع اشتماله على أجزاء الوجود المتخالفة في الحقيقة، أعني الهيولى والصورة وفيه إشارة إلى أن المراد بالأجزاء في قوله فإن لم يقبل القسمة إلى الأجزاء أصلا هو الأجزاء المقدارية أيضاً، فمعنى أصلاً أن لا يقبل القسمة إلى تلك الأجزاء لا حساً ولا حقيقة فلا يقدح في شموله للنقطة والوحدة والمفارق الشخصيات تركبها من الأجزاء المحمولة أعني الجنس والفصل، ولا كون التشخص جزءاً للأشخاص على تقدير القول بهذين التركيبين، لكن تفسيره الواحد الحقيقي فيما سيأتي بما لا يقبل الانقسام، لا بحسب الأجزاء المقدارية ولا بحسب غيرها ينافيه. اللهم إلا أن يقال : الواحد الحقيقي يطلق على معنيين ويؤيده ما سيذكره هناك، وبقي فيه شيء آخر وهو أن تقييد الأجزاء بالمقدارية يختل بالقياس إلى الواحد بالاجتماع، فإن مثل وحدة العشرة الجزئية ليست وحدة اتصالية بل اجتماعية على ما قيل، مع أنها غير منقسمة إلى أجزاء مقدارية غير متشابهة . اللهم إلا أن يقال هي منقسمة إليها نظراً إلى ظاهر انقسامها إلى الأربعة والستة مثلاً، وإن كانت غير منقسمة باعتبار الوحدات وهذا الانقسام الظاهري يكفي هاهنا، كما كفى اتصال الماء حساً على رأي مثبتي الجزء في الوحدة الاتصالية، أو يمنع كون العشرة من الواحد بالاجتماع.
His saying: (into quantitative parts). He restricts parts by 'quantitative' to make clear the example of what divides into similar parts, like water, despite its containing parts of existence that are different in reality, namely hyle and form. In it is an indication that what is meant by 'parts' in his saying 'if it does not accept division into parts at all' is also the quantitative parts. So the meaning of 'at all' is that it does not accept division into those parts, neither sensibly nor in reality. Thus it does not detract from its inclusion of the point, unity, and the separate individuals that they are composed of predicative parts, i.e., genus and differentia; nor does the fact that individuation is a part for individuals, assuming these two compositions. But his later interpretation of the true one as that which does not accept division, neither with respect to quantitative parts nor with respect to others, contradicts it. However, it could be said that the true one is applied to two meanings, and this is supported by what he will mention there. And there remains another point: that the restriction of parts to quantitative is problematic with respect to the one by aggregation (al-wahid bi al-ijtima'), for the unity of ten as a part is not a continuity unity but an aggregation unity, as has been said, even though it is not divided into quantitative parts that are dissimilar. However, it could be said that it is divided into them by virtue of its apparent division into four and six, for example, even if it is not divided in terms of the units, and this apparent division is sufficient here, just as the sensible continuity of water sufficed according to the opinion of the affirmers of the part in the continuity unity; or one could deny that ten is of the one by aggregation.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage comments on the phrase 'into quantitative parts' (ila ajza' miqdariyyah). It explains that the author restricted parts to quantitative to clarify the example of water, which divides into similar parts despite containing different essential parts (hyle and form). The commentary also notes that when the author said 'if it does not accept division at all,' he meant quantitative parts; so non-division into those parts is the criterion. It then addresses a potential conflict: later the author interprets the true one (al-wahid al-haqiqi) as not accepting division into any parts, which would contradict this restriction. The commentator suggests that 'true one' may have two meanings, and the author will clarify later. Another issue is that restricting to quantitative parts creates a problem for the one by aggregation (al-wahid bi al-ijtima') like ten, which is not divisible into quantitative parts. The commentator proposes that ten can be considered divisible into smaller numbers like four and six in an apparent sense, which is sufficient for the classification, just as sensible continuity sufficed for water according to the affirmers of the part. Alternatively, ten might not be considered a true one by aggregation.

Key Terms
الواحد الحقيقي
الواحد بالاجتماع
أجزاء مقدارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The one by aggregation must be divisible into quantitative parts.
Correction: No, a one by aggregation like ten is not quantitatively divisible, but an apparent division (e.g., into four and six) can suffice for classification.

Checkpoint
How does the commentator resolve the contradiction between the restriction to quantitative parts and the later definition of the true one?
A) By reinterpreting the earlier restriction
B) By saying that the true one has two meanings
C) By denying the later definition
D) By excluding the true one from the discussion

Key Takeaway
The commentator resolves apparent contradictions by allowing multiple meanings and considering apparent division, showing the flexibility of the classification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentator allows for multiple meanings of 'true one' to resolve a contradiction. Is there a contradiction in your own understanding of tawhid (oneness of God) that you have tried to force into a single rigid meaning? What would it cost you to accept that multiple valid meanings exist?
?
In the passage, an apparent division of ten into four and six is accepted as sufficient for classification. Think of a time in your life when you insisted on a perfect, indivisible unity (e.g., in a relationship, community, or goal) and rejected any apparent division as a failure. How might accepting an 'apparent division' have actually preserved the deeper unity?
?
The commentator denies the premise that ten is a one by aggregation. When have you clung to a problematic classification (like labeling something as 'one by aggregation') rather than simply denying that it belongs to that category? What real-world belief or attachment might you need to let go of for the sake of intellectual consistency?

The Three Impossibilities of Union
Clarification via case analysis of union's impossibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why union (itihad) between two distinct things is impossible in all three logical scenarios.

Core Idea
Union is impossible whether both things perish, one perishes, or both remain.

This passage from Sanusi's work uses a 'search and eliminate' method to show that the concept of two things becoming one is rationally impossible. He examines every possible outcome of a supposed union and shows that none of them actually produce a true union. The argument is not a theoretical proof but a clarification of an intuitive necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the three conditional clauses starting with 'إن'.
The translation is literal; you may need to read it slowly.
2
Identify each case
Find the three cases: (1) both identities cease, (2) only one ceases, (3) both remain. See how each is shown to be incompatible with union.
3
Study the response to the misconception
The passage ends by addressing a misunderstanding: some thought this was a theoretical proof and objected to the third case. Notice how the response clarifies the assumption.
This shows the author is aware of potential objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فيقال : إن عدم الهويتان بعد الاتحاد وحدث أمر غيرهما ، فلا اتحاد بينهما، بل هما قد عدما ، وحدث هناك أمر ثالث غيرهما . وإن عدم أحدهما فقط فلا اتحاد أيضاً؛ إذ لا يتحد المعدوم بالموجود بديهةً، وإلا كان موجوداً و معدوماً معاً . وإن وجدا أي بقيا موجودين بعد الاتحاد، فهما بعده اثنان متغایران كما كانا كذلك قبله فلا اتحاد أيضاً والغرض من هذا الكلام، هو التنبيه على الضرورة بتجريد الطرفين وتصوير المراد على الوجه الذي هو الحكم. وظن بعض الناس أنهم حاولوا بهذا الكلام الاستدلال على مطلوب نظري فيمنع امتناع الاتحاد على تقدير بقائهما موجودين . وإنما يكونان اثنين لو لم يتحدا أي لا نسلم أنهما لو كانا بعد الاتحاد موجودين لكانا اثنين لا واحداً، وإنما يكونان كذلك لو لم يكن كل منهما موجوداً متحداً بالموجود الآخر، وهو ممنوع .
It is said: If the two identities cease to exist after union and another thing occurs other than them, then there is no union between them; rather they have both ceased, and a third thing other than them has occurred. And if only one of them ceases, then also there is no union; for the non-existent cannot unite with the existent by necessity, otherwise it would be existent and non-existent simultaneously. And if they both exist (i.e., remain existent after union), then they are after it two distinct things as they were before, so there is also no union. And the purpose of this speech is to draw attention to the necessity by stripping the two sides and depicting the intended meaning in the manner that is the judgment. And some people thought that they attempted by this speech to prove a theoretical claim, so they deny the impossibility of union on the assumption that they remain existent. But they would be two only if they did not unite; i.e., we do not grant that if after union they were existent they would be two and not one, and they would only be as such if each of them was not existent united with the other existent, and that is denied.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a clarification (تنبيه) that demonstrates the impossibility of union by considering three cases. The argument proceeds: (1) If both identities cease after union and a third thing comes into being, there is no union; they have perished and a third thing arises. (2) If only one ceases, union is also impossible because a non-existent cannot unite with an existent—that would require it to be both existent and non-existent. (3) If both remain existent after union, they are still two distinct things as before, so no union occurred. Thus, union is impossible in all scenarios. The purpose is to make the necessity clear by abstracting the possibilities. Some people mistakenly thought this was a theoretical proof and objected that on the assumption both remain existent, union is possible. The response is that if they remained existent, they would be two only if they did not unite; but the assumption is that they did unite, so being two after union is not granted.

Key Terms
الاتحاد
العدم
الوجود
الاثنينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that if both things remain after union, they could still be united (i.e., become one while remaining existent).
Correction: If they remain existent, they are still two distinct things; true union would require them to become one, which is contradictory with remaining two.

Checkpoint
In the three-case clarification, what happens to the two things if union occurs?
A) They always become one identical thing.
B) Either both perish, one perishes, or both remain, but in no case does true union occur.
C) They always change into a third thing.
D) One always absorbs the other.

Key Takeaway
Union between two distinct things is impossible because every logical possibility results either in annihilation, contradiction, or continued duality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself mentally comparing God to something in creation, as if they share some 'union'? How does this logical impossibility of union challenge that subtle association?
?
If union between two distinct things is impossible, what does that mean for your heart's attempt to 'unite' love of God with love of worldly things? Is there an area where you are trying to merge contradictory loyalties?
?
The argument shows that union leads to absurdity. Are you tolerating any logical contradiction in your own faith or practices? What would it cost you to resolve it?

The Impossibility of Union: Three Exhaustive Cases
Al-Iji presents three exhaustive cases proving union impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why two distinct things cannot become one, based on the three exhaustive logical cases presented by al-Iji.

Core Idea
Union is impossible: three cases cover all possibilities, and none yield true union.

Al-Iji begins by stating that the impossibility of two things becoming one is a necessary judgment. He then presents three exhaustive cases—both cease, one ceases, both remain—showing that union does not occur in any. This passage establishes the foundational ruling on union and shows why the matter is self-evident.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a ruling followed by three cases.
The author uses a very concise style; each case is stated briefly.
2
Identify the three cases
Find the three conditional scenarios: both cease, one ceases, both remain. Understand why each leads to 'no union'.
The second case uses the term 'بديهة' (self-evident).
3
Reflect on the purpose
The author says the purpose is to point out the necessity by stripping both sides. Ask yourself why this is necessary for kalam reasoning.
This argument is not theoretical but foundational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثامن : الاثنان لا يتحدان وهذا حكم ضروري. فيقال : إن عدم الهويتان بعد الاتحاد وحدث أمر غيرهما ، فلا اتحاد بينهما، بل هما قد عدما ، وحدث هناك أمر ثالث غيرهما . وإن عدم أحدهما فقط فلا اتحاد أيضاً؛ إذ لا يتحد المعدوم بالموجود بديهةً، وإلا كان موجوداً و معدوماً معاً . وإن وجدا أي بقيا موجودين بعد الاتحاد، فهما بعده اثنان متغایران كما كانا كذلك قبله فلا اتحاد أيضاً. والغرض من هذا الكلام هو التنبيه على الضرورة بتجريد الطرفين وتصوير المراد على الوجه الذي هو الحكم. وظن بعض الناس أنهم حاولوا بهذا الكلام الاستدلال على مطلوب نظري فيمنع امتناع الاتحاد على تقدير بقائهما موجودين . وإنما يكونان اثنين لو لم يتحدا أي لا نسلم أنهما لو كانا بعد الاتحاد موجودين لكانا اثنين لا واحداً، وإنما يكونان كذلك لو لم يكن كل منهما موجوداً متحداً بالموجود الآخر، وهو ممنوع. مناط قوله : ( فيقال : إلخ) هذا التنبيه جارٍ في وجهي الاتحاد، كما يظهر في التدبر. ونص عليه الشارح قدس سره في حواشي شرح التجريد . قوله : ( أي بقيا موجودين إلخ) فسر به ليصح مقابلته بقوله : إن عدما بعد الاتحاد. قوله : ( فلا اتحاد أيضاً) إبقاء الاثنينية كما كانت قوله: (فيمنع) عطف على ظن والتعبير بصيغة المضارع لكونه مستقبلاً بالقياس إلى الظن، وإن كان الظاهر صيغة الماضي بالنظر إلى زمان التكلم . قوله : ( فيقال : إن عدم الهويتان إلخ ) الظاهر أن هذا التنبيه مخصوص بأول معنى الاتحاد الحقيقي والتنبيه على الباقي يعلم بالمقايسة. قوله : (أي بقيا موجودين وجه التفسير بهذا أنهما موجودان قبل الاتحاد . قوله : ( فيمنع امتناع الاتحاد فائدة الاختيار على الماضي الذي يستدعيه السوق استحضار للصورة الغريبة . قوله : (لو لم يكن كل منهما موجوداً متحداً بالموجود الآخر فإن قيل: هما إما موجودان بأحد الموجودين الأولين فقط، فيكون فناء لأحدهما وبقاء للآخر، أو بهما معاً فيكونان اثنين أو بغيرهما فيكون فناء لهما، وحدوث ثالث ويجاب بأنهما موجودان بوجود واحد هو نفس الوجودين الأولين صارا واحداً. لا يقال : يلزم أن يكون واحد بعينه حالاً في محلين؛ لأنه يقال : إنما يلزم ذلك لو لم يتحد ذاتاهما بأن كان هناك ذاتان وجدا بوجود واحد، وليس كذلك، بل المفروض أنهما قد اتحدا ذاتاً ووجوداً.
The Fourth Observatory - The Eighth Objective: Two things do not become one, and this is a necessary judgment. It is said: If the two essences cease to exist after union and something other than them occurs, then there is no union between them; rather they have both ceased, and a third thing other than them has occurred. And if only one of them ceases, then also no union, because a non-existent cannot unite with an existent self-evidently, otherwise it would be existent and non-existent simultaneously. And if they both exist, i.e., they remain existent after union, then they are after it two distinct things as they were before it, so also no union. And the purpose of this speech is to point out the necessity by stripping both sides and depicting the intended meaning in the manner that is the judgment. And some people thought that they attempted by this speech to prove a theoretical claim, so they deny the impossibility of union on the assumption that both remain existent. And they are two only if they did not unite, i.e., we do not accept that if they after union were existent they would be two not one; they would be so only if each of them were not existent having united with the other existent, and this is denied. The basis of his saying: ('It is said, etc.') This pointing is valid in both aspects of union, as appears upon reflection. And the commentator (may God sanctify his secret) stated it in his glosses on the commentary on al-Tajrid. His saying: ('i.e., they remain existent, etc.') He interpreted it thus to make correct its contrast with his saying: 'if they cease after union.' His saying: ('so also no union') preservation of duality as it was. His saying: ('they deny') is coordinate with 'they thought,' and the use of the present tense because it is future relative to the thought, although the apparent would be the past tense with respect to the time of speaking. His saying: ('It is said: if the two essences cease, etc.') The apparent is that this pointing is specific to the first meaning of real union, and pointing to the rest is known by analogy. His saying: ('i.e., they remain existent') The reason for this interpretation is that they are existent before union. His saying: ('they deny the impossibility of union') The benefit of choosing the present tense over the past tense which the context demands is to bring to mind the strange scenario. His saying: ('if each of them were not existent having united with the other existent') For if it is said: They are either existent by one of the two original existents only, so that would be the annihilation of one and the continuation of the other; or by both together, so they would be two; or by something else, so that would be the annihilation of both and the occurrence of a third. And the answer is that they are existent by one existence which is the very same as the two original existences having become one. It is not said: This entails that one and the same thing is present in two loci; because it is said: That would only be entailed if their essences did not unite, such that there were two essences existing by one existence, but it is not so; rather the supposition is that they have united in essence and existence.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a proof by exhaustion against the possibility of two things becoming one (union). The author first states the ruling as necessary. He then presents three exhaustive cases: (1) both essences cease after union and a third thing arises—then no union, (2) only one ceases—then no union because the non-existent cannot unite with the existent, (3) both remain—then they are still two, so no union. The purpose is to show the necessity by stripping the options. Some people mistakenly thought this was a theoretical argument and raised an objection: they deny the impossibility on the assumption both remain, claiming they could become one if each is united with the other. The author rejects this as denied. The commentary clarifies that this pointing applies to real union and that the rest is known by analogy. It also explains the grammar and the choice of tense. A further objection: if both remain, they could be existent by one existence that is the same as the two original existences having become one. The answer is that this would require one existence in two loci unless their essences also unite; but here the essences are supposed to have united as well, so no paradox.

Key Terms
اتحاد (union)
هويتان (two essences)
معدوم (non-existent)
موجود (existent)
بديهة (self-evident)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that union could occur if both things remain existent and become one.
Correction: The argument shows that if both remain, they are still two distinct things, so no union.

Checkpoint
According to the three cases, what happens if both things remain existent after union?
A) They become one
B) They are still two
C) They both cease to exist
D) A third thing arises

Key Takeaway
Two things cannot become one because any logical outcome leaves them as two, annihilates them, or produces a third thing—none of which is true union.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there two loyalties in your heart—one to Allah and one to this world—that you pretend are united? Which of the three cases does your actual experience match: both destroyed? one destroyed? both kept separate? What does this passage demand you do?
?
You might claim to love Allah and love sin at the same time. According to this passage, can those two loves truly become one? If not, then which one will you choose to cease—your love for Allah or your love for sin? Answer honestly before Allah.

Objection and Refinement: Union Under Both Remaining
Al-Iji refutes an objection to the impossibility of union and commentator provides refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the union argument and how al-Iji and the commentator refine the concept of genuine union.

Core Idea
The objection that union might occur if both remain is rejected; real union requires the essences to become one.

After presenting the three cases, al-Iji addresses some who think the argument is merely theoretical. He denies their claim that both could remain and still unite. The commentary clarifies the meaning of 'union' and addresses a potential paradox about one existence in two loci, resolving it by insisting on essence-union.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read from 'وظن بعض الناس' to the end. Notice the objection and the denial. Then read the commentator's notes.
The objection focuses on the third case (both remain).
2
Understand the denial
Al-Iji denies that two things can remain existent and yet be one. He says they would be two unless each is united with the other (which is denied).
This is a straightforward rejection of the objection.
3
Explore the commentator's clarifications
The commentator explains the grammar (tense choices) and notes that the pointing applies to real union. He also addresses a further paradox: if both remain, they could be existent by one existence, but that would require one thing in two loci unless essences unite.
The resolution is that the supposition includes union of essences, so no paradox.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to 'unite' two incompatible aspects of your life (e.g., worldly ambition and devotion) while hoping both could remain unchanged? What must you actually surrender for genuine unity of purpose?
?
When you affirm that God is one, do you truly reject any hint of composition in His essence? Examine your heart: is there any subtle belief that God's attributes are 'parts' that could somehow be separate?
?
If real union requires the essences themselves to become one, what does this tell you about the kind of transformation needed for you to be united with the truth? Are you holding onto your old self, expecting to remain unchanged?

Rational Judgments
intermediate
6 min
Defining Tamāthul and the Objection of Differing Causes
Defining tamāthul and presenting the initial objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of tamāthul as participation in the most specific attribute of the essence and why it raises the objection of multiple causes for a single judgment.

Core Idea
Tamāthul is defined by the most specific attribute, not any common attribute.

In this passage, the author defines tamāthul (similarity) as participation in the most specific attribute of the essence. He then notes that this definition leads to an objection: it would cause a single type of judgment to be explained by different causes, which the Mu'tazila reject. We'll explore the definition and the initial objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage that defines tamāthul. Note the phrase 'al-asharr wāṣf al-nafs' (the most specific attribute of the essence).
The definition is: 'al-istirāk fī al-asharr wāṣf al-nafs'.
2
Understand the non-exclusionary qualifier
The author clarifies that 'al-asharr' is not an exclusionary qualifier (iḥtirāzī) but rather a realization of the essence of tamāthul. This means it is not meant to exclude other attributes.
The commentary explains: 'لتحقيق ماهية التماثل'.
3
Grasp the objection
The objection is that this definition leads to causing tamāthul, which is one type of judgment (ḥukm wāḥid bi-l-nawʿ), by different causes (e.g., blackness for black things, whiteness for white things). The Mu'tazila reject this.
The passage states: 'يلزمهم تعليل التماثل وهو حكم واحد بعلل مختلفة'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد التاسع : الاثنان من المتكلمين ثلاثة أقسام
الأعم
( أصرح) فيما هو المراد من الاشتراك في الكل ولهم أن يقولوا : الاشتراك في وإن كان لازماً، لكنه خارج عن مفهوم التماثل، إذ مداره على الاشتراك في الأخص، ( مع أنه يلزمهم تعليل التماثل وهو حكم واحد بعلل مختلفة ، لأن التماثل يقع صفة للسوادين كما يقع صفة للبياضين، فإذا كان التماثل هو الاشتراك في أخص وصف النفس كان تماثل السوادين معللا بأخص ،وصفهما أعني السوادية، وتماثل البياضين معللاً بأخص وصفهما، أعني البياضية. ولا شك أن السوادية والبياضية مختلفان . وقد علل بهما التماثل الذي هو حكم ،واحد ، وهذا الاعتراض مشترك الإلزام
التماثل .
قوله : ( ولهم أن يقولوا إلخ) يعني أن قيد الأخص ليس احترازياً، بل لتحقيق ماهية
قوله : (مع أنه يلزمهم إلخ ) يعني أن المعتزلة لا يجوزون تعليل الحكم الواحد بالنوع، متمسكين بشبهة هي أنه لو جاز ذلك جاز تعلل العالمية بالعلم ،تارة وبالقدرة أخرى مع ظهور بطلانه . فيلزمهم على هذا التعريف تعليل القائل الذي هو حكم واحد بالنوع بعلل مختلفة، كما
بينه الشارح قدس سره .
قوله : ( وهذا الاعتراض مشترك (الإلزام أي بين المعتزلة وأصحابنا القائلين بالحال، وأما أصحابنا النافون لها فيجوزون التعليل المذكور فلا اعتراض عليهم .
قوله : (بعلل مختلفة) قيل لهم أن يقولوا بعد تسليم وحدة المتماثلين أن العلة أخصية الوصف، واختلاف الانواع لا يضر كالمشي تقتضيه الحيوانية إنساناً كان أو فرساً. ورد بأن علة التماثل هو الاشتراك فيما صدق عليه أنه أخص وصف النفس لا في مفهومه . ولا شك أن ما صدق عليه أخص وصف النفس في البياضين وفي السوادين هو السوادية، وأنهما متخالفان حقيقة فتأمل .
قول : ( مشترك الإلزام قيل : هذا نقض إجمالي، والتفصيل فيه أن يقال : إن أريد تعليل حكم واحد شخصي فلا نسلم ،الملازمة وإن أريد تعليل حكم واحد نوعي، فلا نسلم بطلان التالي. والحق أن هذا التفصيل لا يرد ؛ لأن الكلام إلزامي. وأكثر المعتزلة وإن جوزوا تعليل الواحد بالنوع بعلل متحدة به، لكنهم لا يجوزون تعليله بعلل مختلفة بالنوع، مستدلين عليه بأنه لو جاز ذلك لجاز أن يكون حكم العالمية معللة بالعلم ،تارة وبالقدرة أخرى مع ظهور بطلانه فيرد الإلزام عليهم وكذا على القائلين بالحال من الأصحاب فإنهم كالمعتزلة في التجويز والإحالة على الأصحاب مطلقاً. وقيل: بل الكلام برهاني لأن الواحد بالذات لا يعلل بعلتين، سواء كان شخصياً أو لا، فإن مطلق التماثل طبيعة جنسية مخصوصة، فلايجوز أن يعلل تحصلها بعلل كثيرة، كما ذكره الشارح في تعريفات علية الفصل. وفيه أن المعلل بالمختلفات هاهنا هو أفراد التماثل لا طبيعته ولا نزاع عندنا في جواز مثله .
The Fourth Observatory - The Ninth Objective: The Two from the Mutakallimūn are Three Sections. The more general: (I express more clearly) what is meant by participation in the whole. And they may say: Participation in [the general] is necessary, but it is outside the concept of tamāthul (similarity), for its basis is participation in the more specific (al-akhass). (And also it requires them to cause tamāthul, which is one judgment, by different causes, because tamāthul occurs as an attribute of two black things as it occurs as an attribute of two white things. So if tamāthul is participation in the most specific attribute of the essence, then the tamāthul of two black things would be caused by their most specific attribute, namely blackness, and the tamāthul of two white things would be caused by their most specific attribute, namely whiteness. There is no doubt that blackness and whiteness are different. And tamāthul, which is one judgment, has been caused by them, and this objection is commonly binding. Tamāthul. His statement: 'And they may say etc.' means that the qualification 'the most specific' is not exclusionary, but rather for realizing the essence of tamāthul. His statement: 'And also it requires them etc.' means that the Mu'tazila do not allow causing one judgment of a type, holding onto a specious argument that if that were allowed, then it would be allowed for the attribute of being knowing (al-ʿālamiyya) to be caused sometimes by knowledge and other times by power, despite its falsehood being apparent. So according to this definition, they are required to cause the speaker, which is one judgment of a type, by different causes, as the commentator (may God sanctify his secret) explained. His statement: 'And this objection is commonly binding' means between the Mu'tazila and our companions who affirm the state (al-ḥāl). As for our companions who deny the state, they allow the mentioned causing, so no objection applies to them. His statement: 'By different causes' – it was said to them: After conceding the unity of the two similars, they should say that the cause is the specificness of the attribute, and difference of types does not harm, like walking, which animality necessitates whether it is a human or a horse. And it was refuted by saying that the cause of tamāthul is participation in that which is actually the most specific attribute of the essence, not in its concept. There is no doubt that that which is actually the most specific attribute of the essence in two white things and in two black things is blackness, and they are really different in kind, so reflect. His statement: 'Commonly binding' – it was said: This is a general refutation, and the detail regarding it is that one should say: If what is meant is causing one individual judgment, then we do not concede the implication; and if what is meant is causing one generic judgment, then we do not concede the falsehood of the consequent. The truth is that this detail does not apply, because the speech is binding. Most Mu'tazila, although they allow causing one thing of a type by causes united with it, do not allow causing it by causes different in type, arguing that if that were allowed, then it would be allowed for the judgment of being knowing to be caused sometimes by knowledge and other times by power, despite the obvious falsehood. So the binding argument applies to them, and likewise to the proponents of the state among our companions, for they are like the Mu'tazila in permissibility and impossibility regarding our companions absolutely. And it was said: Rather, the speech is demonstrative (burhānī), because one thing in essence cannot be caused by two causes, whether it is individual or not, for absolute tamāthul is a specific generic nature, so it is not permissible for its realization to be caused by many causes, as the commentator mentioned in the definitions of the causality of the differentia. And in it [there is the response] that what is caused by different things here is the individuals of tamāthul, not its nature, and there is no dispute among us regarding the permissibility of such.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a definition and objection. It begins by defining tamāthul (similarity) as participation in the most specific attribute of the essence (aṣarr wāṣf al-nafs). The author clarifies that 'the most specific' is not an exclusionary qualifier but rather serves to realize the essence of tamāthul. The Mu'tazila are then attributed with an objection: this definition entails that tamāthul, which is a single type of judgment (ḥukm wāḥid bi-l-nawʿ), is caused by different causes because the most specific attribute differs between kinds (e.g., blackness vs. whiteness). The commentator explains that the Mu'tazila reject causing one type of judgment by multiple causes of different types, using a specious argument: if it were allowed, then the attribute of being knowing (al-ʿālamiyya) could be caused by knowledge or power, which is absurd. Thus, the definition forces them to accept this. The objection is said to be 'commonly binding' (ishtirāk al-ilzām) on both the Mu'tazila and the Ashʿarī proponents of the state (aṣḥāb al-ḥāl), but not on those Ashʿarīs who deny the state, as they allow the same. A rejoinder is then considered: the cause is the specificness of the attribute, and difference in type does not harm (e.g., animality causes walking in both humans and horses). The reply is that the cause is participation in the actual instance (mā ṣadaqa ʿalayhi) of the most specific attribute, not its concept, and the actual instances are different in kind. Finally, a detailed counter is given: if the judgment is individual, the implication is not conceded; if generic, the falsehood is not conceded. But the commentator argues this detail fails because the speech is binding (ilzāmī), and most Mu'tazila still disallow causing one type by different types due to the specious argument. Some say the speech is demonstrative (burhānī) because one essence cannot have two causes, but the response is that what is caused by different things here is the individuals of tamāthul, not its nature, and that is permissible.

Key Terms
تماثل (tamāthul)
أخص صفات النفس (aṣarr wāṣf al-nafs)
علة (ʿilla)
حكم واحد بالنوع (ḥukm wāḥid bi-l-nawʿ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the phrase 'most specific attribute' is meant to exclude other attributes, i.e., an exclusionary qualifier.
Correction: The author clarifies that it is not exclusionary but rather serves to realize the true essence of tamāthul.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of tamāthul according to the passage?
A) Participation in any common attribute
B) Participation in the most specific attribute of the essence
C) Participation in a positive attribute
D) Being a single judgment

Key Takeaway
Tamāthul is defined as sharing the most specific attribute, not any common attribute, and this definition triggers an objection about multiple causes for a single judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own understanding of Allah's attributes, do you settle for vague similarities between His attributes and created ones, or do you strive to know His most specific attributes as taught by this lesson? Will you commit today to studying the divine names and attributes with greater precision?
?
The objection in this passage exposes an inconsistency in the Mu'tazila position. Is there an inconsistency in your own beliefs about Allah—something you accept in one area but reject in another—that you have been avoiding? Are you willing to bring it before Him in repentance and seek clarity?
?
The definition of tamāthul forces us to recognize that similarity requires the most specific common attribute. In your relationship with Allah, do you seek a shallow similarity based on generalities (e.g., 'He is merciful, so I should be merciful') without delving into the specific ways He has revealed His attributes? How will you deepen your understanding of His most specific attributes starting today?

Rational Judgments

Responding to the Objection: The Cause and Its Nature
Responding to the objection with rejoinders and a detailed analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rejoinder to the objection and the refined analysis of the cause, including the distinction between individual and generic judgments.

Core Idea
The cause of tamāthul is the actual instance of the most specific attribute, not its concept.

Having raised the objection that tamāthul as defined would require different causes for one judgment, the author now presents a rejoinder and a response. He also discusses whether the argument is binding or demonstrative, and distinguishes between individual and generic judgments. Let's follow the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the rejoinder
The rejoinder suggests that the cause is the specificness of the attribute, and difference of types does not harm, using the example of walking being caused by animality in both humans and horses.
The passage says: 'قيل لهم أن يقولوا بعد تسليم وحدة المتماثلين أن العلة أخصية الوصف، واختلاف الانواع لا يضر كالمشي تقتضيه الحيوانية'.
2
Understand the response
The response distinguishes between the concept of the most specific attribute and the actual instance (mā ṣadaqa ʿalayhi). The cause is participation in the actual instance, not the concept, and actual instances differ in kind.
The passage states: 'العلة التماثل هو الاشتراك فيما صدق عليه أنه أخص وصف النفس لا في مفهومه'.
3
Analyze the detailed counter
A detailed objection proposes distinguishing between a personal (individual) judgment and a generic (type) judgment. If the former, the implication is not conceded; if the latter, the falsehood is not conceded. The author argues this detail fails because the argument is binding, not demonstrative.
The passage discusses: 'إن أريد تعليل حكم واحد شخصي فلا نسلم الملازمة وإن أريد تعليل حكم واحد نوعي فلا نسلم بطلان التالي'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Some say the argument is demonstrative because one essence cannot have two causes, but the response is that what is caused by different things here are the individuals of tamāthul, not its nature, which is permissible.
The passage ends with the idea that individuals can have different causes.

Key Terms
أخصية الوصف (aṣariyyat al-waṣf)

حكم نوعي (ḥukm nawʿī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the cause of tamāthul is the concept of the most specific attribute (e.g., 'the most specific attribute' as a universal).
Correction: The passage clarifies that the cause is the actual instance (mā ṣadaqa ʿalayhi) of the most specific attribute, not the concept.

Checkpoint
What is the cause of tamāthul according to the response in the passage?
A) The most specific attribute as a concept
B) The specificness of the attribute itself
C) Participation in the actual instance of the most specific attribute
D) The generic nature of similarity

Key Takeaway
The objection is resolved by recognizing that the cause of tamāthul is the actual most specific attribute, not its concept, and that individuals of tamāthul can have different causes without affecting the nature.

?

Second Objection: Tamāthul as Necessary or Contingent
Objection that tamāthul is either necessary or contingent, each leading to a problem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if tamāthul is either necessary or contingent, both alternatives lead to problems in the Ashʿarī framework.

Core Idea
Tamāthul cannot be necessary or contingent without causing logical contradictions.

This passage presents a powerful objection against the definition of tamāthul. The objector argues that tamāthul, if necessary, cannot have a cause; if contingent, it would be unstable. Both outcomes undermine the proposed definition. Let's examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall flow of the objection.
Pay attention to the two prongs: necessary and contingent.
2
Analyze the necessary case
Understand why necessary tamāthul cannot be caused according to Ashʿarī principles. The commentator gives the example of God's knowledge.
Note the distinction between causing by essence (allowed) and by accidental attribute (disallowed).
3
Analyze the contingent case
If tamāthul is contingent, it would allow similarity to appear and disappear, which is absurd for the two similars.
Contingency implies variability, which is unacceptable.
4
Study the commentator's clarifications
The commentator addresses potential contradictions, explaining that tamāthul is not additional to the essence, and that necessary attributes can be caused by the essence itself.
This refines the understanding of what counts as a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن الأخص إذا كان مختلفاً كان مجموع صفات النفس بين السوادين مخالفاً لمجموعها في البياض، فيكون التماثل المعلل بالمجموع معللاً بعلل مختلفة. والقائلون بالحال من الأشاعرة لا يجوزونه أيضاً . ) وأيضاً فالتماثل للمثلين، إما واجب فلا يعلل) التماثل حينئذ ( على رأيهم)، إذ من قواعدهم أن الصفة الواجبة يمتنع تعليلها، ومن ثمة قالوا : لما كان عالمية الله تعالى واجبة لذاته امتنع أن تكون معللة بالعلم، فلا يجوز تعريفه بالاشتراك في أخص صفات النفس، لاقتضائه أن يكون التماثل معللاً بالأخص كما مر (أولاً) يكون واجباً للمثلين (فيجوز) حينئذ (كون السوادين مختلفين تارة وغير مختلفين أخرى) بأن يثبت لهما التماثل فيكونان متماثلين ويزول عنهما فيكونان ،مختلفين وبطلانه ظاهر.
قوله : ( إما (واجب) أي واجب الحصول لموصوفه عند حصول الموصوف .
قوله : ( فيكون التماثل المعلل بالمجموع إلخ) لا يخفى أن من جملة صفة النفس التماثل، فلا بد أن يراد مجموع ما عداه فإن قلت : تعليل التماثل بمجموع صفات النفس يناقض ظاهر ما سبق، من أنه لا نفس الذاوت . قلت : مراده من كونه لا نفس الذوات أنه ليس معللاً بأمر زائد عليها، كما صرح به هناك. والصفات النفسية ليست زائدة عليها فلا تناقض .
قوله : (إما واجب فلا يعلل) قيل : تعليل الواجب بذات الموصوف جائز عندهم كالجوهرية بذات الجوهر والمحال تعليله بصفة عارضة . فهذا الاعتراض إنما يرد عليهم إذا قالوا بزيادة ذلك الأخص، وجوابه المنع ؛ فإن الظاهر أن الجواب عندهم لا يعقل أصلا يدل على ذلك كلامهم في المقصد العاشر من مرصد العلة والمعلول.
For if the most specific is different, then the total of the attributes of the essence between the two black things is different from the total in the two white things, so tamāthul caused by the total is caused by different causes. And the proponents of the state among the Ashʿarīs also do not allow it. (And also tamāthul for the two similars: either it is necessary, so it cannot be caused) tamāthul then (according to their view), for among their principles is that a necessary attribute cannot be caused. Hence they said: Since the attribute of being knowing (al-ʿālamiyya) of God Most High is necessary for His essence, it cannot be caused by knowledge. So it is not permissible to define it (tamāthul) by participation in the most specific attributes of the essence, because it would require that tamāthul be caused by the most specific as previously. (First) it is necessary for the two similars, (then it is permissible) (for the two black things to be different sometimes and not different other times), such that tamāthul is established for them so they are similar, and then it ceases so they become different, and its falsehood is apparent. His statement: '(Either necessary)' i.e., necessarily obtained for its subject upon obtaining the subject. His statement: '(So tamāthul caused by the total etc.)' It is not hidden that among the attributes of the essence is tamāthul itself, so what is meant must be the total excluding it. For if you say: Causing tamāthul by the total of the attributes of the essence contradicts the apparent meaning of the earlier statement that it is not the essences themselves. I say: What is meant by its not being the essences themselves is that it is not caused by something additional to them, as he explicitly stated there. And the essential attributes are not additional to them, so there is no contradiction. His statement: '(Either necessary so it cannot be caused)' It was said: Causing the necessary by the essence of the subject is permissible according to them, like substantiality (jawhariyya) by the essence of the substance, but it is impossible to cause it by an accidental attribute. So this objection only applies to them if they affirm the additionalness of that most specific attribute, and its answer is denial; for it is apparent that the answer according to them is in principle not intelligible, as indicated by their discourse in the Tenth Objective from the Observatory of Cause and Effect.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection to the definition of tamāthul. It argues that if the most specific attribute differs, then the total set of essential attributes differs between black and white things, so tamāthul caused by that total is caused by different causes. The Ashʿarī proponents of the state also disallow this. Then a further argument is given: tamāthul for two similars is either necessary (wājib) or contingent (jāʾiz). If necessary, it cannot be caused, because according to their principles a necessary attribute cannot have a cause (e.g., God's knowing cannot be caused by knowledge). If contingent, then the definition would allow the two similars to sometimes be similar and sometimes not, which is false. The commentator clarifies that 'necessary' means necessarily obtained for its subject upon its existence. He then addresses a potential contradiction: causing tamāthul by the total of essential attributes seems to contradict an earlier statement that tamāthul is not the essences themselves. The resolution is that the earlier statement meant tamāthul is not caused by something external, but essential attributes are not external, so no contradiction. Finally, regarding the objection that necessary attributes cannot be caused, it is noted that causing by the essence itself is allowed (like substantiality), but causing by an accidental attribute is impossible. Therefore, the objection applies only if the most specific attribute is considered additional, but the correct answer is to deny that it is additional, as per their discussions on causality.

المثلان (al-mithlān)
واجب (wājib)
جائز (jāʾiz)
صفة واجبة (ṣifa wājiba)
علة (ʿilla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that necessary attributes cannot have any cause at all.
Correction: Causing by the essence itself is allowed (e.g., substantiality by the substance's essence), but causing by an accidental attribute is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is one consequence if tamāthul is contingent?
A) It cannot be caused.
B) It would be variable, allowing similars to become dissimilar.
C) It would be caused by the essence.
D) It would be impossible to define.

Key Takeaway
The objection shows that defining tamāthul leads to a dilemma: it cannot be necessary (uncaused) nor contingent (unstable), so the definition fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to find a reason for why God possesses an attribute, forgetting that His attributes are necessary and uncaused? How does this lesson warn you against such a mindset?
?
The objection shows that if tamāthul were contingent, similarity could disappear. Consider your own spiritual identity: Do you treat your relationship with Allah as contingent on circumstances, or as something necessary that cannot be shaken? What would change if you believed it were uncaused and permanent?
?
The Ash'arīs defend their position by denying that the most specific attribute is additional. Think of a belief you hold dear. Are you able to articulate why it is essential to your faith, not an add-on? If not, how can you gain that clarity before facing objections?
