Definition of Tadhaad: First Two Conditions
Defines tadhaad with two conditions and gives counterexamples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of tadhaad (contradiction) and why the condition 'in their essences' is necessary to distinguish true contradiction from mere incompatibility due to external factors.

Core Idea
Tadhaad means two things cannot coexist due to their own essences.

In kalam, precise definitions are essential. The term 'tadhaad' (contradiction) is often misunderstood. This passage clarifies that for two things to be truly contradictory, not only must they be unable to exist together, but that impossibility must stem from their very natures, not from external requirements like the objects they relate to.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author first makes a general statement, then unpacks the definition with conditions and examples.
Pay attention to the examples: blackness/sweetness, knowledge of motion/rest, voluntary motion/incapacity.
2
Identify the two conditions
The definition has two parts: 'their coexistence is impossible' and 'in their essences'. Understand what each condition adds.
The first condition excludes things that can coexist. The second excludes things that cannot coexist due to external factors.
3
Test your understanding with examples
For each example, determine why it is or is not tadhaad. Explain using the conditions.
Blackness and sweetness can coexist → not tadhaad. Knowledge of motion and rest cannot coexist, but not due to their essences → not tadhaad. Voluntary motion and incapacity cannot coexist, but due to necessity of power → not tadhaad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأمور لا تضاد في شيء منها . ( و ) قولنا : ( يمتنع اجتماعهما ) يخرج (نحو السواد والحلاوة)، فإنهما يجتمعان فلا تضاد بينهما، (و) قولنا : (لذاتيهما ) يخرج (العلم بالحركة والسكون معاً فإن هذين العلمين وإن امتنع اجتماعهما، لكن ليس ذلك لذاتيهما، بل لاستلزامهما المعلومين اللذين يمتنع اجتماعهما لذاتيهما، فلا تضاد بين العلمين بل بين معلوميهما، (و) كذا يخرج ) الحركة الاختيارية مع العجز) فإن امتناع الاجتماع بينهما ليس لذاتيهما، بل لأن الحركة الاختيارية تستلزم القدرة المضادة للعجز لكونهما متنافيين بالذات.
Things have no contradiction in any of them. And our saying: (their coexistence is impossible) excludes (such as blackness and sweetness), for they coexist so there is no contradiction between them. And our saying: (in their essences) excludes (knowledge of motion and rest together, for these two knowledges, even if their coexistence is impossible, that is not due to their essences, but due to their necessitating the two known objects whose coexistence is impossible in their essences, so there is no contradiction between the two knowledges but between their two known objects. And similarly, (voluntary motion with incapacity) is excluded, for the impossibility of coexistence between them is not due to their essences, but because voluntary motion necessitates the power opposite to incapacity, as they are mutually exclusive by essence.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage defines tadhaad (contradiction) by explaining the conditions in the definition. It begins by stating that things have no contradiction in any of them, i.e., contradiction is not inherent in all things. Then it examines the phrase 'their coexistence is impossible', which excludes cases like blackness and sweetness that can coexist together, so they are not contradictory. Next, it examines the phrase 'in their essences', which excludes cases like knowledge of motion and knowledge of rest of the same object at the same time; these two knowledges cannot coexist, but the impossibility is not due to the essences of the knowledges but due to the natures of the known objects, which are contradictory in essence. Thus, the contradiction is between the objects, not the knowledges. Similarly, voluntary motion and incapacity: they cannot coexist, but not because they are inherently contradictory; rather, voluntary motion necessitates power, which is opposite to incapacity. Therefore, the condition 'in their essences' ensures that the impossibility of coexistence arises from the essences themselves, not from external factors.

Key Terms
تضاد
يمتنع اجتماعهما
لذاتهما
المعلومين
العلم بالحركة والسكون
الحركة الاختيارية مع العجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any two things that cannot coexist are contradictory.
Correction: True tadhaad requires the impossibility of coexistence to be due to the essences themselves, not due to external factors. For example, knowledge of motion and rest cannot coexist, but the impossibility comes from the objects, so the knowledges are not contradictory.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are knowledge of motion and knowledge of rest of the same object not considered contradictory?
A) Because they can coexist in the mind
B) Because the impossibility of their coexistence is not due to their own essences but due to their objects
C) Because they are both attributes of God
D) Because they are not real knowledges

Key Takeaway
Tadhaad is defined as the impossibility of coexistence of two things due to their own essences, excluding cases where the impossibility arises from external factors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you avoid a conflict because you assume two people or beliefs cannot coexist, ask yourself: Is that impossibility rooted in their true natures, or in your own assumptions and fears? How can you test the essential compatibility before concluding contradiction?
?
Think of a personal habit you consider incompatible with your faith—like worldly ambition and spiritual devotion. Are they truly contradictory in their essences, or do you simply perceive them as such due to your limitations? What would it take to examine their essential natures honestly?
?
Consider a time you judged someone else's actions as contradictory to their stated beliefs. Did you examine whether the contradiction is in the essences of the actions themselves, or merely in your understanding? How can you apply the lesson of tadhaad to be more precise in your judgments?

The Third Condition of Tadhaad: 'From One Aspect'
Discusses the third condition 'from one aspect' in the definition of tadhaad and the debate about its role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of the condition 'from one aspect' in the definition of tadhaad (contradiction), and the debate about whether it generalizes or specifies the definition.

Core Idea
The condition 'from one aspect' either generalizes the definition or specifies it — and that changes everything.

The third condition of tadhaad, 'from one aspect', seems like a simple addition, but it triggers a deep debate. Does it exclude relational attributes or include them? Does it generalize the definition or specify it? In this passage, we explore the different interpretations and their implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense interlinear comments. Focus on the main argument.
2
Identify the basic claim
The passage begins by stating that the condition 'from one aspect' excludes relational attributes like smallness/bigness and nearness/farness. Understand what these relational attributes are.
3
Spot the problem
The author notes that these relational attributes are already excluded by the earlier phrase 'two meanings' because they are not considered existent by theologians. So why add this condition? This leads to a debate.
4
Analyse the two interpretations
One interpretation: this condition is a 'safeguard' to prevent the exclusion of these attributes. Another interpretation: it generalizes the definition because it qualifies the negated. Follow the argument about negation and qualification.
The key is whether the condition qualifies the negated (coexistence) or the negation (impossibility).
5
Reflect on the nature of relational attributes
The passage argues that relational attributes are mental considerations, not real accidents. This affects whether they can be considered contradictory. Consider why this matters for the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) قولنا: (من جهة) يخرج (نحو الصغر والكبر، والقرب والبعد ) من الأمور الإضافية، هذا هو الظاهر من عبارة الكتاب بناء على أن قوله : ومن جهة نحو الصغر عطف على قوله : فمعنيان يخرج العدم والوجود وفيه ،بحث فيقدر هاهنا يدخل بمعونة القرينة العقلية، وإن كان السياق تقتضي تقدير يخرج . قوله : (أنها أمور) يعني أنها ليست من أفراد المحذور، وكيف يمكن إدخالها في الحد، والقول بأن دخولها على تقدير وجودها تكلف . قوله لذاتيهما ، فليس قيداً للمنفي أعني الاجتماع، بل قيداً للنفي أعني الاستحالة، فلذا يفيد تخصيص الحد، وإخراج شيء عنه. وإن شئت فقل : الاجتماع في محل أعم من الاجتماع فيه من جهة واحدة فاستحالة الاجتماع في محل احتراز عن السطح
And our saying: (from one aspect) excludes (such as smallness and bigness, and nearness and farness) from relational things. This is what appears from the wording of the book, based on the fact that his saying: 'and from one aspect' like smallness is conjoined to his saying: 'so two meanings excludes non-existence and existence.' In it is discussion, because smallness and its kind are relational things, and relation is not existent according to the theologians, so they would be excluded from the definition by his saying 'two meanings'. Also, this condition, namely 'from one aspect', falls within the scope of the meaning of negation, and it is a condition for the negated, so its right is to effect generalization of the definition, inclusion of something in it, not its specification, and exclusion of something from it. Therefore, some said: this is a safeguard against the exclusion of these things, and it is objected that they are mental considerations, so how can they be made contradictory? Also, this condition only enters into the definition what is excluded by his saying 'their coexistence is impossible', not what is excluded by his saying 'two meanings', as is not hidden from one with understanding. Also, the 'fa' in his saying: (so the intellect does not necessitate) indicates that it is an explanation of the reason for excluding these things from the definition, i.e., we only excluded them because the intellect does not necessitate contradiction in mental considerations, like these things, and like good and bad, and permissible and forbidden in actions, for they are mental considerations that return according to us to... His saying: (so its right is to effect, etc.) because the negation is directed to the qualified, so it is possible that its negation is by the negation of the origin, or by the negation of the condition. And if it is said: the contradictory of the more specific is more general than the contradictory of the more general. He only said 'its right' because it may be for negation of the condition only, and therefore the rhetoricians said: every speech in which there is a condition, the intended in negation and affirmation is that condition. Perhaps the first is in the demonstrative context, and the second in the oratorical context. His saying: (and exclusion of something from it) and how can exclusion be achieved when smallness and bigness, nearness and farness are impossible to coexist from one aspect. His saying: (safeguard against exclusion, etc.) so here 'enters' is estimated with the aid of intellectual context, even if the context requires estimating 'excludes'. His saying: (that they are things) means they are not individuals of the thing feared, and how can they be entered into the definition? The claim that their entry upon the assumption of their existence is excessive. His saying: (and also) means the exclusion of the excluder is necessitated. His saying: (it only enters, etc.) because the generalization only occurred in it. His saying: (like these things and like good and bad, etc.) means that his saying 'like good and bad' is an example of mental considerations, not that the conjoined and the conjunction are implied in the speech, for there is no aspect to it. In it is a notification. His saying: (so its right is to effect generalization of the definition) because if it is a condition for the negated, then the negation reverts to it thereby negating it, and the negation of the condition necessitates absoluteness and generalization. As for his saying: 'in their essences', it is not a condition for the negated, i.e., the coexistence, but a condition for the negation, i.e., the impossibility, so it effects specification of the definition and exclusion of something from it. And if you wish, say: coexistence in a locus is more general than coexistence in it from one aspect, so the impossibility of coexistence in a locus from one aspect is more general than the impossibility of coexistence in it, necessarily because the contradictory of the more specific is more general. His saying: (and it is objected that they are mental considerations, etc.) and one might strain and say: it is possible that the conditioning is a conditioning upon concession, and the assumption that relations are accidents as the philosophers held, and the safeguard upon concession occurs in the definitions of the people, as the commentator will transmit in the definition of the philosophers for the natural body as 'a substance receptive to dimensions intersecting at right angles', that the condition 'intersecting at right angles' is a safeguard against the surface.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage discusses the third condition 'from one aspect' in the definition of tadhaad and the surrounding debate. It begins by stating that this condition excludes relational attributes like smallness/bigness and nearness/farness. The author notes that this is the apparent meaning of the book, based on the conjunction of 'from one aspect' with 'two meanings' that exclude non-existence and existence. However, a problem arises: since relational attributes are not considered existent in the theologians' view, they are already excluded by 'two meanings', so the condition seems redundant. Additionally, the condition 'from one aspect' falls within the scope of negation (as a qualifier of the negated), so it should generalize the definition (i.e., include more things) rather than specify it. This leads some to claim it is a safeguard to prevent the exclusion of these relational attributes from the definition. But the objection is that these are mental considerations, not real things, so they cannot be contradictory. The passage then examines the phrase 'so the intellect does not necessitate contradiction in mental considerations' and explains that the condition only applies to what is excluded by 'coexistence is impossible', not by 'two meanings'. The discussion continues with various analyses: that the negation directed to the qualified may negate the origin or the condition; that the contradictory of the more specific is more general; and that in the definition, 'from one aspect' as a condition for the negated leads to generalization, while 'in their essences' conditions the negation itself, leading to specification. The passage ends with a reference to a possible concession that relations might be considered accidents as philosophers hold, and an example of the safeguard in the definition of natural body.

تعميم الحد
احتراز
أمور اعتبارية
نقيض الأخص أعم من نقيض الأعم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the third condition 'from one aspect' is simply an additional specification to exclude relational attributes, without realizing the deep debate about its logical role and the nature of those attributes.
Correction: The condition actually triggers a debate: it appears to specify but logically should generalize if it qualifies the negated. The relational attributes are considered mental, not real, affecting their inclusion.

Key Takeaway
The condition 'from one aspect' in the definition of tadhaad leads to a debate about whether it generalizes or specifies the definition, reflecting deeper issues about the nature of relational attributes.

Resolving the Circularity Objection in Defining Opposites
Objection and answer on the definition of opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection of circularity against the definition of opposites and how the commentator resolves it by distinguishing between a justification and an independent explanatory statement.

Core Idea
Circularity objections often mistake definitions for proofs.

In studying definitions, one must be careful not to confuse a definition with a proof. When the commentator says that opposites must be two meanings, it might seem like an assumption. This lesson addresses that objection and clarifies the role of reason in i'tibari attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full translation to get the gist of the objection and answer.
Notice the two objections mentioned: circularity and the role of reason.
2
Identify the two objections
The passage mentions two things refuted. Identify each: (1) the definition is circular, (2) reason's non-necessitation of opposition is unclear.
These are marked by 'أحدهما' and 'والثاني'.
3
Understand the distinction
The commentator clarifies that the statement 'because the two opposites must be two meanings' is not a justification but an independent explanatory statement. Grasp that this removes the circularity.
4
Consider the role of reason
The second objection concerns reason's role. The commentator explains that reason judges after considering existence, not before. Reflect on why this matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( راجعة) عندنا إلى موافقة الشرع ومخالفته وليس الموافقة والمخالفة إلا أمرين يعتبرهما العقل بعد ملاحظة الشرع أو العقل، والاتصاف بها في الخارج، بل في الضمير فقط . قوله : ( فلا تضاد بينها ) أي بين هذه الصفات الاعتبارية . قوله : ( لأن المتضادين لا بد أن يكونا معنيين) أي أمرين قائمين بالغير في الخارج، فيصح القول باجتماعهما فيه بخلاف ما إذا كانا أمرين يكون الاتصاف بهما باعتبار العقل، فإنه يكون استحالة الاجتماع بينهما في الاعتقاد وحكم العقل، وبما حررنا لك ظهر اندفاع أمرين: أحدهما أن قوله : لأن المتضادين إلخ في قوة قولنا المتضادين لا يكونان اعتباريين، ففيه مصادرة. والثاني : أن عدم الإيجاب العقل للتضاد بين الأمور الاعتبارية مع قطع النظر عن اعتبار الوجود في المتضادين غير ظاهر وبعد اعتبار الوجود لا دخل للعقل في عدم الإيجاب قوله : ( كلاماً مستأنفاً) أي ليس تعليلاً للإخراج المذكور، بل كلام مستقل متفرع على تعريف المتضادين، فتقدم الشرط والجزاء لبيان المعنى لا لصحة الكلام.
His statement: ('reverting' according to us to compliance with the law and opposition to it) and the compliance and opposition are nothing but two things that reason considers after observing the law or reason, and being described by them is not externally, but only in the conscience. His statement: ('so there is no opposition between them') i.e., between these i'tibari attributes. His statement: ('because the two opposites must be two meanings') i.e., two things subsisting in another externally, so it is possible to say that they coexist in it, unlike if they were two things the description of which is by consideration of reason, for then there is impossibility of their coexistence in belief and the judgment of reason. And by what we have clarified for you, two things are refuted: first, that his statement: 'because the two opposites' etc. is equivalent to our statement: 'the two opposites are not i'tibari', so there is circularity. Second, that reason's non-necessitation of opposition between i'tibari things without considering existence in the two opposites is not apparent, and after considering existence, reason has no role in non-necessitation. His statement: ('independent speech') i.e., it is not a justification for the mentioned exclusion, but an independent speech branching from the definition of opposites, so the condition and result are presented to explain the meaning, not for the correctness of the speech.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The commentator first explains that compliance and opposition are i'tibari (mental considerations), so they are not opposites. The objection raised is that the definition of opposites as 'two existing meanings' is circular (musadara) because it assumes that opposites cannot be i'tibari. The answer given is that the statement 'because the two opposites must be two meanings' is not a justification for an exclusion but an independent statement clarifying the meaning. The second objection is that reason's non-necessitation of opposition for i'tibari things is unclear without considering existence; the reply is that when existence is already assumed, reason's role is to judge, not to create the non-necessitation. The passage then distinguishes between a justification and an independent explanatory statement.

اعتبارات
مصادرة
كلام مستأنف
تعليل
شرط وجزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The definition of opposites is circular because it assumes what it is trying to prove.
Correction: The definition is not a proof; it is an independent explanation of what 'opposite' means. The statement 'because the two opposites must be two meanings' clarifies the definition, not justifies an exclusion.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The definition of opposites is not circular; it is an independent explanation, not a proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The commentator says 'by what we have clarified for you, two things are refuted.' What objections do you hold in your heart against Allah's commands? Could it be that you are confusing a definition (e.g., 'Allah is just') with a proof that must satisfy your reason?

Rulings and Actions Are Not Truly Contradictory
Commentator explains why rulings and actions are not truly contradictory.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rulings (al-ahkam) and actions (al-af'al) in the sense of effects are not truly contradictory but rather considerational (i'tibari) and abstractive (intiza'i).

Core Idea
Rulings and actions are not real opposites; they exist only in the intellect's consideration.

In this passage, the commentator explains that the five legal rulings and actions (as effects) are not truly contradictory because they are not real entities in the external world. Instead, they are conceptual relations that the intellect considers when connecting divine addresses to human actions. This clarifies a subtle point in kalam: not every pair that cannot coexist is a genuine contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the commentator's distinction between rulings and actions.
The passage is a commentary on a base text, so expect glosses and elaborations.
2
Identify the two types: rulings and actions
The commentator discusses two things that are not truly contradictory: rulings (the five legal categories) and actions (in the sense of effects, not traces). Note how each is described as i'tibari or intiza'i.
Rulings are considerational (i'tibari); actions as effects are abstractive (intiza'i).
3
Understand why each is not a real opposite
For rulings: they are not called rulings except in terms of relation to the action, and relation is a mental construct. For actions: the effect is an abstract concept ascribed to the agent; it has no external existence. Therefore, no real opposition exists.
The condition 'from one aspect' is mentioned but not elaborated here.
4
Reflect on the takeaway
Ask yourself: if rulings and actions are not truly contradictory, what does that imply about the nature of theological opposites? This passage shows that kalam distinguishes between real and considerational opposition.

The obligated persons are taken in their reality, so they are considerational, and likewise actions in the sense of effects, for the category of action has no existence. And you will know that the condition 'from one aspect' is mentioned in the definition of correlatives to guard against the exclusion of the reciprocal ones, so there it has an apparent benefit, unlike here, so it is better to delete it here. (And as for the unity of subject which must be stipulated in contradictories...
His saying: 'the rulings' — i.e., the five legal rulings.
His saying: 'because the relation etc.' — means that the address related to the action of the obligated person, although it is eternal, is not called a ruling except in terms of its relation to the action, and relation is a thing that the intellect considers after observing the address and the action, and it is not subsisting in the action because it occurs before its existence, so the rulings are not described as being contradictory, even though they are described as impossible to coexist in the intellect's consideration. His saying: 'and likewise actions in the sense of effects, not in the sense of traces, for there is nothing in the external world except the agent and the trace, and effect is an abstractive thing with which the agent is described in the intellect, and there is no contradiction between actions either. This is what I have in solving this speech, and God knows best the intent.
His saying: 'and you will know etc.' is conjoined to his saying: 'then that part', so it is from the commentator's speech.
His saying: 'an apparent benefit, which is the inclusion of the reciprocal ones.'
His saying: 'so it is better to delete it here due to the lack of apparent benefit, and therefore he did not say: the correct thing, and what is said: that its benefit is the inclusion of conjunction and separation, for they exist among the mutakallimun, and it is impossible for them to coexist in one subject from one aspect, not from two aspects, since it is permissible for one body to have conjunction with respect to one body and separation with respect to another. It is refuted by the fact that the existing mode (kawn) is a particular thing that has two considerations, so the existent...
His saying: 'unlike here, so it is better to delete it' — it was objected that the correct thing is to mention that condition, for it has an apparent benefit here too, which is guarding against the exclusion of conjunction and separation, for they exist among the mutakallimun and are two opposites, and they may coexist in one subject, like Zayd's conjunction with his beloved and his separation from his watchman, but not from one aspect. And it will come, God willing, that conjunction subsists in each of the two joined things, not in the aggregate, and likewise separation. And the answer: that opposition only occurs between the ultimate species subsumed under one genus, as he will explicitly state, and it will come in the discussions of modes that conjunction and separation are not two types of absolute mode, but rather the distinction between them is by consideration-based matters external to their essences. Rather, there is not multiple modes in what was mentioned of the depiction, for in it there is one mode that has conjunction relative to the beloved and separation relative to the watchman, as he will indicate in the third purpose of the modes. Yes, it is possible that the mentioned condition guards against the exclusion of knowledge and compound ignorance as well, for they are two opposites according to us, as will come, even though they coexist in one subject, which is the soul, but from two aspects: the belief as it is with respect to Zayd's standing and not as it is with respect to his writing, for example.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a base text about the definition of contradictories (al-mutaqabilan) and the condition 'from one aspect' (min jiha wahida). The commentator begins by explaining that rulings (ahkam) and actions (af'al) are not truly contradictory because they are i'tibari (considerational) — rulings depend on the intellect's consideration of the relation between the divine address and the action, while actions in the sense of effects are abstractive (intiza'i) and have no external existence. Thus, the condition 'from one aspect' is not needed here, though it is beneficial in the definition of correlatives (al-mutada'ifan) to exclude reciprocal terms. The commentator then discusses an objection regarding conjunction (ijtima') and separation (iftiraq), which some claim are opposites that can coexist in one subject but not from one aspect. The response is that opposition only occurs between ultimate species under one genus, and that conjunction and separation are not two types of absolute mode (kawn) but rather differ by external considerations; a single mode can be considered conjunction relative to one thing and separation relative to another. Finally, he notes that the condition could also guard against the exclusion of knowledge and compound ignorance, which are opposites according to the mutakallimun but can coexist in the soul from different aspects.

الأفعال
اعتباري
انتزاعي

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that rulings like 'obligatory' and 'prohibited' are truly opposite because they cannot be applied to the same action.
Correction: Rulings are only considerational opposites; they are not real opposites because they are mental constructs based on relation, not inherent properties of actions.

Key Takeaway
Rulings and actions are not real opposites because they are mental constructs (i'tibari/intiza'i), not external realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this truth strip you of any sense of self-sufficiency in performing good deeds? Will you now begin each action by mentally referring its existence back to Allah alone?
?

Does this liberate you from the burden of thinking you 'own' your obedience? How will you transform this into deeper humility before Allah's command?
?

The Condition 'From One Aspect' and Its Role
Commentator discusses the role of the condition 'from one aspect' in defining opposites, addressing objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the condition 'from one aspect' is necessary in the definition of opposites (al-mutaqabilan) and how it applies to conjunction/separation and knowledge/compound ignorance.

Core Idea
The condition 'from one aspect' ensures that apparent co-existing opposites are not truly contradictory.

The commentator continues by defending the inclusion of the condition 'from one aspect' in the definition of opposites. He addresses objections regarding conjunction and separation, and also notes that knowledge and compound ignorance can coexist in the soul from different aspects. This passage refines our understanding of what constitutes true opposition in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note that this passage responds to an objection about the condition's benefit.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the objection regarding conjunction and separation
Some argue that conjunction and separation are opposites that can coexist in one subject (like Zayd with his beloved and separated from his watchman) but not from one aspect. This seems to require the condition.
3
Understand the commentator's reply
The answer is that opposition only occurs between ultimate species under one genus, and conjunction/separation are not two types of absolute mode (kawn) but differ by external considerations. A single mode can be considered conjunction relative to one thing and separation relative to another.
Thus, the condition is not needed for conjunction/separation.
4
Note the additional case: knowledge and compound ignorance
The commentator suggests that the condition could guard against excluding knowledge and compound ignorance, which are opposites but can coexist in the soul from different aspects.
This shows the condition's potential benefit even here.

The obligated persons are taken in their reality, so they are considerational, and likewise actions in the sense of effects, for the category of action has no existence. And you will know that the condition 'from one aspect' is mentioned in the definition of correlatives to guard against the exclusion of the reciprocal ones, so there it has an apparent benefit, unlike here, so it is better to delete it here. (And as for the unity of subject which must be stipulated in contradictories...
His saying: 'the rulings' — i.e., the five legal rulings.
His saying: 'because the relation etc.' — means that the address related to the action of the obligated person, although it is eternal, is not called a ruling except in terms of its relation to the action, and relation is a thing that the intellect considers after observing the address and the action, and it is not subsisting in the action because it occurs before its existence, so the rulings are not described as being contradictory, even though they are described as impossible to coexist in the intellect's consideration. His saying: 'and likewise actions in the sense of effects, not in the sense of traces, for there is nothing in the external world except the agent and the trace, and effect is an abstractive thing with which the agent is described in the intellect, and there is no contradiction between actions either. This is what I have in solving this speech, and God knows best the intent.
His saying: 'and you will know etc.' is conjoined to his saying: 'then that part', so it is from the commentator's speech.
His saying: 'an apparent benefit, which is the inclusion of the reciprocal ones.'
His saying: 'so it is better to delete it here due to the lack of apparent benefit, and therefore he did not say: the correct thing, and what is said: that its benefit is the inclusion of conjunction and separation, for they exist among the mutakallimun, and it is impossible for them to coexist in one subject from one aspect, not from two aspects, since it is permissible for one body to have conjunction with respect to one body and separation with respect to another. It is refuted by the fact that the existing mode (kawn) is a particular thing that has two considerations, so the existent...
His saying: 'unlike here, so it is better to delete it' — it was objected that the correct thing is to mention that condition, for it has an apparent benefit here too, which is guarding against the exclusion of conjunction and separation, for they exist among the mutakallimun and are two opposites, and they may coexist in one subject, like Zayd's conjunction with his beloved and his separation from his watchman, but not from one aspect. And it will come, God willing, that conjunction subsists in each of the two joined things, not in the aggregate, and likewise separation. And the answer: that opposition only occurs between the ultimate species subsumed under one genus, as he will explicitly state, and it will come in the discussions of modes that conjunction and separation are not two types of absolute mode, but rather the distinction between them is by consideration-based matters external to their essences. Rather, there is not multiple modes in what was mentioned of the depiction, for in it there is one mode that has conjunction relative to the beloved and separation relative to the watchman, as he will indicate in the third purpose of the modes. Yes, it is possible that the mentioned condition guards against the exclusion of knowledge and compound ignorance as well, for they are two opposites according to us, as will come, even though they coexist in one subject, which is the soul, but from two aspects: the belief as it is with respect to Zayd's standing and not as it is with respect to his writing, for example.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a base text about the definition of contradictories (al-mutaqabilan) and the condition 'from one aspect' (min jiha wahida). The commentator begins by explaining that rulings (ahkam) and actions (af'al) are not truly contradictory because they are i'tibari (considerational) — rulings depend on the intellect's consideration of the relation between the divine address and the action, while actions in the sense of effects are abstractive (intiza'i) and have no external existence. Thus, the condition 'from one aspect' is not needed here, though it is beneficial in the definition of correlatives (al-mutada'ifan) to exclude reciprocal terms. The commentator then discusses an objection regarding conjunction (ijtima') and separation (iftiraq), which some claim are opposites that can coexist in one subject but not from one aspect. The response is that opposition only occurs between ultimate species under one genus, and that conjunction and separation are not two types of absolute mode (kawn) but rather differ by external considerations; a single mode can be considered conjunction relative to one thing and separation relative to another. Finally, he notes that the condition could also guard against the exclusion of knowledge and compound ignorance, which are opposites according to the mutakallimun but can coexist in the soul from different aspects.

Key Terms
المتقابلان
المتضايفان
الاجتماع والافتراق
الكون
العلم والجهل المركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that conjunction and separation are true opposites that require the condition 'from one aspect' to explain their coexistence.
Correction: They are not true opposites because they are not distinct types under a genus; a single mode can be considered conjunction relative to one thing and separation relative to another.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator argue that conjunction and separation are not true opposites?
A) Because they cannot coexist in the same subject.
B) Because they are not two types of absolute mode (kawn) but differ by external considerations.
C) Because they are both found in the same body.
D) Because they are not mentioned in the definition of opposites.

Key Takeaway
The condition 'from one aspect' is crucial for defining true opposites, and apparent co-existing pairs like conjunction/separation are resolved by understanding they are not distinct types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a person you have labeled as 'contradictory' (e.g., kind sometimes, harsh other times). Can you re-evaluate that judgment by considering they might be acting from different aspects (like the one mode with two considerations)? Write down one such judgment and how the condition 'from one aspect' changes your view. Then, resolve to treat them with fairness by not forcing them into a single category.
?
The lesson shows that knowledge and compound ignorance can coexist in the soul from different aspects. Reflect on your own heart: where do you hold firm belief about something (e.g., a religious truth) but also remain ignorant of its implications for your daily life? Identify one such area and commit to seeking the missing knowledge, asking Allah to unite your belief and action.
?
The commentator discusses that opposition only occurs between ultimate species under one genus. In your own life, conflicts often arise from misclassifying differences as fundamental opposites. Identify a recurring conflict (within yourself or with others) where each side may be focusing on different aspects of the same reality. How can you shift from seeing 'opposition' to seeing a single reality with multiple considerations? Take one step today to apply this insight.

Mu'tazila Rejection of Unity of Place for Contraries
Explains the Mu'tazila view that contraries do not require a single place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila justify the coexistence of contraries in different loci without requiring a single place, and the extension of this principle to divine attributes.

Core Idea
Contraries can coexist in different places if they share the same time.

The Mu'tazila theologians challenged the classical view that contraries must occupy the same place. They argued that knowledge and ignorance of the same object can reside in different parts of the heart, and some extended this to God's will and hatred without a physical location. This passage presents their reasoning and the philosophical distinctions involved.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full literal translation. Let the author's argument flow before analyzing.
Notice the use of parentheses for glosses.
2
Identify the two key arguments
The passage first presents the general Mu'tazila principle rejecting unity of place, illustrated with knowledge and ignorance in the heart. Then it presents an extension by Abu al-Hudhayl regarding divine will and hatred without a place.
The extension is introduced with 'بل زادوا عليه'.
3
Reflect on the theological method
Consider why the Mu'tazila felt it necessary to argue this way. How does their rational approach differ from other schools?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ضرورة جواز اجتماعهما في زمان واحد في محلين فلم يشترطه المعتزلة، فإنهم قالوا : العلم بالشيء ( كالسواد مثلا ) إذا قام بجزء من القلب فإنه يضاد قيام الجهل ( بذلك الشيء ) بجزء آخر من القلب . ( وإلا اتصف الجملة بهما ) أي إن لم يكن بينهما تضاد، وقام العلم بجزء، والجهل بجزء آخر اتصف جملة القلب بكونها عالمة بذلك الشيء، وجاهلة به معاً، (إذ) الصفات (التابعة للحياة)، كالعلم والجهل والقدرة وغيرها (إذا قامت بجزء من شيء (ثبت حكمها)، كالعالمية والجاهلية والقادرية ( للجملة ) أي لمجموع ( ذلك الشيء عندهم، بل زادوا عليه ) أي على عدم اشتراط اتحاد المحل، فلم يشترطوا في التضاد ( المحل، إذ قالوا : إرادة الله تضاد كراهيته وهما صفتان له ( حادثتان لا في (محل أي ليستا في ذاته ؛ لامتناع قيام الحوادث به ولا في غيره لامتناع قيام الصفة بغير موصوفها ، وهما متضادتان؛ لامتناع اجتماع حكميهما في ذاته، أعنى كونه مريداً وكارهاً معاً لشيء واحد . وسيرد عليك
قوله : فلم يشترط المعتزلة وقالوا الضدان معنيان يستحيل اجتماعهما لذاتيهما الجملة، سواء كان في محل واحد أو في محلين. قوله : ( قالوا إلخ) يعني أن هذا العلم والجهل من حيث قيامهما بمحلين، فلا يكون اتحاد المحل شرطاً، فلا يرد أنه إذا كان قيامهما بمحلين مستحيلاً كان قيامهما بمحل واحد مستحيلاً بطريق الأولى فهما داخلان وإن اعتبر اتحاد المحل والمراد الجهل المركب فإن الجهل البسيط عدمي وهذا عند المعتزلة القائلين بتضاد العلم والجهل المركب إذا كانا متعلقين بشيء واحد، لا عند من يقول بتماثلهما .
قوله : (بجزء من القلب هذا على ما ذهب إليه المليون من أن محل العلم القلب، كما يدل عليه ظاهر الآيات، وأنه مركب من أجزاء لا تتجزء، فلا تخير بخلط المذاهب . قوله : ( بل زادوا عليه أي بعضهم وهو أبو الهذيل ومن تبعه حيث ذهبوا إلى أنه تعالى مريد بإرادة حادثة لا في محل .

Fourth Observatory - Ninth Goal: The two (contraries) according to the theologians are three divisions. Necessity of their being together at one time in two places—so the Mu'tazila did not require it. For they said: Knowledge of a thing (e.g., blackness) when it subsists in a part of the heart, it opposes the subsistence of ignorance (of that thing) in another part of the heart. (Otherwise, the whole would be described by both) meaning if there is no opposition between them, and knowledge subsists in a part and ignorance in another part, the whole heart would be described as being knowledgeable of that thing and ignorant of it together. (Since) the attributes (dependent on life), like knowledge, ignorance, power and others, (when they subsist in a part of something, their ruling is established) like being knowledgeable, ignorant, and powerful, (for the whole) i.e., the entirety (of that thing according to them. Rather, they added to it) i.e., to not requiring unity of place, so they did not require in opposition (the place, for they said: God's will opposes His hatred, and they are two attributes of Him (incident without a place) i.e., not in His essence; because it is impossible for incidents to subsist in Him, nor in other than Him because it is impossible for an attribute to subsist without its possessor. And they are two opposites; because it is impossible for their two rulings to be together in His essence, i.e., His being at once willing and hating one thing. And it will come to you... His statement: 'So the Mu'tazila did not require it' and they said: The two opposites are two entities whose combination by their essences is impossible—that sentence, whether in one place or two places. His statement: ('They said' etc.) means that this knowledge and ignorance, from the perspective of their subsistence in two places, so unity of place is not a condition, so it cannot be objected that if their subsistence in two places is impossible, then their subsistence in one place is a fortiori impossible—so they are included even if unity of place is considered. And the intended ignorance is composite ignorance, for simple ignorance is non-existent. And this is according to the Mu'tazila who affirm opposition between knowledge and composite ignorance when they are related to one thing, not according to those who say they are similar. His statement: ('in a part of the heart') this is according to what the philosophers held, that the locus of knowledge is the heart, as indicated by the apparent meaning of the verses, and that it is composed of indivisible parts, so do not become confused by mixing of doctrines. His statement: ('Rather, they added to it') i.e., some of them, namely Abu al-Hudhayl and those who followed him, as they held that He, exalted, is willing by an incident will not in a place. His statement: ('And it will come to you') i.e., at the end of the discussion of cause and effect, that the ruling of an attribute does not go beyond its locus, and that the accident (meaning the attribute) does not subsist in itself.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines two divisions of 'two contraries' according to theologians and focuses on the Mu'tazila position regarding the condition of unity of place. The commentator first explains that the Mu'tazila do not require that contraries reside in a single place; they allow them to be in different places simultaneously, provided they are in the same time. They illustrate this with the example of knowledge and ignorance of the same thing subsisting in different parts of the heart. If there is no direct opposition between them, the whole heart would be described as both knowledgeable and ignorant of that thing, because for them, attributes dependent on life (like knowledge) when present in a part, their ruling (e.g., being knowledgeable) applies to the whole entity. Then the commentator notes that some Mu'tazila (like Abu al-Hudhayl) went further and dispensed with the condition of place altogether for divine attributes: they held that God's will and hatred are opposite attributes that subsist without a place, yet they are opposites because their rulings cannot coexist in His essence (i.e., willing and hating the same thing). The commentary clarifies each quoted phrase: the Mu'tazila define opposites as entities whose coexistence is impossible by their essences, regardless of place. The knowledge and ignorance under discussion are composite ignorance, not simple non-existence. The loc. of knowledge is the heart (as per Muslim philosophers and apparent meaning of Quran). The addition by some Mu'tazila refers to Abu al-Hudhayl's view of God's will as incident without a locus. Finally, the commentator hints at an upcoming objection about the ruling of an attribute not extending beyond its locus, which will be addressed later.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that contraries always require a single place to coexist.
Correction: The Mu'tazila reject this and allow contraries to exist in different loci as long as they are at the same time.

Checkpoint
What condition do the Mu'tazila reject for the existence of two contraries?
A) That they must exist at the same time.
B) That they must exist in the same place (unity of place).
C) That they must be related to the same object.
D) That they must be attributes of life.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila demonstrate that contraries can coexist without a single place, using the coexistence of knowledge and ignorance in different parts of the heart and the coexistence of God's will and hatred without a physical locus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you felt two opposing emotions or beliefs pulling you in different directions—like faith and doubt, or love and anger. How does the Mu'tazila idea that contraries can coexist in different parts of the same heart challenge you to make space for these tensions in your own soul? What is one specific area where you will stop forcing yourself to choose one feeling and instead let both exist, trusting that they do not define the whole of you?
?
The Mu'tazila say that God's will and hatred are opposite attributes that are not located anywhere. If God's attributes are beyond physical and psychological location, what does that mean for how you approach your own contradictory thoughts and feelings about Him? Are you willing to let go of the need to 'place' God's actions in a neat box? Write down one thing you will do this week to worship a God who is not confined by your categories.

Objection from Death and Life Against Mu'tazila on Contraries
Objection using death and life to challenge Mu'tazila on opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the example of death and life challenges the Mu'tazila argument that knowledge and ignorance must be opposites because they cannot coexist.

Core Idea
Impossible coexistence doesn't mean opposites—death and life prove it.

The Mu'tazila argued that knowledge and ignorance cannot coexist in the same heart, so they must be opposites. But what about death and life? They too cannot coexist, yet the Mu'tazila do not consider them opposites. This objection strikes at the heart of their reasoning. In this lesson, we examine the objection and the responses it provoked.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the objection is built: first a principle, then the example, then a challenge.
2
Identify the Objection's Logic
The objector uses death and life as a counterexample. Ask: Why does this undermine the Mu'tazila claim? How does it relate to the principle that the ruling of an attribute doesn't go beyond its locus?
3
Analyze the Responses
The passage contains multiple responses: from the author of al-Qunya, and the discussion about the third division. Identify each response and how it reinforces the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن حكم الصفة لا يتعدى عن محلها وأن المعنى أي العرض لا يقوم بنفسه . (و) مع ذلك ( يرد عليهم الموت والحياة فإنهما ليسا ضدين عندهم، مع امتناع اجتماعهما ) . وإذا لم يكن بينهما تضاد عندهم مع ثبوت امتناع الاجتماع، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون العلم القائم بجزء، والجهل القائم بجزء آخر ممتنعي الاجتماع، لما ذكروه، ولا يكون بينهما تضاد قال صاحب القنية : إن أوجب أصلكم امتناع ثبوت علم وجهل، كما صورتموه فلم عللتم ذلك بالتضاد بينهما ؟ ألستم قلتم يستحيل اجتماع العلم : والموت مع أنهما ليسا بضدين عندكم؟ فهلا قلتم إن العلم والجهل لا يثبتان في جزئين من القلب وليس المانع من ذلك تضادهما ؟ ( وثالثها ) : أي ثالث أقسام الاثنين محل الصفة، فالقول بأن الصفات التابعة للحياة إذا قامت بجزء يثبت حكمها للجملة باطل فالقول بالتضاد بين العلم والجهل المذكورين باطل . قوله : ( وأن المعنى أي العرض لا يقوم إلخ) أي في بحث الأعراض ، فالقول بالإرادة الحادثة لا في محل باطل .

قوله : ( مع أنهما ليسا بضدين عندكم لعدم استحالة اجتماعهما لذاتيهما، لكن لا يخفى أنه لا فائدة حينئذ بالتقييد بقوله : عندكم . قوله: (وليس) المانع من ذلك تضادهما لأن استحالة اجتماعهما ليس لذاتيهما، بل لامتناع اجتماع حكميهما بينهما.
قوله : ( يرد عليهم الموت والحياة) إذا ثبت كون الموت وجودياً، وعدم قولهم بالتضاد
قوله : (قال صاحب القنية إلخ) قيل : كان الشارح استبعد عدم جعل الموت ضداً للحياة على تقدير وجوديته، فنقل كلام القنية إشارة إلى احتمال خلل في النقل من المصنف، فإن كلامه في العلم والموت لا في الموت ،والحياة لكنه يندفع عنه باعتبار قيد لذاتيهما في تعريف الضدين؛ إذ ليس عدم اجتماع الموت والعلم لذاتيهما، وكان المصنف غير كلامه لذلك، والحق أن ما ذكره المصنف مأخوذ من أبكار الأفكار فإن الاعتراض هناك بالموت والحياة .
That the ruling of an attribute does not go beyond its locus, and that the accident (meaning the attribute) does not subsist in itself. (And) despite that (it is objected against them: death and life, for they are not opposites according to them, yet their coexistence is impossible). And when there is no opposition between them according to them, while there is impossibility of coexistence, then why may not it be that knowledge subsisting in a part and ignorance subsisting in another part are impossible of coexistence, for the reason they mentioned, yet there is no opposition between them? Said the author of al-Qunya: If your principle requires the impossibility of establishing knowledge and ignorance as you have pictured, then why did you explain that by the opposition between them? Did you not say that knowledge and death cannot coexist, even though they are not opposites according to you? So why do you not say that knowledge and ignorance are not established in two parts of the heart, and the preventer is not their opposition? (And the third of them): i.e., the third division of the two is the locus of the attribute. So the statement that the attributes dependent on life, when they subsist in a part, their ruling is established for the whole, is false—so the statement of opposition between the mentioned knowledge and ignorance is false. His statement: ('And that the accident does not subsist' etc.) i.e., in the discussion of accidents, so the statement about the incident will not in a place is false. His statement: ('It is objected against them: death and life') on the supposition that death is existent, as indicated by the apparent meaning of His statement, 'He created death and life' [Surah al-Mulk: 2], and the summary is that we do not concede that there is opposition between the mentioned knowledge and ignorance, for the impossibility of their coexistence does not necessitate opposition, as in the case of death and life according to you. So the mentioned objection is a refutation and support, and it is not a contradiction as it might seem from his statement: 'And it is objected against them: death and life.' His statement: ('Said the author of al-Qunya' etc.) since it has not been established that the one who denied opposition between life and death, and that death is existent, is one person; rather, only two statements from them are established, so perhaps the speaker is multiple as is apparent, for the denial of opposition between them while death is existent is very improbable. The commentator, may his secret be sanctified, transmitted the words of the author of al-Qunya and that he raised the objection concerning death and knowledge. His statement: ('While they are not opposites according to you') because their coexistence is not impossible by their essences, but it is not hidden that there is no benefit then in the restriction by 'according to you'. His statement: ('And the preventer is not') their opposition because the impossibility of their coexistence is not by their essences, but rather due to the impossibility of the coexistence of their rulings between them. His statement: ('It is objected against them: death and life') when it is established that death is existent, and their denial of opposition. His statement: ('Said the author of al-Qunya' etc.) It was said: The commentator found it improbable that death is not made an opposite of life on the supposition of its existence, so he transmitted the words of al-Qunya as an indication of a possible flaw in the transmission from the author, for his words concern knowledge and death, not death and life. But it is rebutted by considering the condition of 'by their essences' in the definition of the two opposites; for it is not that the non-coexistence of death and knowledge is by their essences, and the author changed his words accordingly. The truth is that what the author mentioned is taken from Abkar al-Afkar, for the objection there is about death and life.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents and discusses an objection against the Mu'tazila position. The commentator begins by stating the principle that the ruling of an attribute does not extend beyond its locus (a point that will be used). Then the objection is introduced: death and life are not considered opposites by the Mu'tazila, yet they cannot coexist. Therefore, the impossibility of coexistence does not necessarily indicate opposition. The same could apply to knowledge and ignorance in different parts of the heart: they might be impossible to coexist without being opposites. The objection is further reinforced by the author of al-Qunya, who asks: if your own principle requires the impossibility of knowledge and ignorance coexisting in two parts, why do you attribute it to opposition? They themselves say knowledge and death are impossible to coexist but are not opposites. So why not say the same for knowledge and ignorance? Then the commentator notes that the third division of 'the two' is 'locus of the attribute', and this refutes the Mu'tazila claim that attributes dependent on life, when in a part, apply to the whole—thus the opposition claim is false. The commentary then explains each phrase: the objection assumes death is existent (based on Quran), and the response is that we do not concede opposition between knowledge and ignorance; the impossibility of their coexistence does not entail opposition. The objection is a refutation (man') and support (saned). The commentator also analyzes the words of the author of al-Qunya, noting that the denial of opposition between life and death while death is existent is improbable, so maybe the objector is not the same person. Finally, a possible flaw in the transmission is discussed: the original objection might have been about knowledge and death, not death and life. But the truth is that the author took it from al-Abkar where the objection is about death and life.

صاحب القنية
حكم الصفة
محل الصفة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things cannot coexist, they must be opposites.
Correction: The example of death and life shows that impossibility of coexistence does not necessarily imply opposition; there may be other reasons.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what do death and life demonstrate about the Mu'tazila's reasoning?
A) That death and life are opposites.
B) That impossibility of coexistence does not necessarily indicate opposition.
C) That knowledge and ignorance must be opposites.
D) That the ruling of an attribute extends beyond its locus.

Key Takeaway
Death and life serve as a powerful refutation: they cannot coexist yet are not opposites, breaking the Mu'tazila's inference from impossibility to opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that the impossibility of two things coexisting does not automatically make them opposites. In your own life, when have you assumed that a conflict or incompatibility was inherent in the things themselves, only to later discover the real cause lay elsewhere? Will you commit to pausing before concluding that something is fundamentally opposed to your beliefs?
?
The Mu'tazila were caught in a contradiction: their own principle (that death and life are not opposites) undermined their argument. Are you willing to examine your own deeply held convictions with the same rigorous honesty, even if it means exposing inconsistencies? What is one belief you hold that you have never seriously questioned, and how might this lesson challenge it?
?
Death and life are mentioned as a powerful reminder of the Hereafter. Amidst your intellectual pursuits and daily distractions, do you truly reflect on the reality that death will overtake you? How will you let this knowledge transform your priorities starting today?

Defining the Third Pair: Mutakhalifan
Defines mutakhalifan and distinguishes from mithlan and diddan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of mutakhalifan (the different pair), how it differs from identicals and opposites, and why sharing common attributes does not disqualify two things from being different.

Core Idea
Two things are different if they share no essential attribute but can coexist.

In this passage, the author defines the third type of relational pair: mutakhalifan (the two different). This concept is crucial for distinguishing between identity, opposition, and mere difference. Let's explore the precise definition, alternative formulations, and the important nuance that sharing common attributes like existence does not violate the definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the primary definition
Read the first part of the Arabic passage where the author gives the definition of mutakhalifan as two existents that do not share an essential attribute and whose coexistence is not impossible in a subject.
Notice the two conditions: no sharing in essential attributes and no impossibility of coexistence.
2
Understand the exclusion of identicals and opposites
See how the first condition excludes identicals (which share all essential attributes) and the second condition excludes opposites (which cannot coexist in the same subject from the same aspect).
Opposites like black and white cannot be in the same subject at the same time and aspect.
3
Consider the alternative view
Read the alternative formulation that treats opposites as a subset of mutakhalifan, making the division binary rather than threefold.
This view simplifies the classification but changes the nature of the pair.
4
Grasp the nuance about sharing common attributes
Understand the clarification that sharing common essential attributes like existence or subsistence in a subject does not harm the definition, as these are universal attributes that apply to all existents or all accidents.
This prevents a too-strict interpretation of 'no sharing in all essential attributes'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتخالفان وهما غير الأولين) أي غير المثلين والضدين (فرسمه) أي رسم الثالث أن يقال : المتخالفان ( هما موجودان لا يشتركان في صفة النفس)، أي في الصفات النفسية، فخرج عن الحد المثلان ، ( ولا يمتنع اجتماعهما لذاتيهما في محل من جهة، فخرج عنه الضدان. ( وقيل ) : المراد بالمتخالفين ( غير المثلين فيكفي ) في رسمهما حينئذ أن يقال : هما ( موجودان لا يشتركان في صفة النفس) أي في جميعها، فيخرج المثلان ويكون الضدان قسما من المتخالفين، فتكون قسمة الاثنين ثنائية . ولما كان المقصود من نفي الاشتراك المذكور في تعريف المتخالفين إخراج المثلين كان محمولاً على نفي الاشتراك في جميع صفات النفس، كما ذكرناه، وذلك لا ينافي أن يشتركا في بعضها، فلذلك أشار إليه وإلى ما يتفرع عليه، فقال : ( ولا يضر الاشتراك ( بين المتخالفين وإن كانا ضدين ( في بعض صفة النفس كالوجود، فإنه صفة نفسية مشتركة بين جميع الموجودات، والقيام بالمحل فإنه صفة نفسية مشتركة بين الأعراض كلها وكالعرضية والجوهرية، فإنهما قوله : ( فإنه صفة نفسية ) أي منتزعة من نفس العرض، حتى لو تصور عرض غير قائم بمحل لا يكون عرضاً، بخلاف التحيز للأجسام ، فإنه منتزع باعتبار الحيز، حتى لو تصور جسم من غير حيز يكون جسماً، فما قيل الفرق بين القيام بالمحل والتحيز بأن الأول صفة نفسية، والثاني معنوية تحكم وهم . قوله: (فخرج عن الحد (المثلان أطلق الرسم أولاً على التعريف المذكور؛ إشارة إلى جواز أن يكون له ماهية ملزومة لذلك المفهوم المساوي لها والحد ثانياً بناء على أنه مفهوم اصطلاحي، فالظاهر أن ليس له حقيقة غيره والتعريف ثالثاً نظراً إلى الاحتمالين، أو لأن المراد بالعبارات معنى واحد، إذ قد تستعمل مترادفة . قوله : ( والقيام بالمحل فإنه صفة نفسية مشتركة بين الأعراض إلخ) سيذكر في أوائل موقف الأعراض أن قبول الأعراض ليس بصفة نفسية للجواهر، لأن كون الشيء قابلا لغيره إنما يعقل بالقياس إلى الغير وعد هاهنا القيام بالمحل صفة نفسية للأعراض أن القيام بالغير أيضاً مع إنما يعقل بالقياس إلى الغير وهو المقوم به أعني المحل فإن قلت القيام بالمحل معتبر في مفهوم العرض، ولا كذلك قبول الأعراض بالنسبة إلى الجوهر . قلت هذا إنما يفيد إذا كان مفهوم العرض ذاتياً لما تحته إذ لو كان عارضاً له لكان الفرق بين القيام بالمحل، وبين الحدوث في كون الأول صفة نفسية للأعراض، والثاني صفة معنوية للحوادث، بناءً على الاحتياج في وصف الحادث به إلى تعقل أمر زائد عليه وهو العدم السابق المعتبر في مفهومه، محل تأمل على أن عموم العرض لو كان ذاتيا لما تحته كان مفهوم الجوهر، أعني المتحيز بالذات كذلك، فلم يعد لتحيز للجوهر صفة معنوية والقيام بالمحل للعرض صفة النفسية فتدبر.
The two different ones (al-mutakhalifān) are other than the first two, i.e., other than the two identicals (al-mithlān) and the two opposites (al-ḍiddān). Its definition (rasm) i.e., the definition of the third, is that it is said: The two different ones are two existents that do not share in an essential attribute (ṣifat al-nafs), i.e., in the essential attributes, so the two identicals are excluded from the definition, and their coexistence is not impossible in themselves in a subject from one aspect, so the two opposites are excluded from it. And it was said: What is meant by the two different ones is 'other than the two identicals,' so it suffices in their definition then that it is said: They are two existents that do not share in an essential attribute, i.e., in all of them, so the two identicals are excluded and the two opposites become a subdivision of the two different ones, so the division of the two becomes binary. And since the purpose of the mentioned negation of sharing in the definition of the two different ones is to exclude the two identicals, it is taken as the negation of sharing in all essential attributes, as we mentioned, and that does not contradict that they share in some of them, therefore he pointed to it and to what branches from it, and said: (And sharing does not harm) between the two different ones, even if they are two opposites, in some essential attribute, such as existence, for it is an essential attribute common to all existents, and subsistence in a subject (qiyām bil-maḥall) for it is an essential attribute common to all accidents, and like accidentality and substantiality, for they are... His statement: (For it is an essential attribute) i.e., derived from the essence of the accident, such that if an accident was conceived not subsisting in a subject it would not be an accident, unlike spatial occupation (taḥayyuz) for bodies, for it is derived by consideration of spatial location, such that if a body was conceived without spatial location it would still be a body. So what was said about the difference between subsistence in a subject and spatial occupation, that the first is an essential attribute and the second is a mental attribute, is ruled out. His statement: (So the two identicals are excluded from the definition) He used 'definition' (rasm) first for the mentioned definition, indicating the permissibility that it have an essence that is entailed by that concept equal to it, and 'limit' (ḥadd) secondly based on its being a conventional concept, for it is apparent that it has no reality other than it, and 'definition' (taʿrīf) thirdly considering the two possibilities, or because what is meant by the expressions is one meaning, since they may be used synonymously. His statement: (And subsistence in a subject is an essential attribute common to accidents etc.) He will mention at the beginning of the station on accidents that receptivity of accidents is not an essential attribute of substances, because the fact that a thing is receptive to another is only understood in relation to another, and here subsistence in a subject is counted as an essential attribute of accidents because subsistence in another also is only understood in relation to another, which is that in which it subsists, i.e., the subject. If you say: Subsistence in a subject is considered in the concept of accident, but receptivity of accidents is not so for substance. I say: This only benefits if the concept of accident is essential to what is under it, for if it were accidental to it, then the difference between subsistence in a subject and temporality (ḥudūth) would be that the first is an essential attribute of accidents and the second is a mental attribute of temporal things, based on the need in describing the temporal thing by it to conceive an additional matter beyond it, which is the prior non-existence considered in its concept—this is subject to reflection. For if the universal of accident were essential to what is under it, then the concept of substance, namely the self-subsisting spatial entity, would be likewise, so spatial occupation would not be considered a mental attribute for substance, and subsistence in a subject would not be an essential attribute for accident. So reflect.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of pair (mutakhalifan) in the classification of two things. It begins by stating that mutakhalifan are distinct from both identicals (mithlan) and opposites (diddan). The definition (rasm) is given: two existents that do not share in any essential attribute (sifat al-nafs)—this excludes identicals—and whose coexistence is not impossible in themselves in a single subject from any aspect—this excludes opposites. An alternative view holds that mutakhalifan simply means 'other than identicals,' so that opposites become a subset. The passage then addresses a nuance: since the purpose of excluding identicals is central, the negation of sharing applies to all essential attributes, but sharing in some attributes (like existence or subsistence in a subject) does not harm the definition. The explanation then delves into what constitutes an essential attribute, using subsistence in a subject (qiyam bil-mahal) and spatial occupation (tahayyuz) as examples. The commentator also discusses the terminology of 'rasm' and 'hadd' and ends with a reflection on whether certain attributes are essential or accidental.

Key Terms
متخالفان (mutakhalifān)
مثلان (mithlān)
ضدان (ḍiddān)
صفة النفس (ṣifat al-nafs)
قيام بالمحل (qiyām bil-maḥall)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things share any attribute at all, they cannot be mutakhalifan.
Correction: Sharing universal attributes like existence or subsistence in a subject does not disqualify them; the definition only negates sharing in all essential attributes.

Checkpoint
According to the primary definition in the passage, what two conditions define mutakhalifan?
A) They must be different in all attributes and cannot coexist.
B) They do not share in any essential attribute, and their coexistence is not impossible in a subject.
C) They are not identicals and not opposites.
D) They share some attributes but not all.

Key Takeaway
Mutakhalifan are two existents that share no essential attributes, but may share common attributes like existence or subsistence in a subject.

What does it mean that they are distinct yet compatible? Spend a few minutes reflecting on this and then make a resolve to trust that harmony in a specific situation you face this week.
?
The passage distinguishes between essential and common attributes. In your own life, do you sometimes confuse what is essential about your identity as a believer with what is merely common or accidental? How can this lesson help you clarify your self-conception before Allah? Commit to one specific change in how you view yourself or others based on this distinction.
?
The alternative view classifies opposites as a subset of the different. How does this remind you that even apparent contradictions in your life—like trials and blessings—can coexist as part of a larger divine plan? What specific trial are you facing right now that you need to see as a form of 'different but compatible' in Allah's wisdom? The Mu'tazila Position on Coexistence of Similars
States Mu'tazila's general allowance and minority exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila's general position that two similar entities can coexist, and the exception made by a minority regarding two similar movements in one location.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila mostly allow two similars to coexist, except for two similar movements.

This lesson introduces the Mu'tazila's stance on the coexistence of two similar entities. While the majority of the Mu'tazila affirm absolute permissibility, a subgroup denies the possibility of two similar movements in the same place. This sets the stage for the Ash'arite proofs that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic sentence about the Mu'tazila and their exception. Then read the translation.
2
Identify the two groups
Notice that the Mu'tazila are split: most allow coexistence absolutely, a small group (shardhima) says no to two similar movements.
Remember: شرذمة means a small group.
3
Understand the exception
The exception is specifically about 'two similar movements' in one location. Why would they make this exception? Consider the nature of movement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنعه المعتزلة، واتفقوا على جواز اجتماعهما مطلقاً إلا شرذمة منهم، فإنهم قالوا لا تجتمع حركتان متماثلتان في محل. لنا في إثبات امتناع الاجتماع مسالك أربعة: الأول: يجب على تقدير اجتماعهما في محل عدم تمايزهما بالذات وبالعوارض أيضاً، لأن الذات أعني الماهية مشتركة بينهما، وكذا لوازمها من الصفات النفسية مشتركة أيضاً، فلا امتياز إلا بالعوارض المشخصة.
And the Mu'tazila denied it, and they agreed on the permissibility of their co-existence absolutely, except a small group of them, for they said: two similar movements do not co-exist in one location. For us, in establishing the impossibility of co-existence, there are four ways: The first: it is necessary, on the assumption of their co-existence in one location, their non-distinction by essence and by accidents as well, because the essence, meaning the quiddity, is shared between them, and likewise its concomitants of the psychic attributes are also shared, so there is no distinction except by individuating accidents.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's general position that two similars can always co-exist, except a minority who exclude two similar movements in one location. Then the author (presumably al-Iji) states that the Ash'arites have four proofs for the impossibility. The first proof is given: if two similars were to co-exist in the same location, they would not be distinguishable by essence (dhat) or by accidents (awarid). Since their essence (quiddity, mahiyya) is identical, and their essential attributes (sifat nafsiyya) are also identical, the only possible distinction would be by individuating accidents (al-awarid al-mushakhkhisa). However, if the two similars share the same location, the individuating accidents (which typically differentiate individuals) would also be shared or insufficient to differentiate, leading to the impossibility of two distinct entities—thus they cannot co-exist. This is a classic argument from the principle of indiscernibles.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
شرذمة
حركتان متماثلتان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the exception that a small group of Mu'tazila made?
A) Two similar entities cannot coexist at all.
B) Two similar movements cannot coexist in one location.
C) Two similar essences cannot coexist.
D) Two similar accidents cannot coexist.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila generally permit coexistence of similars, but a minority excludes two similar movements in the same place.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
When you encounter a theological disagreement, are you more concerned with winning the argument or with understanding the nuance? Reflect on a time you missed an exception like this one.
