First Proof: Indistinguishability of Two Similars
Presents the first proof for impossibility of coexistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Ash'arite proof for the impossibility of two similars coexisting, based on the argument from indistinguishability.

Core Idea
Two similars cannot coexist because they would become indistinguishable.

In this lesson, we examine the first of four Ash'arite proofs that two similar entities cannot coexist in one location. The proof uses the principle that if two things share the same essence and essential attributes, they would be indistinguishable unless distinguished by accidents. However, if they share the same location, the accidents cannot differentiate them, leading to a contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the Arabic text of the first proof carefully. It is a conditional argument: if two similars coexist, then they must be indistinguishable, which is impossible.
The proof is concise.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify terms: ذات (essence), ماهية (quiddity), صفات نفسية (psychic/essential attributes), عوارض مشخصة (individuating accidents).
These are technical kalam terms.
3
Follow the logical chain
The proof assumes coexistence, then argues: essence (mahiyya) is shared, essential attributes are shared, so only accidents can distinguish them. But in the same location, individuating accidents cannot differentiate, so the two entities would be indistinguishable and hence not truly two.
This is akin to Leibniz's principle of indiscernibles.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Why is indistinguishability a problem? Two distinct entities must be distinguishable by at least something. The proof shows that under coexistence, they are not, so coexistence is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنعه المعتزلة، واتفقوا على جواز اجتماعهما مطلقاً إلا شرذمة منهم، فإنهم قالوا لا تجتمع حركتان متماثلتان في محل. لنا في إثبات امتناع الاجتماع مسالك أربعة: الأول: يجب على تقدير اجتماعهما في محل عدم تمايزهما بالذات وبالعوارض أيضاً، لأن الذات أعني الماهية مشتركة بينهما، وكذا لوازمها من الصفات النفسية مشتركة أيضاً، فلا امتياز إلا بالعوارض المشخصة.
And the Mu'tazila denied it, and they agreed on the permissibility of their co-existence absolutely, except a small group of them, for they said: two similar movements do not co-exist in one location. For us, in establishing the impossibility of co-existence, there are four ways: The first: it is necessary, on the assumption of their co-existence in one location, their non-distinction by essence and by accidents as well, because the essence, meaning the quiddity, is shared between them, and likewise its concomitants of the psychic attributes are also shared, so there is no distinction except by individuating accidents.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila's general position that two similars can always co-exist, except a minority who exclude two similar movements in one location. Then the author (presumably al-Iji) states that the Ash'arites have four proofs for the impossibility. The first proof is given: if two similars were to co-exist in the same location, they would not be distinguishable by essence (dhat) or by accidents (awarid). Since their essence (quiddity, mahiyya) is identical, and their essential attributes (sifat nafsiyya) are also identical, the only possible distinction would be by individuating accidents (al-awarid al-mushakhkhisa). However, if the two similars share the same location, the individuating accidents (which typically differentiate individuals) would also be shared or insufficient to differentiate, leading to the impossibility of two distinct entities—thus they cannot co-exist. This is a classic argument from the principle of indiscernibles.

Key Terms
ذات
ماهية
صفات نفسية
عوارض مشخصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the proof assumes two things must be distinct by accidents; it argues that if they are not distinct by essence, they must be distinct by accidents, but coexistence makes that impossible.
Correction: The proof does not assume distinction by accidents; it shows that essence and essential attributes are identical, so the only possible distinction is by accidents, but that fails under coexistence.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what makes two similars indistinguishable if they coexist?
A) Their essences are different.
B) Their essential attributes are different.
C) Their individuating accidents are the same.
D) Their essence and essential attributes are identical, and accidents cannot differentiate.

Key Takeaway
The first proof argues that two similars coexisting would be indistinguishable, which contradicts their being two distinct entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever masked your true self to appear identical to others in virtue or piety, only to realize that what made you distinct before Allah were the individual struggles hidden in your heart? How can you cultivate those 'individuating accidents' of sincere repentance and unique devotion that no one else can imitate?
?
If two similars cannot coexist because they would be indistinguishable, what does that say about the authenticity of your faith? Are your actions mere replicas of others, or do they bear the stamp of your personal encounter with Allah? What must change so that your deeds are truly yours?
?
This proof shows that distinction comes only through individuating accidents—the particular circumstances and choices that define you. In what area of your life have you been relying on shared 'essence' (like general knowledge or outward practice) while neglecting the personal, costly acts that make your faith distinct and real before Allah? What is one such act you will undertake today?

The First Proof: Two Similars Cannot Coexist
Al-Jurjani presents the first proof that two similars cannot coexist, including an initial objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that two similars cannot coexist in one locus and why it leads to the negation of similarity.

Core Idea
Two similars in one locus become indistinguishable, so twoness is impossible.

This lesson introduces the first proof (maslak) that two similars cannot coexist. The proof argues that if they did, they would share all essential and accidental attributes due to the single locus, making them indistinguishable. Without distinction, there is no twoness, and without twoness, similarity is meaningless. This is a foundational argument in kalam about the nature of attributes and substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the first proof. Notice the structure: a statement of impossibility, an objection, a mention of the Mu'tazila position, and then the proof itself.
2
Identify key concepts
Focus on the terms 'متماثلين' (similars), 'ذات' (essence), 'ماهية' (quiddity), 'صفات نفسية' (essential attributes), 'عوارض مشخصة' (individuating accidents). Understand their roles in the argument.
3
Trace the proof step by step
The proof assumes two similars in one locus. It then shows no distinction by essence or accidents, hence no twoness. Follow each step carefully.
4
Reflect on the role of the locus
Why is the locus so important? The proof argues that the unity of the locus makes the accidents common. Reflect on how this affects the possibility of distinction.

المرصد الرابع - المقصد العاشر : كل متماثلين لا يجتمعان

قلت : لا اندراج أيضاً، إذ ليس امتناع الاجتماع لذاتيهما، ألا ترى أن جماعة جوزوا اجتماعهما وأيضاً المراد بالمعنيين في حد الضدين معنيان لا يشتركان في الصفات النفسية يرشدك إلى ذلك إيراده بعد حد المثلين. ( ومنعه المعتزلة)، واتفقوا على جواز اجتماعهما مطلقاً ) إلا شرذمة منهم، فإنهم قالوا : لا تجتمع حركتان ( متماثلتان في محل (لنا) في إِثبات امتناع الاجتماع ( مسالك ) أربعة : (الأول: يجب على تقدير اجتماعهما في محل (عدم تمايزهما بالذات وبالعوارض) أيضاً، لأن الذات أعني الماهية مشتركة بينهما، وكذا لوازمها من الصفات النفسية مشتركة أيضاً، فلا امتياز إلا بالعوارض المشخصة. ولما كان المحل واحداً كان العوارض أيضاً مشتركة فلا امتياز بينهما حينئذ أصلاً، فلا اثنينية، فلا تماثل لأنه فرع الاثنينية

قوله : ( لذاتيهما) يعني ليس منشأ امتناع الاجتماع ذاتيهما، بل للمحل مدخل في ذلك، فإن وحدته رافع للاثنينية بينهما، حتى لو فرض عدم استلزامها لرفع الاثنينية لم يستحل اجتماعهما. ولذا جوز بعضهم اجتماعهما بناء على عدم ذلك الاستلزام، فاندفع بما حررنا ماقيل: إن تجويز البعض اجتماعهما إنما يفيد أن امتناع اجتماعهما ليس ببديهي وأنه يحتاج إلى الواسطة في الإثبات وهو لا يستلزم الواسطة في الثبوت . قوله : ( وأيضاً المراد إلخ) أي لا نسلم الدخول المذكور، لم لا يجوز أن يراد بالمعنيين ما يشترك كان في الصفة النفسية؟ كما يرشد إلى ذلك إيراد الحد المذكور بعد حدهما هاهنا . فقوله : يرشدك إلخ تأييد للسند، فالمناقشة بأن مثل هذا لم يعهد قرينة للتقييد في الحد في استعمالاتهما، وأنه إنما يتم لو كان حد الضدين مذكوراً بعد حد المثلين في كلام الشيخ لا الأشعري أيضاً ليس بشيء . قوله : ( على جواز اجتماعهما مطلقاً ) أي يدعون الموجبة الكلية، ويقولون : كل متماثلين يجوز اجتماعهما إلا قليل منهم فإنهم يستثنون منها الحركتين المتماثلتين بناء على أن من مذهب الشيخ أن لا اشتراك بين شيئين من الموجودين إلا في الأسماء والأحكام، فما نقل عنه أن كل متماثلين لا يجتمعان لا بد أن يكون على التنزل وفرض وجود المماثلة، ومثله كثير في كلامهم. ثم المفهوم من أبكار الأفكار أن المتماثلين عند الشيخ قسم من الضدين حيث قال : مذهب الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري ومتبعيه أن كل عرضين متماثلين، كسوادين وبياضين، ونحو ذلك فهما ضدان يمتنع اجتماعهما في محل واحد . اللهم إلا أن يحمل على التشبيه أي كضدين، ولا تخلو عبارته عن الإيماء إلى ذلك . قوله : (إذ ليس امتناع الاجتماع (لذاتيهما ولإخراج المتماثلين بقوله : لذاتيهما وجه آخر، وهو أن المتماثلين متحدان ذاتاً، وكلمة لذاتيهما تقتضي تعدد ذات، فإن قلت : هذا إنما يتم إذا أريد بالذات الماهية لا الهوية ولا دليل عليه قلت دليله أنه لو حمل على الهوية لصدق تعريف المتضادين على بعض المتخالفين، كالسواد الحال في هذا المحل والحلاوة الحالة في ذلك المحل . قوله : ( فلا اثنينية فلا تماثل بخلاف ما إذا تعاقبا على محل واحد، فإن عوارض المحل مختلف في الوقتين .
The Fourth Observatory - The Tenth Goal: All two similar things do not come together. I said: There is also no inclusion, since the impossibility of coming together is not due to their essences (dhātay-himā). Do you not see that a group permitted their coming together? And also the intended meaning by the two meanings in the definition of opposites (al-ḍiddayn) is two meanings that do not share in the essential attributes (al-ṣifāt al-nafsiyya); this is indicated by his mentioning it after the definition of similars (al-mithlayn). (And the Mu'tazila denied it), and they agreed on the permissibility of their coming together absolutely, except a small group among them, for they said: Two similar motions do not come together in a locus. (For us) in proving the impossibility of coming together (there are) four (methods): (The first: It is necessary, on the assumption of their coming together in a locus, (the lack of their distinction by essence and by accidents) also, because the essence, i.e., the quiddity (al-māhiyya), is common between them, and likewise their necessary concomitants (lawāzimuhā) from the essential attributes are also common, so there is no distinction except by individuating accidents (al-ʿawāriḍ al-mushakhkhiṣa). And since the locus is one, the accidents are also common, so there is no distinction between them then at all, so no twoness, so no similarity (tamāthul) because it is a branch of twoness. His saying: (due to their two essences) means that the source of the impossibility of coming together is not due to their two essences, but rather the locus has an entry in that, for its unity is the remover of twoness between them, such that if its non-entailment of removing twoness were supposed, their coming together would not be impossible. Hence some permitted their coming together based on the absence of that entailment, and it is repelled by what we have written: that the permission of some for their coming together only indicates that the impossibility of their coming together is not self-evident (badīhī) and that it needs a mediator in proof, and this does not necessitate a mediator in existence (thubūt). His saying: (And also the intended meaning, etc.) i.e., we do not concede the mentioned inclusion; why is it not permissible that by the two meanings is meant what shares in the essential attribute? As is indicated by his mentioning the mentioned definition after defining them here. So his saying: 'it indicates you' etc. is a support for the proof. So the objection that such a thing is not known as a contextual clue for restriction in the definition in its usages, and that it is only complete if the definition of opposites was mentioned after the definition of similars in the speech of the Shaykh, and not al-Ashʿarī also, is nothing. His saying: (on the permissibility of their coming together absolutely) i.e., they claim the universal affirmative, and they say: Every two similars may come together, except a few of them who except from them the two similar motions, based on the fact that from the doctrine of the Shaykh is that there is no sharing between two things among existents except in names and judgments. So what is transmitted from him that all two similars do not come together must be upon concession and assumption of the existence of similarity. Many such examples occur in their speech. Moreover, the understood from Abkār al-Afkār is that similars according to the Shaykh are a species of opposites, where he said: The doctrine of Shaykh Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī and his followers is that every two similar accidents, like two blacknesses and two whitenesses and the like, are two opposites that cannot come together in one locus. Unless it is interpreted as a simile, i.e., like opposites, and his expression is not devoid of allusion to that. His saying: (since the impossibility of coming together is not due to their two essences) and for removing the two similars by his saying: 'due to their two essences' there is another aspect, which is that the two similars are one in essence, and the phrase 'due to their two essences' necessitates multiplicity of essence. For if you say: This is only complete if by essence is meant quiddity, not identity (al-huwiyya), and there is no proof for it; I say: Its proof is that if it were interpreted as identity, then the definition of two opposites would be true of some two different things, like blackness existing in this locus and sweetness existing in that locus. His saying: (so no twoness, so no similarity) unlike if they alternate in one locus, for the accidents of the locus are different in the two times.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage begins by introducing the Fourth Observatory and Tenth Goal: the statement 'all two similar things do not come together'. The author then presents an initial objection and response about the definition of two opposites and two similars. He clarifies that the impossibility of their coexistence is not due to their essences themselves but has a role in the locus. Then he mentions that the Mu'tazila generally permit their coexistence except for a small group. The author then states that for proving the impossibility of coexistence, there are four methods, and he begins with the first. The first proof argues that if two similars were to coexist in one locus, they would be indistinguishable in essence and accidents because they share the same quiddity and essential attributes, and the locus being one makes the accidents also common. Therefore, no distinction remains, which negates twoness, and since similarity presupposes twoness, similarity would be negated. The following commentary (qāla) explains each part of the proof in detail. It clarifies that the locus's unity removes twoness; if that were not the case, coexistence would not be impossible. It also addresses an objection about the definition of opposites and similars, showing how the definition is applied. The commentary also discusses the Mu'tazila's position and the Ash'ari view on similars and opposites, noting that for al-Ash'ari, two similar accidents are considered opposites that cannot coexist. The passage then discusses the phrase 'due to their two essences' and whether it refers to quiddity or identity, concluding that if it were identity, the definition of opposites would mistakenly apply to different things. Finally, the commentary explains that if similars alternate in a locus, the accidents differ over time, preserving distinction.

Key Terms
متماثلين (mithlayn)
ذات (dhāt)
ماهية (māhiyya)
صفات نفسية (ṣifāt nafsiyya)
عوارض مشخصة (ʿawāriḍ mushakhkhiṣa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the impossibility of coexistence is due to the essences themselves.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the impossibility is not due to essences but due to the unity of the locus leading to indistinguishability.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, what is the reason that two similars cannot coexist in one locus?
A) Their essences are incompatible.
B) They would become indistinguishable, negating twoness.
C) The locus cannot accommodate two similar accidents.
D) The Mu'tazila denied it.

Key Takeaway
The first proof shows that two similars cannot coexist because they would become indistinguishable, negating the twoness required for similarity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your heart, have you allowed 'two similars' to compete for the same locus of devotion—such as love for Allah and love for a created thing in the same degree? This proof shows such coexistence is impossible; they become indistinguishable. Which 'similars' must you separate to restore true tawheed in your heart?
?
The proof demonstrates that when two things are truly alike and placed in the same locus, they collapse into one. Have you experienced moments where you tried to serve two equally important goals in life, only to find that one absorbed the other? How can the principle of 'no two similars in one locus' guide you to prioritize with clarity?
?
If you cannot distinguish between two things, you cannot have two of them. Is there an area of your Islamic knowledge where you have blurred distinctions (e.g., between iman and Islam, or between sunnah and bid'ah) leading to confusion in practice? What specific step will you take today to clarify that distinction based on this lesson's reasoning?

Commentary on the First Proof: Clarifications and Objections
Al-Jurjani provides a detailed commentary on the first proof, clarifying the locus argument, definitions, and various scholarly positions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key clarifications and objections addressed in the commentary on the first proof, including the role of the locus and the definition of similars and opposites.

Core Idea
The locus is essential: its unity removes twoness, so the impossibility is not from the essences alone.

This lesson explores the detailed commentary on the first proof. It explains why the impossibility is not due to the essences but to the locus, addresses the Mu'tazila's exceptions, and clarifies the definition of similars and opposites. You will also see how the proof relates to the Ash'ari view on similar accidents as opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentary passage
Start with the phrase 'قوله : ( لذاتيهما)' and read through the commentary. Notice how each part of the proof is explained.
2
Understand the locus argument
The commentary emphasizes that the locus unity removes distinction. Why is this important?
3
Explore the definitions
How does the commentary relate the definition of opposites to similars? What is the role of essential attributes?
4
Consider the Mu'tazila and Ash'ari views
The commentary discusses the Mu'tazila's absolute permission except for similar motions, and the Ash'ari position that similar accidents are opposites. Compare these views.
5
Reflect on the terminology
The commentary discusses whether 'essence' means quiddity or identity, and why that matters.

The Fourth Observatory - The Tenth Goal: All two similar things do not come together. I said: There is also no inclusion, since the impossibility of coming together is not due to their essences (dhātay-himā). Do you not see that a group permitted their coming together? And also the intended meaning by the two meanings in the definition of opposites (al-ḍiddayn) is two meanings that do not share in the essential attributes (al-ṣifāt al-nafsiyya); this is indicated by his mentioning it after the definition of similars (al-mithlayn). (And the Mu'tazila denied it), and they agreed on the permissibility of their coming together absolutely, except a small group among them, for they said: Two similar motions do not come together in a locus. (For us) in proving the impossibility of coming together (there are) four (methods): (The first: It is necessary, on the assumption of their coming together in a locus, (the lack of their distinction by essence and by accidents) also, because the essence, i.e., the quiddity (al-māhiyya), is common between them, and likewise their necessary concomitants (lawāzimuhā) from the essential attributes are also common, so there is no distinction except by individuating accidents (al-ʿawāriḍ al-mushakhkhiṣa). And since the locus is one, the accidents are also common, so there is no distinction between them then at all, so no twoness, so no similarity (tamāthul) because it is a branch of twoness. His saying: (due to their two essences) means that the source of the impossibility of coming together is not due to their two essences, but rather the locus has an entry in that, for its unity is the remover of twoness between them, such that if its non-entailment of removing twoness were supposed, their coming together would not be impossible. Hence some permitted their coming together based on the absence of that entailment, and it is repelled by what we have written: that the permission of some for their coming together only indicates that the impossibility of their coming together is not self-evident (badīhī) and that it needs a mediator in proof, and this does not necessitate a mediator in existence (thubūt). His saying: (And also the intended meaning, etc.) i.e., we do not concede the mentioned inclusion; why is it not permissible that by the two meanings is meant what shares in the essential attribute? As is indicated by his mentioning the mentioned definition after defining them here. So his saying: 'it indicates you' etc. is a support for the proof. So the objection that such a thing is not known as a contextual clue for restriction in the definition in its usages, and that it is only complete if the definition of opposites was mentioned after the definition of similars in the speech of the Shaykh, and not al-Ashʿarī also, is nothing. His saying: (on the permissibility of their coming together absolutely) i.e., they claim the universal affirmative, and they say: Every two similars may come together, except a few of them who except from them the two similar motions, based on the fact that from the doctrine of the Shaykh is that there is no sharing between two things among existents except in names and judgments. So what is transmitted from him that all two similars do not come together must be upon concession and assumption of the existence of similarity. Many such examples occur in their speech. Moreover, the understood from Abkār al-Afkār is that similars according to the Shaykh are a species of opposites, where he said: The doctrine of Shaykh Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī and his followers is that every two similar accidents, like two blacknesses and two whitenesses and the like, are two opposites that cannot come together in one locus. Unless it is interpreted as a simile, i.e., like opposites, and his expression is not devoid of allusion to that. His saying: (since the impossibility of coming together is not due to their two essences) and for removing the two similars by his saying: 'due to their two essences' there is another aspect, which is that the two similars are one in essence, and the phrase 'due to their two essences' necessitates multiplicity of essence. For if you say: This is only complete if by essence is meant quiddity, not identity (al-huwiyya), and there is no proof for it; I say: Its proof is that if it were interpreted as identity, then the definition of two opposites would be true of some two different things, like blackness existing in this locus and sweetness existing in that locus. His saying: (so no twoness, so no similarity) unlike if they alternate in one locus, for the accidents of the locus are different in the two times.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage begins by introducing the Fourth Observatory and Tenth Goal: the statement 'all two similar things do not come together'. The author then presents an initial objection and response about the definition of two opposites and two similars. He clarifies that the impossibility of their coexistence is not due to their essences themselves but has a role in the locus. Then he mentions that the Mu'tazila generally permit their coexistence except for a small group. The author then states that for proving the impossibility of coexistence, there are four methods, and he begins with the first. The first proof argues that if two similars were to coexist in one locus, they would be indistinguishable in essence and accidents because they share the same quiddity and essential attributes, and the locus being one makes the accidents also common. Therefore, no distinction remains, which negates twoness, and since similarity presupposes twoness, similarity would be negated. The following commentary (qāla) explains each part of the proof in detail. It clarifies that the locus's unity removes twoness; if that were not the case, coexistence would not be impossible. It also addresses an objection about the definition of opposites and similars, showing how the definition is applied. The commentary also discusses the Mu'tazila's position and the Ash'ari view on similars and opposites, noting that for al-Ash'ari, two similar accidents are considered opposites that cannot coexist. The passage then discusses the phrase 'due to their two essences' and whether it refers to quiddity or identity, concluding that if it were identity, the definition of opposites would mistakenly apply to different things. Finally, the commentary explains that if similars alternate in a locus, the accidents differ over time, preserving distinction.

Key Terms
ذات (dhāt)
ماهية (māhiyya)
هوية (huwiyya)
صفات نفسية (ṣifāt nafsiyya)
عوارض مشخصة (ʿawāriḍ mushakhkhiṣa)
متماثلين (mithlayn)
ضدين (ḍiddayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the impossibility of coexistence is self-evident (badīhī).
Correction: The commentary notes that some permitted coexistence, so it is not self-evident; a proof is needed.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why does the phrase 'due to their two essences' imply multiplicity of essence when applied to two similars?
A) Because similars are one in essence, so the phrase is contradictory.
B) Because the essences are different.
C) Because the locus is one.
D) Because accidents are common.

Key Takeaway
The commentary clarifies that the impossibility of coexistence depends on the locus, and it addresses definitions and scholarly positions to defend the proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The commentary warns against hastily concluding that the impossibility of coexistence is self-evident. In your own understanding of divine attributes, have you ever assumed a conclusion without tracing the intermediate reasoning? What does this reveal about the care you take in matters of faith?
?
The discussion of 'essence' as quiddity versus identity shows how a single term can lead to error if misdefined. Are there any terms in your theological vocabulary that you have not precisely defined, potentially leading to confusion? How will you seek clarity?
?
Some scholars allowed the coexistence of opposites because they denied the locus's role. Consider an area where you have allowed a compromise in your beliefs due to lack of thorough investigation. What step can you take today to rectify that?

The Impossibility of Two Theoretical Knowledges Coexisting
Main reductio argument from the second proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is rationally impossible for two theoretical knowledges of the same thing to coexist in one mind.

Core Idea
Two theoretical knowledges of one thing cannot coexist because reflection cannot be duplicated.

Al-Jurjani begins the second proof by reductio. He argues that if similars could coexist, two theoretical knowledges of the same object could coexist, but this leads to an absurdity: the soul cannot direct two reflections on one object, and the second reflection would be acquiring what is already acquired.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation to understand the argument.
The passage includes commentary markers like 'قوله' which are part of the source.
2
Identify the reductio structure
Notice how the author assumes the possibility of two similars coexisting and derives an impossibility.
The reductio hinges on the concept of 'taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil' (acquiring the already acquired).
3
Understand why taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil is impossible
The second reflection would be on the known as already known, which is acquiring what is already present, an absurdity.
This shows that the original assumption (coexistence of similars) is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثاني : الإلزام في العلمين النظريين) أي لو جاز اجتماع المثلين لجاز أن يجتمع علمان نظريان بشيء واحد لأنهما مثلان فإذا قام بشخص علم نظري بشيء، جاز أن يقوم به أيضاً علم نظري آخر بذلك الشيء، وهو محال؛ ( إذ يلزم النظر في المعلوم

قوله : ( بشيء واحد ) أي بالذات والاعتبار، فلا يتجه أنه قد يتصور الشيء بوجهين بالنظر، فقد اجتمع العلمان النظريان بشيء واحد . قوله : (إذ يلزم النظر في المعلوم لأن أحد النظرين يكون مقدماً على الآخر، لامتناع توجه النفس قصداً إلى شيئين والفرض أن المعلوم شيء واحد بالذات والاعتبار، فيلزم أن يكون النظر الثاني في المعلوم من حيث إنه معلوم، فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل المحل، فإنه يمتنع اجتماعهما بهويتهما ؛ إذ لا يجوز الانتقال على شيء منهما حتى يتصور اجتماعهما في محل . قوله : فلا اثنينية فلا (تماثل لا يقال لو تم ما ذكره لدل على امتناع عروضهما لمحل واحد بدلا أيضاً، لأنا نقول : إذا لم يجتمعا جاز أن يكون للمحل في أحد الزمانين عوارض مخصوصة، وفي الزمان الآخر عوارض أخرى، فلا تكون نسبة المثلين إلى جميع العوارض نسبة واحدة، فجاز امتيازهما بحسب العوارض بخلاف ما لو اجتمعا؛ إذ هاهنا يدعي اتحاد نسبتهما إليها . فإن قلت محل كل من النقطتين اللتين هما طرفا خط واحد مجموع ذلك الخط، كما تقرر عندهم، ولا شك أنهما مثلان فقد اجتمع مثلان في محل مع وجود الامتياز بينهما . قلت : أولاً : ما ذكرته مبني على قواعد الفلاسفة، وثانياً : إن محل إحدى النقطتين مجموع الخط باعتبار انتهائه في جانب ومحل النقطة الأخرى ذلك المجموع لكن باعتبار انتهائه في جانب آخر فقد تعدد محلهما بحيثية موجبة لامتياز الحالتين ولا كلام فيه. قوله : ( إذ يلزم النظر في المعلوم هذا على امتناع حصول المثلين معاً من نظر واحد مبني فإ تأمل.
(The second: The entailment regarding two theoretical knowledges) i.e., if the coming together of two similars were possible, then it would be possible for two theoretical knowledges of one thing to come together, because they are similars. So if a person has a theoretical knowledge of something, it would be possible for him to also have another theoretical knowledge of that same thing, which is impossible; (because it necessitates reflection (naẓar) on the known (al-maʿlūm)). His saying: (of one thing) i.e., in essence and consideration, so it is not valid that a thing can be imagined in two aspects by reflection, for then two theoretical knowledges would have come together on one thing. His saying: (because it necessitates reflection on the known) because one of the two reflections would be prior to the other, due to the impossibility of the soul directing itself intentionally to two things, and the assumption is that the known is one thing in essence and consideration. So it follows that the second reflection is on the known insofar as it is known, which entails acquiring the already acquired (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil) in the locus, for it is impossible for them to come together in their identity; since transition upon either of them is not permissible until their coming together in a locus is conceived. His saying: (so no twoness, so no similarity). It is not said: if what he mentioned were complete, it would indicate the impossibility of their occurring to one locus in alternation as well; because we say: If they do not come together, it is possible that the locus has in one time particular accidents and in another time other accidents, so the relation of the two similars to all accidents is not one, so their distinction according to accidents is possible, unlike if they were to come together; for here their relation to them is claimed to be one. For if you say: The locus of each of the two points which are the two ends of a single line is that entire line, as is established with them, and there is no doubt that they are two similars, so two similars have come together in a locus while there is distinction between them. I say: First, what you mentioned is based on the rules of the philosophers; and second, the locus of one of the two points is the entire line in terms of its termination on one side, and the locus of the other point is that same entire line but in terms of its termination on another side; so their locus is multiple by a mode that necessitates distinction of the two states, and there is no dispute about that. His saying: (because it necessitates reflection on the known) this is based on the impossibility of two similars being acquired from a single reflection; so reflect.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof. The author argues that if two similars could coexist, then two theoretical knowledges (ʿilmāni naẓariyyān) about one and the same thing could coexist in one person, since they are similars. But this is impossible because it would entail that the person reflects on the same known thing twice, which is absurd: reflection requires intent, and the soul cannot simultaneously intend two acts of reflection on a single object. Moreover, the second reflection would be on the known as already known, which is acquiring the already acquired (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil), an impossibility. The commentary explains that 'one thing' means in essence and consideration, so that even if the thing is considered under two aspects, the two knowledges would still be of the same thing and thus cannot coexist. The commentary then addresses an objection: if what the proof says were true, it would also prevent the same similars from alternating in one locus. The answer is that in alternation, the accidents of the locus differ over time, so the relation of the similars to those accidents is not identical, allowing distinction. A further objection is raised about two points at the ends of a line being similars that coexist in the same locus (the line). The author responds that this argument is based on philosophic assumptions, and also that the loci of the two points are not identical: each point's locus is the entire line but considered from a different side (termination point), thus the loci are multiple by a mode that distinguishes the two states. The passage ends with a note that the necessity of reflection on the known is based on the impossibility of two similars being acquired from a single reflection.

Key Terms
علم نظري (ʿilm naẓarī)
معلوم (maʿlūm)
نظر (naẓar)
تحصيل الحاصل (taḥṣīl al-ḥāṣil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two theoretical knowledges of the same thing could coexist if considered under different aspects, because the object is known in two ways.
Correction: The proof shows that the act of reflection is unitary and cannot be duplicated for the same object in the same consideration, and the second reflection would be redundant.

Checkpoint
What is the key reason the author gives for the impossibility of two theoretical knowledges of one thing coexisting?
A) The two knowledges would be identical, contradicting multiplicity.
B) The soul cannot direct two intentional reflections on one known object.
C) The object would change between the two acts of knowledge.
D) The second knowledge would be of a different object.

Key Takeaway
Two theoretical knowledges of the same thing cannot coexist because it would require two simultaneous reflections on one object, which is rationally impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself trying to 'know' something you already know, as if seeking a duplicate? What would it mean to truly acquire knowledge once and stop redundantly 'acquiring the already acquired'?
?
The passage argues that the soul cannot simultaneously direct itself to two reflections on the same thing. Where in your life are you attempting to split your attention between two incompatible views of the same reality? How might you unify your reflection?

Defending the Proof: Objections and Replies
Objections and replies to the second proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani defends his proof against two objections: the possibility of alternation and the example of two points on a line.

Core Idea
The proof is defended by showing that alternation allows distinction, and the points example relies on philosophical assumptions.

After presenting the main argument, the commentary anticipates two objections. The first argues that if the proof were valid, it would also prevent similars from appearing in the same locus at different times. The second raises the example of two endpoints of a line, which are similars yet coexist in the same line. Al-Jurjani answers both, reinforcing the original conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections and replies
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice the two objections: alternation and the points on a line.
The objections are introduced with 'لا يقال' and 'فإن قلت'.
2
Analyze the response to the alternation objection
The author distinguishes between simultaneous coexistence (which is impossible) and temporal alternation (which is possible because accidents differ).
This shows the proof does not rule out alternation.
3
Examine the points on a line objection
The objection claims two points at the ends of a line are similars coexisting in one locus (the line). The reply states that this is based on philosophical assumptions and that the loci differ by the mode of termination.
Thus, they are not truly in the same locus in the same respect.

(The second: The entailment regarding two theoretical knowledges) i.e., if the coming together of two similars were possible, then it would be possible for two theoretical knowledges of one thing to come together, because they are similars. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author notes that the points example is 'based on the rules of the philosophers' — assumptions not shared in kalam. What are some assumptions from your culture or education that you may have uncritically adopted about Allah or His attributes? Examine yourself: where have you let outside ideas shape your faith? This week, seek to identify one such assumption and compare it with the Quran and Sunnah.
?
The proof distinguishes between simultaneous existence and alternation. Are there areas in your life where you confuse what is true at all times with what is true only in certain circumstances? For instance, do you sometimes treat God's love as conditional like human love? How can you realign your understanding with the timeless truths of revelation?
?
When faced with objections to your beliefs, do you quickly dismiss them as based on false premises, or do you carefully analyze them as the author did? The author engaged with the objection and found a nuanced reply. Think of a recent doubt or criticism you encountered. Did you give it a fair hearing? Commit to studying it with the same depth, using the sources of knowledge.

The Third Proof: Coexistence of Similars Is Contingent and Contradictory
Al-Jurjani presents the third proof against coexistence of similars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Jurjani's third proof that the coexistence of two similars in one locus is impossible because their coexistence would be contingent and lead to a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
If similars could coexist, one could vanish while the other remains—a contradiction.

In this passage, al-Jurjani presents a third line of argument against the possibility of two similars coexisting in one locus. He shows that even if we grant such coexistence, it would not be necessary; one similar could cease while the other remains. This leads to an absurd consequence that undermines the very notion of similarity. Let's work through the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing. Notice the parentheses indicating the main text and the commentary.
The passage is dense. Focus on the logical flow first.
2
Understand the assumption and its consequence
The proof assumes for argument's sake that two similars (e.g., two blacknesses) can coexist. It then argues that if they do, their coexistence is not necessary; one could cease while the other remains.
This is the key move: 'it is not necessary such that its removal after its occurrence is impossible.'
3
Trace the resulting contradiction
If one similar is removed, the locus can acquire the opposite of that removed similar. This would mean the locus simultaneously has one similar and the opposite of the other—an impossibility.
The passage mentions motion and endpoints to illustrate the removal of opposites.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof concludes that the original assumption (coexistence of two similars) leads to a contradiction, so it must be false.
This reinforces the Ash'ari position that two similars are like opposites and cannot coexist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه أي الاجتماع على تقدير جوازه ( لا يجب ) بحيث يمتنع زواله بعد حصوله فإذا اجتمع سوادان مثلا في محل واحد، جاز أن ينتفي عنه أحدهما بقاء الآخر، وإذا انتفى عن المحل أحد المثلين ( فيجوز مع اتصافه ) أي اتصاف ذلك المحل ) بضد (المثل ( المنتفي ؛ لأن زوال أحد الضدين عن تماثلهما باتحاد المتحرك وما فيه الحركة والمبدأ والمنتهى وإذا كان كذلك ترتفع الاثنينية عنهما .
The third: That is, that coming together, on the assumption of its permissibility, (is not necessary) such that its removal after its occurrence is impossible. So if two blacknesses, for example, come together in one locus, it is possible that one of them is removed while the other remains. And if one of the two similars is removed from the locus, (then it is permissible along with its characterization), i.e., the characterization of that locus, with the opposite (of the removed similar); because the removal of one of the two opposites due to their similarity is by the union of the moving object and what contains the motion and the starting point and endpoint. And when that is so, twoness is removed from them.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof. The argument is that if two similars could coexist, their coexistence would not be necessary, meaning that it would be possible for one to cease while the other remains. For example, if two blacknesses coexist in one substance, it would be permissible for one to vanish while the other persists. Then, after one of the similars is removed, the substance can acquire the opposite of that removed similar. This leads to an absurdity because the substance would simultaneously have one blackness and a non-blackness (or its opposite) while still sharing in the similarity with the removed one? The text is somewhat compressed. The key idea is that if similars can coexist, then they are not necessarily linked, so the removal of one while the other remains is possible. But then the remaining similar would be in the same locus with the opposite of the removed one, which contradicts the notion that similars are opposites. The proof relies on the Ash'ari position that two similars are like opposites. Therefore, the coexistence of two similars would entail that they are not true opposites, and thus the impossibility is shown. The commentary adds that the removal of one opposite due to their similarity is by the union of the moving object, etc., which may refer to the conditions for motion and change.

Key Terms
لا يجب
زوال
بقاء
ضد
تماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if two similars can coexist, they must be able to persist together necessarily.
Correction: The proof shows their coexistence is contingent, not necessary, and this leads to a contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what would be the consequence if two similars could coexist in one locus?
A) They would both necessarily remain forever.
B) One could cease while the other remains, allowing the locus to be characterized by the opposite of the removed one.
C) They would merge into a single entity.
D) The locus would be destroyed.

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that assuming the coexistence of two similars leads to a logical contradiction because one can cease while the other remains, allowing the locus to acquire the opposite of the removed similar.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof uses the example of two blacknesses in one locus. Consider your own heart: are there two 'blacknesses'—sins or bad habits—you allow to coexist, assuming you can keep both? What one thing must you remove to avoid logical and spiritual contradiction?
?
The argument shows that if two similars could coexist, one could vanish while the other remains. Are there commitments in your life that you treat as equally valid even though they conflict? Which one is calling you to let it go for the sake of integrity?
?
The proof ends by saying 'twoness is removed.' In your relationship with Allah, is there any 'twoness'—loving Him yet clinging to what He forbids? How will you remove that today?

The Second Argument: Conditional and Clarification
Al-Jurjani states the second argument and clarifies key terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the second argument against coexistence of similars, specifically the conditional premise and the clarification of 'one thing'.

Core Idea
If two theoretical knowledges could coexist, they would have to be of the same object in a single respect.

Al-Jurjani now presents the second argument against the coexistence of similars, focusing on the case of two theoretical knowledges of the same thing. He begins with a conditional statement: if similars could coexist, then two theoretical knowledges could subsist in one person concerning one thing. This lesson unpacks that conditional and the crucial refinement that 'one thing' means in essence and consideration, not two aspects.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument statement
Start by reading the Arabic sentence that begins with 'والثاني: الإلزام في العلمين النظريين' and ends with 'وهو محال'. This is the core claim: if similars could coexist, two theoretical knowledges of one thing would be possible, which is impossible.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...' which is the form of many kalam arguments.
2
Understand the gloss on 'one thing'
Read the gloss beginning with 'قوله: (بشيء واحد)'. Al-Jurjani clarifies that 'one thing' means in essence and consideration, not two different aspects. This prevents a misunderstanding that the two knowledges might be of different aspects of the same object.
The distinction between 'one thing' absolutely and 'one thing' under two aspects is subtle but essential.
3
Reflect on why the argument is problematic for the opponent
Ask yourself: if the opponent admits that two theoretical knowledges of the same thing in the same respect cannot coexist, then he must also deny the coexistence of similars in general, since theoretical knowledges are a clear case of similars.
This argument is a reductio ad absurdum against the opponent's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: الإلزام في العلمين النظريين أي لو جاز اجتماع المثلين لجاز أن يجتمع علمان نظريان بشيء واحد لأنهما مثلان فإذا قام بشخص علم نظري بشيء جاز أن يقوم به أيضاً علم نظري آخر بذلك الشيء وهو محال. قوله: (بشيء واحد) أي بالذات والاعتبار فلا يتجه أنه قد يتصور الشيء بوجهين بالنظر فقد اجتمع العلمان النظريان بشيء واحد. قوله: (إذ يلزم النظر في المعلوم) لأن أحد النظرين يكون مقدماً على الآخر لامتناع توجه النفس قصداً إلى شيئين والفرض أن المعلوم شيء واحد بالذات والاعتبار فيلزم أن يكون النظر الثاني في المعلوم من حيث إنه معلوم فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل. قوله: (فلا اثنينية فلا تماثل) بخلاف ما إذا تعاقبا على محل واحد فإن عوارض المحل مختلف في الوقتين. لا يقال لو تم ما ذكره لدل على امتناع عروضهما لمحل واحد بدلاً أيضاً لأنا نقول إذا لم يجتمعا جاز أن يكون للمحل في أحد الزمانين عوارض مخصوصة وفي الزمان الآخر عوارض أخرى فلا تكون نسبة المثلين إلى جميع العوارض نسبة واحدة فجاز امتيازهما بحسب العوارض بخلاف ما لو اجتمعا إذ هاهنا يدعي اتحاد نسبتهما إليها.
And the second: the entailment regarding the two theoretical knowledges, meaning: if the coexistence of similars was permissible, then it would be permissible for two theoretical knowledges to coexist concerning one thing, because they are similars. So if a theoretical knowledge of something subsists in a person, it would be permissible for another theoretical knowledge of that thing to also subsist in him, which is impossible. His saying: (concerning one thing) meaning in essence and consideration, so it is not directed that the thing might be conceptualized in two aspects by reasoning, for then the two theoretical knowledges would have coexisted concerning one thing. His saying: (for reasoning about the known entails) because one of the two reasonings would be prior to the other, due to the impossibility of the soul directing itself intentionally to two things, while the assumption is that the known is one thing in essence and consideration. So it necessarily follows that the second reasoning is about the known inasmuch as it is known, and thus obtaining the obtained is necessitated. His saying: (so no duality, so no similarity) contrary to when they succeed each other in one locus, for the accidents of the locus differ in the two times. It cannot be said: if what he mentioned were valid, it would indicate the impossibility of their occurring in one locus as a substitute as well; for we say: if they do not coexist, it is permissible for the locus to have specific accidents in one time and other accidents in another time, so the relation of the two similars to all accidents is not one and the same, so their differentiation according to accidents is permissible, unlike if they coexisted, for here it is claimed that their relation to them is unified.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the second argument) against the coexistence of two similar things, specifically using the case of two theoretical knowledges. Al-Jurjani begins by stating the argument: if two similars could coexist, then two theoretical knowledges of the same thing could coexist in one person, which is absurd. He then explains the glosses: 'one thing' means in essence and consideration, not two aspects. The entailment of 'reasoning about the known' is that the second reasoning would be about something already known, leading to 'obtaining the obtained' (تحصيل الحاصل). He then clarifies that the impossibility does not apply when the two knowledges occur in succession, because the locus has different accidents at different times. Finally, he addresses an objection: that the argument would also prove that two similars cannot occur in one locus even in succession. The answer is that in succession, the locus has different accidents at different times, allowing differentiation; but when they coexist, the relation to all accidents is one, removing any basis for distinction.

Key Terms
المثلان
العلمان النظريان
الاعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does 'one thing' (بشيء واحد) mean in the argument?
A) Any object, regardless of aspects
B) One object in essence and consideration
C) One object but possibly with two different aspects
D) Two distinct objects

Key Takeaway
The second argument uses a conditional to show that allowing coexistence of similars leads to the impossible conclusion of two theoretical knowledges of the same thing in the same respect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Jurjani argues that holding two theoretical knowledges of the same thing in the same respect is impossible. Is there an area in your faith where you entertain two conflicting understandings of a single truth (e.g., Allah's mercy and justice)? How might this argument push you to seek a consistent, coherent understanding rather than allowing comfortable contradictions?
?
The argument relies on the premise that two identical things (similars) cannot coexist in the same subject. Reflect: Do you ever treat similar situations in your life with double standards? Where might you be allowing 'coexistence of similars' in your moral judgments? What would it take to apply the same standard consistently?
?
This lesson shows that even a purely logical argument can have profound implications for belief. How often do you subject your own theological assumptions to rigorous logical scrutiny? Are you willing to abandon a cherished idea if it leads to absurdity? What would that cost you?

The Entailment: Obtaining the Obtained
Al-Jurjani explains the entailment leading to تحصيل الحاصل.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the second reasoning about an already known object leads to the absurdity of 'obtaining the obtained' (تحصيل الحاصل).

Core Idea
Two theoretical knowledges of the same thing cannot coexist because the second would be redundant, repeating what is already known.

The core of the second argument lies in demonstrating why two theoretical knowledges of one thing are impossible. Al-Jurjani explains that any second reasoning about the same object would be a repetition of what has already been attained, leading to the logical fallacy of 'obtaining the obtained' (تحصيل الحاصل). This lesson unpacks that entailment and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the entailment passage
Start with the line 'قوله: (إذ يلزم النظر في المعلوم)' and read until 'فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل'. This explains why a second reasoning is futile.
Focus on the phrase 'تحصيل الحاصل' – it is a key concept in Islamic logic and theology.
2
Analyze the reasoning
The soul cannot direct attention to two things intentionally at the same time. Since the known is one thing in essence and consideration, the second reasoning must be about that same known as already known, thus obtaining what is already obtained.
This is a temporal argument as well: one reasoning precedes the other.
3
Connect to the conclusion 'no duality, no similarity'
Read the line 'قوله: (فلا اثنينية فلا تماثل)'. Because the second reasoning is impossible, there is no duality of knowledges, and hence no similarity (since similarity requires two distinct things).
This directly refutes the possibility of two similars coexisting in a single locus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: الإلزام في العلمين النظريين أي لو جاز اجتماع المثلين لجاز أن يجتمع علمان نظريان بشيء واحد لأنهما مثلان فإذا قام بشخص علم نظري بشيء جاز أن يقوم به أيضاً علم نظري آخر بذلك الشيء وهو محال. قوله: (بشيء واحد) أي بالذات والاعتبار فلا يتجه أنه قد يتصور الشيء بوجهين بالنظر فقد اجتمع العلمان النظريان بشيء واحد. قوله: (إذ يلزم النظر في المعلوم) لأن أحد النظرين يكون مقدماً على الآخر لامتناع توجه النفس قصداً إلى شيئين والفرض أن المعلوم شيء واحد بالذات والاعتبار فيلزم أن يكون النظر الثاني في المعلوم من حيث إنه معلوم فيلزم تحصيل الحاصل. قوله: (فلا اثنينية فلا تماثل) بخلاف ما إذا تعاقبا على محل واحد فإن عوارض المحل مختلف في الوقتين. لا يقال لو تم ما ذكره لدل على امتناع عروضهما لمحل واحد بدلاً أيضاً لأنا نقول إذا لم يجتمعا جاز أن يكون للمحل في أحد الزمانين عوارض مخصوصة وفي الزمان الآخر عوارض أخرى فلا تكون نسبة المثلين إلى جميع العوارض نسبة واحدة فجاز امتيازهما بحسب العوارض بخلاف ما لو اجتمعا إذ هاهنا يدعي اتحاد نسبتهما إليها.
And the second: the entailment regarding the two theoretical knowledges, meaning: if the coexistence of similars was permissible, then it would be permissible for two theoretical knowledges to coexist concerning one thing, because they are similars. So if a theoretical knowledge of something subsists in a person, it would be permissible for another theoretical knowledge of that thing to also subsist in him, which is impossible. His saying: (concerning one thing) meaning in essence and consideration, so it is not directed that the thing might be conceptualized in two aspects by reasoning, for then the two theoretical knowledges would have coexisted concerning one thing. His saying: (for reasoning about the known entails) because one of the two reasonings would be prior to the other, due to the impossibility of the soul directing itself intentionally to two things, while the assumption is that the known is one thing in essence and consideration. So it necessarily follows that the second reasoning is about the known inasmuch as it is known, and thus obtaining the obtained is necessitated. His saying: (so no duality, so no similarity) contrary to when they succeed each other in one locus, for the accidents of the locus differ in the two times. It cannot be said: if what he mentioned were valid, it would indicate the impossibility of their occurring in one locus as a substitute as well; for we say: if they do not coexist, it is permissible for the locus to have specific accidents in one time and other accidents in another time, so the relation of the two similars to all accidents is not one and the same, so their differentiation according to accidents is permissible, unlike if they coexisted, for here it is claimed that their relation to them is unified.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a proof (the second argument) against the coexistence of two similar things, specifically using the case of two theoretical knowledges. Al-Jurjani begins by stating the argument: if two similars could coexist, then two theoretical knowledges of the same thing could coexist in one person, which is absurd. He then explains the glosses: 'one thing' means in essence and consideration, not two aspects. The entailment of 'reasoning about the known' is that the second reasoning would be about something already known, leading to 'obtaining the obtained' (تحصيل الحاصل). He then clarifies that the impossibility does not apply when the two knowledges occur in succession, because the locus has different accidents at different times. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two theoretical knowledges of the same thing could coexist if they occur simultaneously or if the soul can multitask.
Correction: Al-Jurjani argues that the soul cannot direct itself intentionally to two acts of reasoning at the same time, so one must be prior. Additionally, the object is the same, so the second would be redundant.

Checkpoint
Why is the second reasoning about the known as known considered impossible?
A) Because the soul cannot reason twice about the same thing
B) Because it would entail obtaining what is already obtained (تحصيل الحاصل)
C) Because reasoning must be simultaneous
D) Because the known changes

Key Takeaway
Two theoretical knowledges of one thing cannot coexist because the second would be redundant, resulting in the logical fallacy of 'obtaining the obtained'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a truth about Allah, do you immediately seek to 'obtain' it again through more study, or do you act on what you already know? What truth have you 'obtained' but not lived? Name it and commit to implementing it today.
?
The soul cannot direct itself to two intentions at once. What two competing intentions in your heart prevent you from fully directing your soul to the knowledge of Allah? What will you remove or prioritize to achieve single-focused intention?
?
Is there an area of your faith where you are seeking a 'second knowledge' of the same thing—perhaps through unnecessary debates or over-analysis—while neglecting the practical demands of the first? How will you break this cycle of redundancy and start applying what you know?

Objection and Distinction: Coexistence vs. Succession
Al-Jurjani distinguishes succession from coexistence and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument does not apply when two knowledges occur in succession, and how the distinction based on accidents answers the objection.

Core Idea
Successive knowledges avoid the fallacy because the locus has different accidents at different times, allowing differentiation.

Al-Jurjani anticipates an objection: if the argument shows that two theoretical knowledges cannot coexist, why can they not occur in succession? He clarifies that the impossibility applies only to coexistence, not succession, because the accidents of the locus differ over time. This lesson examines that distinction and the response to the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the distinction passage
Start from 'بخلاف ما إذا تعاقبا على محل واحد' and read to the end. This contrasts coexistence with succession.
Note the phrase 'عوارض المحل مختلف في الوقتين' – the accidents of the locus differ in the two times.
2
Understand the objection
Read 'لا يقال لو تم ما ذكره لدل على امتناع عروضهما لمحل واحد بدلاً أيضاً' – the objection states that the argument would also prevent two knowledges from occurring in the same locus even one after the other.
The opponent is trying to show that the argument proves too much.
3
Analyze the response
Al-Jurjani answers that in succession, the locus has different accidents at each time, so the relation of the similars to all accidents is not uniform. This allows differentiation, which is not possible in coexistence where the relation to accidents is unified.
The key is the unity of 'نسبة المثلين إلى جميع العوارض' in coexistence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Why does the argument not rule out two theoretical knowledges occurring in succession?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a single truth in Islam cannot be learned in stages or that your past and present understanding are identical? This lesson shows that differentiation through changing states is rationally sound. Will you now embrace the growth Allah grants you through successive knowledge?
?
When you look at your spiritual journey, do you treat your past sins and present obedience as if they are the same 'you'? Allah differentiates your states by their times and contexts. How will you use this distinction to turn to Him with renewed sincerity?
?
The objection in the passage reveals a common tendency to overgeneralize a logical argument. In your own life, have you ever rejected a truth because you misunderstood its scope? Will you commit to examining the precise conditions of divine commands before dismissing them?

Addressing the Objection of Two Endpoints
Al-Jurjani raises an objection about two points on a line and answers it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani resolves the apparent counterexample of two endpoints of a line to the rule that two similars cannot coexist.

Core Idea
Similar things can coexist if their loci differ in aspect.

The rule that two similars cannot coexist seems to have a clear counterexample: the two endpoints of a line are both points (similar) and yet they exist on the same line. In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses this objection by refining the notion of 'locus'—showing that the loci are not truly identical because they differ in the consideration of termination. This clarifies the rule and deepens our understanding of how distinction is possible without violating the principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening phrase 'فإن قلت...' which presents the objection. Notice the claim that two endpoints are similars and that they coexist in a single locus (the line).
The objector is using a physical example to challenge a logical rule.
2
Examine al-Jurjani's first answer
Al-Jurjani responds with 'قلت: أولاً...' pointing out that the objection is based on the principles of philosophers, implying it may not apply within the Kalam framework.
This answer defuses the objection by questioning its premises.
3
Examine the second answer
The second answer ('وثانياً...') is the main resolution: the locus of each point is the same line but with a different consideration—one as termination on one side, the other on the other side. Thus the loci are distinct in modality.
The key term is 'اعتبار' (consideration) which differentiates the otherwise identical loci.
4
Reflect on the final gloss
The passage ends with a note from the commentator about the assumption that two theoretical knowledges cannot arise from a single act of reasoning. This links back to the deeper epistemological principle underlying the rule.
This shows how the objection is embedded in a larger debate about the nature of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت محل كل من النقطتين اللتين هما طرفا خط واحد مجموع ذلك الخط، كما تقرر عندهم، ولا شك أنهما مثلان فقد اجتمع مثلان في محل مع وجود الامتياز بينهما. قلت: أولاً: ما ذكرته مبني على قواعد الفلاسفة، وثانياً: إن محل إحدى النقطتين مجموع الخط باعتبار انتهائه في جانب ومحل النقطة الأخرى ذلك المجموع لكن باعتبار انتهائه في جانب آخر فقد تعدد محلهما بحيثية موجبة لامتياز الحالتين ولا كلام فيه. قوله: (إذ يلزم النظر في المعلوم هذا على امتناع حصول المثلين معاً من نظر واحد مبني فليتأمل.
For if you say: The locus of each of the two points that are the endpoints of a single line is the totality of that line, as established with them, and there is no doubt that they are two similars; so two similars have coexisted in a locus along with the existence of distinction between them. I say: First, what you have mentioned is based on the rules of the philosophers; and second, the locus of one of the two points is the totality of the line in respect of its termination on one side, and the locus of the other point is that same totality but in respect of its termination on another side, so their loci have multiplied due to a modality that necessitates the distinction of the two states, and there is no dispute about this. His saying: (for reasoning about the known entails) this is based on the impossibility of the occurrence of two similars together from a single reasoning, so reflect.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the general rule that two similars cannot coexist. The objector argues that the two endpoints of a line are two similars (both are points) and they coexist in the locus of the entire line, yet they are distinct. Al-Jurjani gives a two-part answer. First, this objection relies on the principles of the philosophers, not on sound Islamic premises (perhaps implying a different ontological framework). Second, and more importantly, the locus of each point is the same line but with different considerations: one point is the endpoint on one side, the other on the other side. Thus, the 'locus' is not truly identical; it is multiplied by the modality of termination. Therefore, there is no genuine coexistence of two similars in a single, undifferentiated locus. The final gloss notes that this entire discussion rests on the assumption that two theoretical knowledges cannot arise from a single act of reasoning.

Key Terms
النقطتان
طرفا خط
مجموع الخط
الاعتبار
الحيثية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The two endpoints of a line are a counterexample to the rule that two similars cannot coexist.
Correction: The endpoints are not truly in the same undifferentiated locus because the locus is considered differently for each point (one termination on one side, the other on the other side).

Checkpoint
How does al-Jurjani respond to the objection that two endpoints of a line are similar and coexist in one locus?
A) He rejects the example outright
B) He argues that the locus differs by consideration of the side of termination
C) He says the points are not really similars
D) He admits the rule fails for points

Key Takeaway
The objection is resolved by noting that the locus is the same line but the consideration of termination differs, so the two points are not in the same locus in the same respect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a contradiction between a principle you hold and a counterexample, do you immediately abandon the principle or do you search for a deeper consideration (اعتبار) that might reconcile them? What would it mean for you to adopt the patience of the scholars in such moments?
?
The objector relied on the 'rules of the philosophers' without examining their foundation. Are there beliefs or opinions you hold simply because they are common or widely accepted, without verifying their basis? Will you commit to tracing one such belief back to its root this week?
?
Al-Jurjani distinguishes between two aspects of the same locus to resolve the objection. In your own life, do you conflate intentions or perspectives when judging others or yourself? How might your relationships change if you consistently distinguished between different aspects of a person's actions?

The Third Argument: If Two Similars Coexist, One Can Be Removed and Its Opposite Appears
Al-Jurjani presents the third argument that if two similars could coexist, one could be removed and its opposite arise, leading to the impossible coexistence of opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's third argument showing that allowing two similars to coexist leads to the impossible coexistence of opposites.

Core Idea
If two like things can coexist, one can vanish and its opposite appear, making opposites coexist.

Al-Jurjani now presents a third argument that targets the possibility of two like things existing together. He shows that if they could, it would lead to the impossible scenario of opposites coexisting. This argument relies on the removal of one similar and the subsequent fitness of the locus for the opposite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Let the flow of the argument come through.
Notice that the passage includes a gloss introduced by 'قوله'.
2
Identify the logical steps
The argument proceeds from assuming coexistence is possible, to the removal of one similar, to the locus becoming fit for the opposite, and finally to the contradiction of opposites coexisting. List these steps in order.
3
Understand the gloss
The gloss explains that the removal of one opposite makes the locus receptive to the other opposite, and that opposite is also opposite to the remaining similar. This solidifies the contradiction.
The gloss is clarifying a point about why the opposite can arise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه أي الاجتماع على تقدير جوازه لا يجب بحيث يمتنع زواله بعد حصوله فإذا اجتمع سوادان مثلا في محل واحد جاز أن ينتفي عنه أحدهما بقاء الآخر وإذا انتفى عن المحل أحد المثلين فيجوز مع اتصافه بضد المثل المنتفي لأن زوال أحد الضدين عن المحل فإنه يمتنع اجتماعهما بهويتهما إذ لا يجوز الانتقال على شيء منهما حتى يتصور اجتماعهما في محل. قوله: (المحل مصحح لاتصافه بالضد الآخر) وأنه أي ذلك الضد ضد أيضاً له أي للمثل الباقي فيلزم اجتماع السواد الباقي مع ضده هذا خلف.
The third: namely, that coexistence—given its permissibility—is not necessary such that its removal after occurrence is impossible. For if two blacknesses, for example, coexist in one locus, it would be permissible for one of them to be removed from it while the other remains. And when one of the two similars is removed from the locus, then it is permissible for that locus to be qualified by the opposite of the removed similar, because the removal of one of the two opposites from the locus—for it is impossible for them to coexist in their essences—since it is not permissible to transition from something until their coexistence in a locus is imagined. His saying: (the locus makes it fit to be qualified by the other opposite) and that this opposite is also an opposite of the remaining similar, so the coexistence of the remaining blackness with its opposite is necessitated—this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the coexistence of two similars. It argues that if two similars (e.g., two instances of blackness) could coexist in one locus, they would not necessarily persist together forever; one could cease while the other remains. If one blackness disappears, the locus becomes fit to receive its opposite, whiteness. Since black and white are opposites, the remaining blackness would then coexist with whiteness, which is impossible. The gloss clarifies that the removal of one opposite makes the locus receptive to the other opposite, and that opposite is also an opposite of the remaining similar, leading to the impossible coexistence of opposites.

Key Terms
السوادان
الضدان
الزوال
الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument assumes that removal of one similar necessarily leads to the opposite arising, but the argument only claims it is possible given the locus's capacity.
Correction: The argument does not require that the opposite must arise; it only says that if it is possible for one similar to vanish, then it is also possible for its opposite to appear, and that possibility is enough to derive a contradiction because the opposite would clash with the remaining similar.

Checkpoint
What does the third argument conclude would happen if two like things could coexist in one locus?
A) They would remain together forever
B) One could be removed, allowing its opposite to arise, leading to coexistence of opposite and the remaining similar
C) They would become distinct
D) The locus would be destroyed

Key Takeaway
The third argument shows that allowing coexistence of similars leads to the absurdity of opposites coexisting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever try to hold two contradictory beliefs at the same time? What is the 'impossible combination' you are tolerating, and how can you honestly resolve it using the clarity this argument demands?
?
This argument shows that if we allow one impossibility, it leads to another. Is there a small compromise in your faith or actions that you have allowed, thinking it harmless, which actually sets up an inevitable contradiction with what you truly believe? What will you do about it now?
?
The argument uses a logical reductio to force a conclusion. When have you avoided following an argument to its end because you feared the conclusion? What would it cost you to be consistent with the truth you already know?
