Contradiction of Positive and Negative from One Cause
Analyses an objection about contradiction from one cause and resolves it with a distinction in attribution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator resolves the apparent contradiction of a single cause producing both an effect and its negation by distinguishing real from conceptual attribution.

Core Idea
Contradiction is avoided when we distinguish real attributes from merely conceptual ones.

This passage tackles a key objection in the third aim: If a single simple cause is the source of both (a) and non-(a), does that not entail a logical contradiction? The commentator carefully analyzes the logical forms of the propositions involved and introduces a crucial distinction between real attribution (ittiṣāf ḥaqīqī) and conceptual attribution (ittiṣāf iʿtibārī) to dissolve the paradox.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection claims that if a cause is a source for (a) and also a source for non-(a) (by being a source for something else), then both a positive and a negative predicate apply to it, leading to contradiction.
Notice the objection assumes both predicates are real attributes.
2
Identify the mistaken response
An initial response says contradiction only occurs if time is unified, but that time unity is impossible. The commentator says this is a mistake.
The mistake lies in misinterpreting the logical form of the propositions.
3
Follow the correction of logical forms
The commentator shows that 'source for non-(a)' is itself a simple positive, not a modified positive that entails a simple negative. The genuine modified positive is 'not a source for (a)'.
This subtle distinction prevents the entailment of a simple negative.
4
Grasp the distinction between real and conceptual attribution
Even if the cause is a complete cause for both (a) and (b), the attribution of 'non-occurrence of (a)' to the cause is merely conceptual—i.e., we can abstract it from the cause, but it is not a real attribute. Thus there is no real contradiction.
This resolves the objection without requiring temporal disunity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثالث : استناد آثار متعددة إلى مؤثر واحد
المعدولة مستلزمة للسالبة المحصلة فيصدق أن هذه الجهة مصدر لـ (ا)، وغير مصدر لـ (ا)، وهما متناقضان . قلنا : إنما يتناقضان إن لو كان الزمان فيهما متحداً، وهو ممتنع . كذا ذكره بعضهم، وهو سهو ؛ لأن قولنا هذه الجهة مصدر لـ (ا)، وإن كانت موجبة محصلة، لكن قولنا هذه الجهة مصدر لغير (ا) ليست موجبة معدولة، حتى يستلزم سالبة محصلة هي نقيض لتلك الموجبة المحصلة، بل هي أيضاً موجبة محصلة المحمول لكن لمحمولها متعلق معدول . نعم قولنا : هذه الجهة غير مصدر لـ (ا) موجبة معدولة والفرق بينه وبين قولنا هذه الجهة مصدر لغير (۱) بين [لاسترة؟]. قوله : ( إنما يتناقضان إلخ) يعني أن صدور (۱) وصدور (ب) ، وإن اتحد زمانهما لكون الجهة علة تامة لهما، لكن اتصاف صدور (ب) بسلب صدور (۱) ليس اتصافاً حقيقياً، حتى يلزم اتحاد زمان صدور (۱) وسلبه، بل هو اتصاف انتزاعي مصداقه كونه بحيث يصح انتزاعه منه، فلا يلزم اتصاف الجهة بالنقيضين في زمان واحد فاندفع ما قيل : إن اتحاد الزمان هاهنا ضروري بناء على فرض كون البسيط علة تامة لكل منهما .
The Fifth Observatory - Third Aim: Attribution of Multiple Effects to a Single Cause. The modified [proposition] entails the simple negative, so it is true that this source is a source for (a) and not a source for (a), and they are contradictory. We said: They are contradictory only if the time in them is unified, which is impossible. Such is mentioned by some, and it is a mistake; because our statement 'this source is a source for (a)', even if it is a simple positive, but our statement 'this source is a source for non-(a)' is not a modified positive, so that it entails a simple negative which is the contradictory of that simple positive; rather it is also a simple positive in predicate, but its predicate has a modified relation. Yes, our statement 'this source is not a source for (a)' is a modified positive, and the difference between it and our statement 'this source is a source for non-(a)' is [unclear]. His words: (They are contradictory only if etc.) means that the occurrence of (a) and the occurrence of (b), even if their time is unified because the source is a complete cause for both, yet the attribution of the occurrence of (b) to the negation of occurrence of (a) is not a real attribution, so that it necessitates the unity of time of occurrence of (a) and its negation; rather it is a conceptual attribution, its referent being that it is such that it is valid to abstract it from it; so it does not necessitate the attribution of the source to the two contradictories in one time. Thus is rebutted what was said: That the unity of time here is necessary based on the assumption that the simple is a complete cause for each of them.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection within the third aim concerning the attribution of multiple effects to a single cause. The objection claims that if a simple cause is a source for (a) and also not a source for (a) (by way of producing non-(a)), then both positive and negative propositions hold of the same thing, implying a contradiction. The initial response states that contradiction requires unity of time, which is impossible here. However, the commentator notes that this response is a mistake because the proposition 'this source is a source for non-(a)' is not a modified positive that entails a simple negative; rather it is itself a simple positive with a modified predicate. The genuine modified positive is 'this source is not a source for (a)'. Then the commentator explains that the unity of time is not problematic because the attribution of 'non-occurrence of (a)' to the source is not a real attribution but a conceptual one (iqtisāf iʿtibārī). Just because we can say 'this source is such that it does not produce (a)' does not mean it actually has the attribute of non-production in a real sense. Thus the source is not really described by both contradictories in one time. This rebuts the claim that temporal unity is necessary given the cause is complete.

موجبة محصلة
موجبة معدولة
سالبة محصلة
اتصاف حقيقي
اتصاف انتزاعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'non-occurrence' is a real predicate attaching to the cause, just like occurrence.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the attribution of non-occurrence is only conceptual (ittiṣāf iʿtibārī), not a real attribute, so there is no real contradiction.

Checkpoint

B) Because the proposition 'this source is a source for non-(a)' is not a modified positive.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between real and conceptual attribution resolves the apparent contradiction of a single cause producing both an effect and its negation.

The Third Premise and Conditions for the Proof of Finite Natural Power
Al-Jurjani explains the objection to the third premise and outlines two necessary conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the third premise (power of half is half of power of whole) is not conceded and what two conditions are required for the proof of finite natural power.

Core Idea
The proof of finite natural power requires uniform power distribution, not guaranteed by dividing the body.

This passage addresses a critical objection to one of the premises in the kalam argument for the finitude of natural powers. The commentator explains that the premise assuming proportional division of power is not universally conceded, and outlines two specific conditions that must hold for the proof to work. This clarifies the scope of the argument and distinguishes it from proofs regarding forced power.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Note the technical terms and the flow of the argument.
The passage begins with 'الثالث' indicating this is the third premise being discussed.
2
Identify the objection and the two conditions
Find the objection to the premise and then locate the two matters (الأمران) that are necessary for the proof. Understand why they are required.
The conditions are: proportional division of power (قوة النصف نصف قوة الكل) and uniform distribution (التشابه).
3
Contrast with forced power proof
Notice the final sentence where the commentator contrasts this proof with the proof of non-finite forced power. Understand why the forced power proof does not need these conditions.
In forced power, the bodies themselves are proportional, so the powers are automatically proportional without needing uniform distribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أنها أي قوة النصف نصف قوة الكل، وهو أيضاً غير مسلم؛ لجواز تفاوت القوة في أجزاء الجسم، فلا يكون انقسامها على نسبة انقسام الجسم، وهذان الأمران معتبران في برهان تناهي القوة الطبيعية، ولهذا قيل : إن هذا البرهان إنما يجري في قوة حالة في جسم لا معاوقة فيه منقسمة بانقسام ذلك الجسم على التشابه كالطبائع في الأجسام العنصرية وكالنفوس المنطبعة في الأجرام الفلكية، لكن التحريك الطبيعي المقابل للتحريك القسري يتناول أيضاً التحريك الصادر عن النفوس. قوله: (معتبران إلخ) بخلاف برهان لاتناهي القوة القسرية فإن الجسمين المتناسبين بالضعفية والنصفية موجودان والقوتان على التناسب المذكور متحققتان فيهما، فلا حاجة في ذلك البرهان إلى هذين الأمرين.
The third: that the power of the half is half of the power of the whole, and it is also not conceded, because of the possibility of disparity of power in the parts of the body, so its division is not proportional to the division of the body. And these two matters are considered in the proof of the finitude of natural power. For this reason it was said: This proof runs only in a power subsisting in a body without resistance, divided by the division of that body uniformly, like the natures in elemental bodies and like the impressed souls in celestial bodies, but natural motion in contrast to forced motion includes also the motion issuing from souls. His saying: (considered etc.) differs from the proof of the non-finite of forced power, because two bodies proportional as double and half exist, and the two powers exist in them on the mentioned proportion, so there is no need in that proof for these two matters.
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage deals with the objection to the third premise of the philosophers' argument for the finitude of natural power. The premise claimed that the power of half a body is exactly half the power of the whole body. The commentator states that this premise is not conceded because power may vary among parts, so its division is not necessarily proportional to the division of the body. He then notes that two matters are necessary for the proof: (1) the division of power is proportional to the division of the body, and (2) the division is uniform (on التشابه). Therefore, the proof only applies to powers that are uniformly distributed like natural qualities in elemental bodies or impressed souls in celestial bodies, not to forced motion. The commentator then contrasts this with the proof of the non-finite of forced power, where the proportional relation between bodies and powers directly obtains without needing these extra conditions.

Key Terms
قوة النصف
تناهي القوة الطبيعية
التشابه
القوة القسرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof of finite natural power applies to any power regardless of distribution.
Correction: The proof only applies to powers that are uniformly distributed and without resistance, as explained by the two conditions.

Checkpoint
Why is the third premise ('power of half is half of power of whole') not conceded?
A) Because the division of power may not be proportional to the division of the body
B) Because the whole body has no half
C) Because forced power is indivisible
D) Because natural power is infinite

Key Takeaway
The proof of finite natural power is conditional: it requires the power to be uniformly distributed in the body, otherwise the third premise fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a good habit you are trying to build in your life (like prayer, fasting, or charity). Do you assume that your effort is uniformly effective across all areas? Where might you be neglecting parts of your life, and how does this lesson’s demand for 'uniform distribution' challenge you to examine the consistency of your devotion?
?
This lesson distinguishes between natural power (coming from within, uniformly) and forced power (externally imposed). Are your acts of worship flowing naturally from a uniform faith, or are they forced by external circumstances like peer pressure or routine? What would it take for your obedience to become a natural, uniform disposition of your soul?
?
The proof requires 'no resistance' (لا معاوقة) for the power to fully manifest. What resistances—such as distractions, sins, or doubts—are hindering the full expression of your faith? Commit to removing one specific resistance this week so that your spiritual power can act without obstruction.

Ibn Sina's refutation of the objections and its critique
Al-Jurjani presents Ibn Sina's argument and then refutes it point by point.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument for infinite power being diffused in the whole, and Al-Jurjani's critique that such reasoning relies on mere mental estimation.

Core Idea
A part of a power is not necessarily a power—Al-Jurjani dismantles Ibn Sina's diffusion argument.

Al-Jurjani presents Ibn Sina's attempt to refute objections against the philosophers' proof of finite natural power. Ibn Sina argues that infinite power, though belonging to the whole, is diffused throughout it, so parts have a share. However, Al-Jurjani systematically rejects each premise, showing that the whole argument is a mere mental estimation without actual physical reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the two-part structure: Ibn Sina's claim followed by the critique.
2
Identify Ibn Sina's analogies
Ibn Sina compares the diffusion of power to mixture properties and ship movers. Understand why he thinks parts retain some power.
He claims the power is 'sāriya' (diffused).
3
Examine Al-Jurjani's objections
Al-Jurjani gives three specific objections: denial of diffusion, rejection that part of power is power, and rejection of proportionality. Find each in the text.
4
Understand the final dismissal
Al-Jurjani concludes that the entire proof is a mere mental estimation (taqdīr) like the hypothetical constructions of engineers, with no actual existence.
This undermines the force of Ibn Sina's argument.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider how Al-Jurjani's critique defends the original objection and shows the limits of purely hypothetical reasoning in theology.
This lesson highlights the importance of distinguishing between mental constructs and physical reality.

Know that the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) in the Shifa strained to repel these objections, and said: 'Then a questioner may say that this infinite power possibly exists only for the whole body; if the body is divided, it ceases, so nothing of that power exists for the part, so the part cannot do anything that the whole can; because all this power belongs to the whole, as is found with powers in composite bodies after mixture, and it does not exist for any of the elements from which they were mixed. And just as the movers of a ship, one of them does not move it at all.' We say: The matter is not as you have stated. For although the power belongs to the body in the state of the composition of its parts and in the state of its mixture, it is nevertheless diffused throughout its entirety; otherwise it would be a power for some of the whole and not the whole. And if it is diffused in its entirety, then some of it has some power, so the simple part in the state of mixture thus carries the power acquired after mixture that is diffused in the whole. And it carries it only in the state of isolation; for it is not necessary that our assuming a part of the body compels us to take that part under the condition of cutting and separating it, so that someone might say: the separated part carries no power. Rather, it suffices for us to point to a part while it remains as it is, and we then ascertain the state of what issues from that part from the power that is in it alone, by way of estimation. And the movers of the ship: one of them, even if he cannot move the whole ship, can nonetheless move a smaller one inevitably, and what we said follows.' End. And it is not hidden what is in it, for we do not concede that the power is diffused in the whole. His saying: 'otherwise it would be a power for some of the whole and not the whole' is denied, because it is possible for it to subsist in the whole as a whole without any of its parts. And if it were conceded that it is diffused, we do not concede the entailment derived from his saying 'and if it is diffused in its entirety, then some of it has some power', because it is not necessary that some part of the power be a power. And if that were conceded, it does not entail that the two powers are proportional to the two bodies. So the mentioned objections still apply on this estimation as well, I mean by considering the part connected to the whole, and building the proof on these matters is like the estimations of engineers in their non-existence in actuality, for we deny the possibility of these matters in actual existence, and mere postulation does not avail.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's attempted refutation of the objections raised against the philosophers' proof of finite natural power, followed by the commentator's critique. Ibn Sina argues that infinite power belongs to the whole body as a composite, not to its parts when separated, analogous to mixture properties or the movers of a ship who individually cannot move it. He claims that the power is diffused throughout the whole, so a part (while still attached) possesses a portion. The commentator rejects this on several points: (1) power may subsist in the whole without being diffused into parts. (2) Even if diffused, a part of the power need not itself be a power (it could be a fraction that is not effective). (3) Even if it were a power, the ratio between powers need not match the ratio between bodies. Finally, the commentator dismisses the entire proof as a mere mental estimation (تقدير) without actual existence, echoing engineers' hypothetical constructions that have no reality.

التمحل
القوة السارية
تقدير المهندسين
المنوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A part of a power is always a power.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that a part of a power need not be effective power; it could be a fraction that is not a power.

Checkpoint
What is the commentator's main objection to Ibn Sina's claim that power is 'sāriya' (diffused) in the whole?
A) He denies that the power is diffused in the whole
B) He accepts diffusion but denies that a part of power is necessarily a power
C) Both A and B
D) He agrees with Ibn Sina

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani demonstrates that Ibn Sina's diffusion argument fails because it relies on unproven assumptions and mere mental estimation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever confidently believed an argument was sound simply because it was structured logically, even though its premises were not grounded in reality? How does Al-Jurjani's rejection of 'mere estimation' challenge you to examine the foundations of your own beliefs?
?
When you find yourself defending a cherished view, do you—like Ibn Sina—resort to strained arguments (tamaḥḥul) to shield it from criticism? What would it cost you to admit that your proof depends on unproven assumptions?
?
Al-Jurjani denies that a part of a power is necessarily a power. In your own life, do you assume that small fragments of effort, knowledge, or virtue automatically add up to something real? Where might you be counting fractions as wholes, deceiving yourself about your actual capacity or character?

The Impossibility of Circularity and the Correlatives Objection
Definition of circularity and refutation of the correlatives objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why circularity is impossible in causal relationships and how the objection from correlatives is refuted by distinguishing real entities from mental considerations.

Core Idea
Circularity is impossible; correlatives are mental constructs, not real dependencies.

Theologians argue that circular reasoning in causality is impossible, either by necessity or inference. An opponent might point to correlatives (like father and son) as examples of mutual dependence, but this objection fails because correlatives are mental considerations, not external realities. This lesson explains the key distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms: دور, مفتقر, مضافان.
2
Identify the two meanings of 'dependent'
The passage distinguishes between the apparent meaning (effect must have a cause, but cause need not have an effect) and the correct meaning (mutual entailment when taken as such).
The correct meaning is that cause and effect are correlatives.
3
Understand the objection from correlatives
The objection claims that correlatives like father and son each depend on the other, which would imply circularity. See how the objection is presented.
The objection is a potential counterexample to the impossibility of circularity.
4
Analyze the refutation
The refutation states that correlatives are mental considerations (اعتباريان), not real external entities, so they are not subject to real dependence. There are two answers: the first is that they are not real; the second involves relational existence.
The commentator prefers the second answer.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this refutation preserves the proof that circularity is impossible in real causal chains.
This connects to the learning objective.

The impossibility of circularity, either necessarily or through inference, with respect to the dependent and that on which it depends: the dependent is possible relative to that on which it depends, and the apparent meaning of the two is that the effect must have a cause, unlike the cause, for it does not necessarily have an effect as such, but it can have one. You may interpret it by the first meaning, which is correct. Then the Imam said: 'The two correlatives do not refute the first or stronger proof as an objection that each of them depends on the other, implying each's dependence on itself, and that the relation of each to the other is by necessity and possibility. If what you mentioned were correct, the two correlatives would be impossible. They do not refute what he mentioned because they are mental considerations, not existing in the external world, so they are not described as dependent at all.' His statement: 'The Lubab al-Arba'in by al-Qadi al-Urmawi.' His statement: 'You may interpret it etc.' means that by the dependent and that on which it depends the two meanings are intended, and by his saying 'necessary relative' and 'possible relative' [the meanings are] 'its relation is necessary' and 'its relation is possible.' His statement: 'It is the correct one' restricts correctness to the first meaning, indicating that the apparent meaning is corrupt. This is because if effect and cause are taken as such, there is mutual entailment, as one of the two correlatives cannot exist without the other; if taken in themselves, there is no entailment from the side of the effect either, while the discussion concerns effect and cause as such. His statement: 'So they are not described as dependent at all' means with respect to the existence predicated of them. What is said that the non-existence of the effect depends on the non-existence of the cause is refuted by what has been established: that the causality of non-existence for non-existence is in reality nothing but the non-causality of existence for existence. As for relational existence, each of the two real correlatives needs the other's subject, not the other itself, so there is no dependence at all. This answer is according to the theologians who deny the existence of relative accidents. From this aspect, the dependent and that on which it depends arise, and each becomes a source for a relation opposite to the other. That aspect is really the source for these two contradictory relations. For the necessary of the necessary of something is necessary for that thing, and the mediation of the attribute of dependent and depended-upon does not allow the conjunction of these two contradictory relations. This is clear to anyone with minimal reflection. No. His statement: 'But you may interpret it by the first meaning, which is correct' — the corruption that the second refers to is that the specific cause entails the specific effect, as previously mentioned, so his statement that 'the cause does not necessarily have an effect' is invalid. His statement: 'Because they are mental considerations' — mental matters have no intrinsic possibility with respect to existence and non-existence, though they have intrinsic possibility with respect to being attributed to something. Thus the difference between them and a non-existent possible becomes clear. So it cannot be objected that a non-existent possible is described as dependent on a preponderant for the side of non-existence. However, the establishment of dependence for the correlatives by virtue of the possibility of the subject's being described by them is sufficient for the objection. So the correct answer is the second.

Commentary
This passage begins by defining the impossibility of circularity (دور) either by necessity or by inference. It then distinguishes two meanings of the terms 'dependent' (مفتقر) and 'that on which it depends' (مفتقر إليه): the apparent meaning is that the effect must have a cause, but the cause need not have an effect; the correct meaning is the first. The commentary explains that if cause and effect are considered as such, they are mutually entailing (like correlatives), but if considered in themselves, there is no entailment. Then an objection is raised: correlatives (المضافان) like father and son are each dependent on the other, which would imply circularity. The answer is that they are mental considerations (اعتباريان), not real external entities, so they are not subject to real dependence. The commentator also addresses a nuance: even if correlatives are not real, the fact that a subject can be described by them might still pose a problem, but the second answer (the one about relational existence) is considered correct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that mutual dependence of correlatives like father and son proves circularity and thus refutes the impossibility of circularity.
Correction: Correlatives are mental considerations (اعتباريان), not real entities in the external world. Therefore, they are not subject to real dependence and do not constitute a counterexample.

B) Because they are both necessary.
C) Because they are both possible.
D) Because they are not dependent on each other.

Key Takeaway
Circularity is impossible in real causal chains; apparent mutual dependence like correlatives is only conceptual, not real.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any relationship or habit you treat as though you depend on it for your identity, even though in reality it is only a mental consideration? How would your life change if you reoriented that dependence solely on Allah?
?
You have just learned that circular dependency is impossible in real existence. Where in your spiritual life might you be unknowingly relying on a cycle of self-justification, where you depend on your deeds and your deeds depend on you, instead of a linear submission to Allah? What step can you take today to break that cycle?
?
The passage teaches that effect depends on cause, not vice versa. Are there people or things you act as though they depend on you for their existence or well-being, when in fact you are the one who depends on Allah for everything? Will you acknowledge your true dependence today?

The Two Meanings of Iftiqār and the Circularity Proof
Al-Lubab distinguishes two meanings of iftiqār for the circularity proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why mutual inseparability (تلازم) between cause and effect does not necessarily imply circularity, and how the definition of افتقار resolves the ambiguity in the circularity proof.

Core Idea
The circularity objection collapses if افتقار means mere inseparability, not posteriority.

This lesson unpacks a subtle but critical distinction in the kalam argument against circularity. The author of al-Lubab clarifies that the term افتقار can be understood in two ways: as absolute inseparability or as inseparability with posteriority. Depending on which meaning is used, the proof of circularity either succeeds or fails. We will trace this argument and see why the ambiguity applies to both the rejected and approved proofs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
Recall that in the argument against circularity, the proof relies on the concept of افتقار (dependence). The author now clarifies what افتقار means.
2
Read the passage from al-Lubab
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the two definitions of افتقار presented.
Pay attention to the words 'امتناع الانفكاك مطلقاً' and 'مع نعت المتأخر'.
3
Identify the two interpretations
The passage contrasts (1) افتقار as mere inseparability, and (2) افتقار as inseparability plus posteriority. Recognize that mutual inseparability is possible without circularity.
4
See the consequence of the second interpretation
If افتقار includes posteriority, then the proof of circularity becomes circular itself because it assumes what is disputed. The ambiguity applies to both the rejected and approved proofs.
5
Reflect on the resolution
Understand that the approved proof (الدليل المرضي) is based on the first interpretation, which avoids the ambiguity. Thus mutual inseparability from a common cause does not refute the proof.
This is a key insight: two things can be mutually inseparable due to a common cause, not mutual dependence.

أردت بتأخر المعلول معنى المعلولية، كان قولك : لزم تأخر الشيء عن معلوله جارياً مجري قولك : لزم معلولية الشيء لمعلوله، فيمنع بطلانه؛ لأنه عين المتنازع فيه . وإن أردت به معنی آخر فلا بد من تصويره وتقريره فالشبهة مشتركة بين الدليلين المردود والمرضي. قوله : ( تلازمهما على تقدير كونهما إلخ كما ذهب إليه الفلاسفة، وما قيل : على تقدير التلازم بينهما يلزم استلزام الشيء لنفسه، وحينئذ يتوجه أن اللزوم نسبة تقتضي التغاير فوهم مدفوع بما يذكره الشارح بقوله : وليس يلزم من تعاكس هذا المعنى بين المعلول والعلة إلخ، كما لا يخفى . قوله : ( لوحدة السبب ) كالتولد الذي هو سبب الأبوة والبنوة . قوله : ( من جواب إلخ) وهو قوله والجواب أن معنى التقدم قوله: (بين الدليلين المردود والمرضي) أي المردود عند الإمام وهو ما ذكره أولاً، والمرضي عنده وهو الأولى. قوله : ( ومع ما سبق من جواب شبهة (الإمام إنما بين الموصول بقوله : من جواب إلخ رداً لزعم من زعم أن المراد بما سبق كون النسبة الواحدة ممكنة وواجبة بجهتين إذ الدور لا يتحقق إلا باتحاد الجهة . قوله : ( الذي هو مبنى الدليل المرضي عنده المراد بالدليل المرضي هو الدليل الأول الذي عنونه بالأقوى؛ لأن السياق لا يناسبه ويمكن أن يكون جهة كون الدليل الثاني أقوى من الأول عدم ورود هذا الاعتراض عليه .
Upon itself, the Fifth Observation - Sixth Intention: Circularity is impossible either necessarily or by inference, let alone each depending on the other. Or we say: their mutual entailment, assuming they exist, is due to the unity of the cause that necessitates them, not due to each depending on the other, so they do not refute [the proof] at all. The author of al-Lubab said: 'With what preceded of the answer to the Imam's ambiguity concerning the priority of the cause, for iftiqār (dependence) that is the basis of the approved proof is absolute inseparability, then mutual dependence in this sense from both sides is possible because the inseparability of each of the two things from the other may be impossible. There is no impossibility in that; rather it occurs between mutually entailing things. Nothing follows from the reciprocity of this sense between effect and cause except the impossibility of each's separation from the other, and there is no harm in that. But if what is intended by iftiqār is inseparability together with the attribute of being posterior, i.e., the posteriority of the dependent to that on which it depends, then with respect to posteriority—namely the posteriority of the dependent which is the effect—the same ambiguity arises as with respect to priority—namely the priority of that on which it depends which is the cause. For the conclusion of the proof then becomes: the dependent, i.e., the effect, is posterior to the cause; so if the cause were an effect of it, it would depend, i.e., be posterior to it, resulting in the thing's being posterior to itself by two degrees. It is said: if by the posteriority of the effect you mean effectness, then your statement 'the thing's posteriority to its effect' is like 'the thing's being an effect of its effect,' which is not absurd because it is the point in dispute. If you mean something else, it must be imagined and established. So the ambiguity is common to both the rejected and the approved proofs.' His statement: 'Their mutual entailment assuming they exist etc.' as the philosophers hold. What is said that assuming mutual entailment between them implies the thing's implying itself, and then the implication is a relation requiring difference, but this illusion is refuted by what the commentator mentions in his statement: 'Nothing follows from the reciprocity of this sense between effect and cause etc.' as is not hidden. His statement: 'Due to the unity of the cause' like filiation (tawallud) which is the cause of fatherhood and sonship. His statement: 'From the answer etc.' is his statement: 'And the answer is that the meaning of priority...' His statement: 'Between the rejected and the approved proofs' means the rejected according to the Imam is what he mentioned first, and the approved according to him is the first. His statement: 'With what preceded of the answer to the Imam's ambiguity' he only clarified the relative clause by his statement 'from the answer etc.' to refute the claim of one who claimed that the intended meaning of what preceded is that one relation is possible and necessary in two respects, since circularity only occurs with unity of respect. His statement: 'Which is the basis of the approved proof according to him'—the intended proof is the first one that he called the stronger; because the context does not suit it, it may be that the reason the second proof is stronger than the first is that this objection does not apply to it.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage clarifies the meaning of افتقار (dependence) as used in the proof of the impossibility of circularity. It begins by arguing that even if two things are mutually inseparable (تلازم), this does not imply circularity because the inseparability may be due to a common cause, not mutual dependence. The author of al-Lubab is cited: if افتقار means mere inseparability, then mutual inseparability is possible and does not entail circularity. But if افتقار includes the notion of posteriority (تأخر) of the dependent to that on which it depends, then the proof of circularity becomes circular itself: it assumes what it tries to prove, because the posteriority of the effect to the cause is the very thing in dispute. Thus the ambiguity (شبهة) applies to both the rejected and the approved proofs. The commentary then explains that the philosophers' view of mutual entailment does not force the thing to imply itself; the illusion is refuted. Finally, it identifies the 'approved proof' (الدليل المرضي) as the first proof, called the stronger (الأقوى), and notes that the second proof is stronger because this particular objection does not apply to it.

Key Terms
افتقار
امتناع الانفكاك
تلازم
تأخر
تقدم
دليل مرضي
دليل مردود
شبهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things are mutually inseparable (تلازم), then they necessarily depend on each other, implying circularity.
Correction: Mutual inseparability may be due to a common cause (e.g., fatherhood and sonship both depend on filiation), not mutual dependence; circularity is not entailed unless افتقار includes posteriority.

Checkpoint
According to the author of al-Lubab, when does mutual dependence (تعاكس الافتقار) between cause and effect not imply circularity?
A) When افتقار is understood as absolute inseparability.
B) When افتقار is understood as priority and posteriority.
C) When the cause and effect are not real.
D) When the effect is prior to the cause.

Key Takeaway
The definition of افتقار determines whether mutual inseparability implies circularity; only when افتقار includes posteriority does the circularity proof become problematic, and that interpretation is self-defeating.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about your dependence on Allah, do you ever assume that this dependence implies a kind of 'posteriority' or inferiority that affects your worth? How does the lesson's distinction between mere inseparability and dependence with posteriority challenge that assumption? Will you commit to seeing your need for Allah as an inseparable connection that elevates, rather than diminishes, your existence?
?
Have you ever found yourself in a situation where you assumed that a relationship of mutual need (like between friends or spouses) necessarily leads to a harmful circular dependency? Reflect on how the concept of 'mutual inseparability without circularity' might apply to your human relationships. Which relationship will you re-examine in light of this?
?
This passage warns against assuming a definition that leads to self-contradiction. In your own thinking, are there concepts you define in a way that creates unnecessary confusion or doubt? Can you identify one belief or assumption where redefining a key term (like 'need' or 'priority') could resolve a personal struggle? Write down one term you will reconsider.

Causes of a Contingent Series: Three Possibilities and a Key Distinction
Al-Iji enumerates possibilities and clarifies a confusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three logical possibilities for the cause of a contingent series and why the first possibility is impossible, as well as the crucial distinction between two types of causation that are often confused.

Core Idea
A cause must precede its effect; a series cannot cause itself.

Al-Iji begins by laying out the logical options for what could cause a contingent series. He immediately eliminates one option by appealing to the basic causal principle that a cause must exist before its effect. He then warns against a common confusion that might lead someone to think the series can cause itself in a different sense. This passage sets the stage for the subsequent refutation of infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three possibilities
Read the passage and note the three candidates for the cause of the series: the series itself, something inside it, or something outside it.
These are exhaustive logical possibilities.
2
Understand why the first is impossible
Notice the reasoning: the cause must precede the effect, so the series cannot be its own cause. Ask yourself why this is a strong argument.
This relies on the principle of causal priority.
3
Distinguish between two types of causation
Al-Iji points out a confusion between causing each individual member by another and causing the whole series by the whole. Make sure you see why these are different.
The first is a chain, the second is self-causation.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Realize that the first type (each caused by another) is what the opponent claims, and it leads to infinite regress. The second type is obviously false.
This passage sets up the need to refute the first type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إما أن تكون عين السلسلة أو داخلة فيها أو خارجة عنها والأول محال ; لأن العلة الموجدة لشيء سواء كان ذلك الشيء واحداً معيناً أو مركباً من آحاد متناهية، أو غير متناهية، يجب أن يتقدم بالوجود على ذلك الشيء، ومن المستحيل تقدم المجموع على نفسه بالوجود والاشتباه إنما وقع بين تعليل كل واحد من السلسلة بآخر منها، وبين تعليل مجموعها بمجموعها وهما أمران متغايران والأول هو المتنازع فيه الذي نحن بصدد إبطاله بطريق الاستدلال والثاني مما ينبه على بطلانه، فإنه باطل
Either it (the cause) is the very series, or inside it, or outside it. The first is impossible, because the efficient cause of a thing, whether that thing is a single determinate or composed of finite or infinite individuals, must precede that thing in existence, and it is impossible for the whole to precede itself in existence. The confusion only occurs between the causation of each individual of the series by another of them, and the causation of the whole series by its entirety; they are two distinct matters. The first is the disputed point which we are engaged in refuting by way of demonstration, and the second is that which indicates its own falsity, for it is false.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a definitional argument from al-Iji. It begins by stating the three possibilities for what the cause of a contingent series could be: either the series itself, or something inside the series, or something outside the series. The first possibility is immediately ruled out because a cause must exist before its effect; the whole series cannot precede itself. The author then clarifies a common confusion: the interlocutor might think that explaining each individual member of the series by another member is the same as explaining the whole series by the whole series. Al-Iji insists these are two distinct types of causation. The first (each individual caused by another within the series) is the point at issue, which he intends to refute. The second (the whole series caused by the whole) is self-evidently false because it would entail the series causing itself. Thus, the argument sets up the need to examine the remaining possibilities.

Key Terms
العلة الموجدة
السلسلة
الاشتباه
تعليل الآحاد
تعليل المجموع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The causation of each individual member of a series by another member is the same as the causation of the whole series by the whole series.
Correction: They are distinct: the first is a form of internal causal explanation, the second is self-causation of the entire series, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for the cause of a contingent series to be the series itself?
A) Because the series is infinite and cannot be a cause.
B) Because the cause must precede its effect, and the series cannot precede itself.
C) Because the series is composed of parts that are all contingent.
D) Because the series has no external cause.

Key Takeaway
The cause of a contingent series cannot be the series itself due to the principle of causal priority, and a common confusion between two types of causation must be avoided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that a chain of explanations could go on forever without needing an ultimate cause? How does al-Iji's distinction between two types of causation expose the flaw in that assumption? Examine your own reasoning: are you willing to accept that you are not self-sufficient and must rely on a Prior Cause?
?
The confusion here is subtle—many people conflate internal explanation with external causation. When you think about your own existence, do you ever feel like you can explain it entirely by your parents, their parents, and so on, without needing a First Cause? Let this lesson force you to see that the series as a whole cannot cause itself. Are you ready to acknowledge that you owe your existence to something outside the chain?
?
If a cause must precede its effect, then nothing in this world can be its own cause. What does that imply about your own life? You are an effect—do you live as if you are self-created, or do you live in conscious dependence on your Creator? Let this truth reshape how you see your daily breath.

The First Proof Against Infinite Regress: Independent Cause Cannot Be Self-Causing
Presents the first argument that an independent cause of a series would be self-causing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof against infinite regress: that an independent cause of a contingent series would be self-causing, which is impossible, and that this proof applies only to ascending causal chains.

Core Idea
An independent cause of a series would have to cause itself—an impossibility.

This lesson delves into the first argument against infinite regress presented by al-Ṭaftāzānī. The key idea is that if a contingent series had a single independent cause, that cause would itself be contingent and therefore require a cause, leading to a contradiction. The passage presents this reasoning and also refutes a potential counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the dense logical structure.
2
Understand 'independent cause'
The term 'independent in effect' (mustaqilla bi-al-ta’thīr) means the cause has no partner in bringing about the series. Grasp why this matters for the argument.
3
Follow the argument logic
Trace the steps: if such an independent cause exists within the series, it is contingent → needs an external cause → but then it's not independent → so it must cause itself → impossible.
Self-causation is impossible because a thing cannot act before existing.
4
Consider the refutation
The author rejects the idea that the penultimate cause could be the cause of all while being caused by something else. This still leads to an independent cause in the chain.
5
Note the scope
The proof only applies to ascending chains of causes, not descending series of effects. Why might that be important?
This prevents misapplication of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثامن : التسلسل محال أن يستند الممكن إلى علة مستقلة بالتأثير على معنى أن لا يكون له شريك في التأثير في تلك السلسلة، وإلا كان ذلك البعض مؤثراً في نفسه؛ لأنه ممكن، فلا بد له من علة مؤثرة، ولا يمكن أن تكون تلك العلة المؤثرة غير ذلك البعض، وإلا لم يكن ذلك البعض مستقلا بالتأثير في السلسلة، بل كان له شريك فيه ولا يمكن أن يكون في السلسلة المفروضة بعض مستغن عن المؤثر، كما في المركب من الواجب والممكن، وبهذا تبين بطلان ما قد قيل : من أنه يجوز أن يكون ما قبل المعلول الأخير علة للجميع، وهو معلول لما قبله بمرتبة واحدة وهكذا. واعلم أن هذا الدليل إنما يجري في تسلسل الممكنات متصاعدة في العلل لا متنازلة في المعلولات كما لا يخفى على ذي فكرة.
The Fifth Observatory - The Eighth Objective: Regress is impossible. It is impossible for the contingent to depend on a cause that is independent in effect, meaning that it has no partner in the effect in that series. Otherwise, that part would be effective upon itself, because it is contingent, so it must have an effective cause, and that effective cause cannot be other than that part, otherwise that part would not be independent in effecting the series, but would have a partner in it. And it is not possible in the supposed series for a part to be independent of the effecter, as in a composite of necessary and contingent. And by this the invalidity of what has been said becomes clear: that it is permissible for that which is before the last effect to be the cause of all, while it is itself an effect of what is before it by one rank, and so on. And know that this proof only applies to an ascending series of contingents in causes, not a descending series in effects, as is not hidden from one who reflects.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against infinite regress. The author states that a contingent cannot depend on a cause that is completely independent in effect, meaning it has no partner in causing that series. If a part of the series were independent, it would have to be self-causing, which is impossible. The proof then refutes the claim that the penultimate cause (the one before the final effect) could be the cause of the entire series, because that penultimate cause is itself an effect of what precedes it. A final note clarifies that this proof only applies to ascending chains of causes, not descending chains of effects.

Key Terms
التسلسل
علة مستقلة بالتأثير
مؤثراً في نفسه
ما قبل المعلول الأخير
متعلول لما قبله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof against regress applies to both ascending and descending series.
Correction: The author explicitly states the proof only applies to ascending chains of causes, not descending series of effects.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that an independent cause for the entire series leads to self-causation?
A) Because the independent cause must be outside the series.
B) Because the independent cause would be a part of the series and thus contingent, requiring an external cause.
C) Because the independent cause would have a partner in effect.
D) Because the independent cause is the penultimate cause.

Key Takeaway
The first proof against infinite regress shows that an independent cause of a contingent series would be self-causing, which is impossible, and this argument applies only to ascending causal chains.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are there areas in your life where you rely on endless justifications—'If only this, then that'—to avoid facing the ultimate cause of your existence? How does this proof expose the emptiness of such infinite excuses?
?
This argument shows that an independent chain of causes collapses in impossibility. Where have you falsely claimed independence—in your actions, your sustenance, or your faith—as if you were a cause unto yourself? Will you renounce that today?
?
If an infinite regress of causes is impossible, then your entire being hangs on a single, uncaused Cause. Have you lived today as if that were true—or have you acted as if your life were a self-sufficient chain?

The Second Proof: Mapping Two Infinite Series
Presents the second proof using a one-to-one mapping of two series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof against infinite regress, which uses the mapping of two infinite series where one is a finite subset of the other.

Core Idea
Two infinite series, one containing a finite extra, cannot be mapped one-to-one without contradiction.

The author presents a second, more elegant proof against infinite regress. Instead of focusing on a single chain, he constructs two series and compares them. This approach reveals a logical contradiction that undermines any claim of an actual infinite regress of causes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Let the author's phrasing sink in.
Notice the concise style.
2
Identify the two series
The proof assumes two series: one from an effect ascending ad infinitum, and another from what is before it finite ad infinitum.
Also consider the descending case.
3
Understand the mapping
The two series are superimposed from a common beginning, matching first to first, second to second, etc.
This is a one-to-one correspondence.
4
Grasp the contradiction
Because one series has a finite extra segment at the start, the one-to-one mapping would run out of partners for the excess, showing impossibility.
Infinite regress would require such a mapping.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الثاني) من وجوه إبطال التسلسل (أنا نفرض من معلول (ما ) بطريق التصاعد ) إلى غير النهاية جملة ومما قبله بمتناه إلى غير النهاية جملة أخرى، هذا إذا كان التسلسل في جانب العلل، وإذا كان في جانب المعلولات فرضنا من علة معينة بطريق التنازل إلى غير النهاية جملة ومما بعدها بمتناه إلى غير النهاية جملة أخرى فيحصل هناك جملتان غير متناهيتين إحداهما زائدة على الأخرى بعدد متناه. (ثم نطبق الجملتين ) أي إحداهما على الأخرى ( من ذلك المبدأ أي من ذلك الجانب الذي لكل واحدة منهما فيه مبدأ، فالأول) من إحداهما ( بالأول ) أي بإزاء الأول من الأخرى ) والثاني بالثاني، وهلم جراً. فإن كان بإزاء كل واحد من الجملة ( الزائدة واحد من الجملة ( الناقصة ) في عدة الآحاد جميع تلك الأحاد سوى قوله : ( لا متنازلة في المعلولات إلخ فيه بحث لأنه إذا فرض المبدأ علة معينة صدر عنها معلول، ومن ذلك معلول ،آخر وهلم جرا إلى غير النهاية، يكون كل واحد من المبدأ علة من وجه معلولاً من وجه . فنقول : كما أن لكل واحد من تلك الآحاد معلول، كذلك يكون لمجموعها أيضاً معلول، لأنه ليس عبارة إلا عن الآحاد التي كل واحد منها علة، فمعلوله إما نفسه، أو جزؤه، فيلزم تأخر الشيء عن نفسه بمرتبة أو بمراتب وإما خارج عنه والخارج عن السلسلة التي فرضت متنازلة إلى غير النهاية يكون علة لا معلول له، فينقطع السلسلة. فخلاصة البرهان جار في المعلولات الغير المتناهية أيضاً، وما قيل في وجه عدم الجريان من أنه لو تسلسلت المعلولات من الواجب إلى غير النهاية، فحينئذ يمكن اختيار كون علة الجملة داخلة ولا نسلم أن علة الجملة لا بد أن تكون علة لكل واحد من أجزائها فيما إذا كان بعض أجزاء الجملة غير مفتقر إلى علة أصلاً، أي الواجب كما عرفت . فلا يلزم علية الشيء لنفسه كما في التسلسل في جانب العلة فوهم محض؛ لأنه إجراء للبرهان في جانب العلة، والكلام في إجرائه في جانب المعلول السلسلة، الشيء بنفسه مع أنه لو تصور هذا لزم بطلان الاستدلال إذ على هذا التقدير لم تحتج السلسلة إلى علة خارجة عنها حتى يلزم انقطاعها ويثبت الواجب كما هو المدعى وليس المقصود من الاعتراض إلا هذا. قوله : (هذا إذا كان التسلسل في جانب العلل) أي الفرض بطريق التصاعد. وأما فرض الجملة الثانية مما قبل المعلول، فهو بطريق الأنسبية لا الوجوب، لجواز فرض الجملة الثانية أولاً، وعلى هذا القياس فرض الجملة الثانية مما بعد العلة في إبطال التسلسل من جانب المعلول.
The second aspect from the aspects of disproving regress: We assume from some effect (ascending) ad infinitum a series, and from what is before it finite ad infinitum another series. This is if the regress is on the side of causes. And if it is on the side of effects, we assume from a specific cause (descending) ad infinitum a series, and from what is after it finite ad infinitum another series. So there result two infinite series, one of which exceeds the other by a finite number. Then we superimpose the two series, i.e., one upon the other, from that beginning, i.e., from that side where each has a beginning. So the first of one series corresponds to the first of the other, the second to the second, and so on. Then if corresponding to each of the excess series [there is] one of the deficient series in the number of individuals, all those individuals except... His statement: (not descending in effects, etc.) There is discussion, because if we assume the beginning is a specific cause from which an effect proceeds, and from that another effect, and so on ad infinitum, then each of the individuals is a cause in one respect and an effect in another. We say: Just as each of those individuals has an effect, so too their aggregate has an effect, because it is nothing but the individuals each of which is a cause. So its effect is either itself, or its part, in which case the thing is delayed from itself by one rank or ranks, or it is outside it, and what is outside the series assumed as descending ad infinitum would be a cause without an effect, so the series is cut off. So the essence of the proof applies also to infinite effects. And what is said regarding the reason for its non-application—that if effects were to regress from the Necessary ad infinitum, then one could choose to make the cause of the series internal, and we do not grant that the cause of the series must be the cause of each of its parts when some parts of the series are not in need of a cause at all, i.e., the Necessary, as you know—so self-causation does not follow as in the regress on the side of cause. This is pure fantasy, because it is an application of the proof on the side of cause, while the discussion is about applying it on the side of effects. ... His statement: (This is if the regress is on the side of causes) i.e., the assumption is ascending. As for assuming the second series from what is before the effect, it is by way of suitability not necessity, because it is permissible to assume the second series first. And analogously, assuming the second series from what is after the cause in disproving regress from the side of effects.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against infinite regress. It involves taking two series: one starting from an effect and going upward ad infinitum, and another starting from what is before that effect (finite) and going upward ad infinitum. When superimposed, one series exceeds the other by a finite number, implying a mapping that leads to a contradiction if the excess series has more members. The proof also applies to descending series of effects. The commentary then addresses an objection: in a descending series, each individual is both cause and effect, so the aggregate of these individuals would have an effect which is either itself (leading to self-delay) or external (cutting off the series). Thus the proof holds. A further objection that the necessary being could be internal is refuted, as it confuses application on the side of cause versus effect. The final remark clarifies that assuming the second series is by suitability, not necessity.

بطريق التنازل
جملة غير متناهية
نطبق الجملتين
المبدأ
الزائدة
الناقصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite series can be compared like finite ones, so one-to-one mapping is always possible.
Correction: The presence of a finite extra segment makes one-to-one mapping impossible, as the excess series will have unmatched members.

Checkpoint
What contradiction arises from superimposing the two infinite series in the second proof?
A) The two series are equal in length.
B) One series is finite while the other is infinite.
C) The excess series has more members than the deficient series, but a one-to-one mapping would imply they have the same number.
D) The series have no beginning.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that an actual infinite regress of causes is logically impossible because two such series would lead to a mapping contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If an infinite chain of causes is impossible, then your own existence is not the product of an endless series. What does that mean for the ultimate cause of your being? Are you living as if you have no ultimate purpose, or does this truth press you to seek the First Cause?
?
The proof shows that any addition of a finite extra term breaks the infinity. Consider the finite life you are living. Are you treating it as an endless opportunity, or are you aware that it is finite and leads to an end? How should that awareness change your priorities today?
?
If Allah is the necessary First Cause, then every moment of your existence is sustained by Him. Does your daily life reflect that dependence, or do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What concrete step can you take today to acknowledge your utter need for Him?

Objections and Extensions: The Proof Applied to Descending Series
Extends the proof to descending series and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the second proof applies to descending series of effects and how an objection regarding the cause of the aggregate is refuted.

Core Idea
The proof works for descending series too; the aggregate of effects must have a cause outside the series.

The author now addresses a potential objection: in a descending chain, each member is both cause and effect, so the aggregate might cause itself. He shows this is impossible and reaffirms the proof's validity for both directions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage that begins with the objection about descending effects.
Notice the subtlety: each individual is a cause in one respect and an effect in another.
2
Analyze the argument
The objection suggests the cause of the aggregate could be internal, but the author argues this leads to self-causation or a cut in the series.
3
Understand the refutation
The author shows that the proof indeed applies to descending series, despite the objection, and clarifies the necessity of an external cause.
The final remark distinguishes between necessity and suitability in assuming the second series.

First Argument Against Infinite Regress: Self-Causation
Sanusi presents the first argument against infinite regress via self-causation reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first rational proof that infinite regress is impossible, specifically how it reduces to the absurdity of self-causation.

Core Idea
An infinite regress of causes forces a cause to be its own cause—impossible.

This lesson introduces the classic kalam argument against an infinite chain of contingent causes. Before we can affirm a Creator, we must show that an endless regress of causes is logically impossible. The first argument uses a reductio: assuming a complete chain leads to a cause causing itself, which is absurd.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage Carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: a thesis, a hypothetical, a conclusion, and a final note.
The author uses very precise logical language.
2
Identify the Reductio Structure
Identify the assumption made (that the cause before the last effect is independent creator of the whole chain) and the absurd conclusion drawn (it would be cause of itself). Understand why this is impossible.
Self-causation is logically impossible.
3
Note the Scope Restriction
The author explicitly states that this proof only works for ascending chains of causes, not descending chains of effects. Understand why this distinction matters.
This prevents a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الخامس - المقصد الثامن : التسلسل محال أن يستند الممكن إلى علة قبله بمرتبة واحدة وهكذا ؛ لأنه لو كان ما قبل المعلول الأخير علة موجدة للسلسلة بأسرها مستقلة بالتأثير فيها حقيقة لكان علة لنفسه قطعاً. واعلم أن هذا الدليل إنما يجري في تسلسل الممكنات متصاعدة في العلل لا متنازلة في المعلولات كما لا يخفى على ذي فكرة .
The Fifth Observatory - The Eighth Objective: Infinite regress is impossible: that the contingent should depend on a cause [that is] before it by one rank, and so on; because if what is before the last effect were a cause bringing the entire chain into existence, independent in its influence over it in reality, it would inevitably be a cause of itself. And know that this proof only applies to an ascending regress of contingents in causes, not to a descending one in effects, as is not hidden from one who reflects.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument (al-wajh al-awwal) against infinite regress (tasalsul). It begins by stating the impossibility of an infinite chain where each contingent depends on a cause preceding it by one rank, and so on ad infinitum. The proof is a reductio: if the cause immediately preceding the last effect were the sole and independent creator of the entire chain in reality, then it would be the cause of itself—which is absurd. The argument then notes a limitation: this proof only works for ascending chains (where causes go upwards to a first cause), not for descending chains (where effects go downwards from a cause). The author warns that this is obvious to anyone who thinks. The passage is a concise statement of the classic kalam argument that an infinite regress of causes would entail self-causation, which is impossible.

Key Terms
التسلسل
العلة
المعلول
الممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument also applies to descending chains of effects.
Correction: The author explicitly states that this proof only works for ascending chains of causes, not descending ones.

Checkpoint
Why does the first argument against infinite regress lead to a contradiction?
A) It implies a circle of causes.
B) It entails that something would be the cause of itself.
C) It requires an infinite number of causes.
D) It assumes a first cause exists.

Key Takeaway
The first argument against infinite regress shows that an independent cause of the entire chain would self-cause, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This argument is purely logical, not emotional. But consider: the very ability to reason through this proof is a gift you did not give yourself. When you finish studying this lesson, will you take a moment to thank the One who gave you the mind to understand it? Or will you simply move on to the next task, treating this truth as mere information?

Burhan al-Tatbiq: Correlating Infinite Series to Refute Regress
Introduces Burhan al-Tatbiq and explains its method for two cases of correlation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the method of Burhan al-Tatbiq and how it refutes infinite regress in both causes and effects.

Core Idea
Correlating two infinite series forces them to be finite—that is the proof.

The concept of infinite regress poses a challenge to arguments for God's existence. Theologians developed several proofs against it. The second proof, Burhan al-Tatbiq (Proof of Correlation), is considered the strongest because it applies to many types of series, whether ordered or not. In this passage, the author explains how the proof works by correlating two supposedly infinite series and showing that a contradiction arises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic once, then the translation, to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the two cases that follow the correlation.
2
Understand the setup
Two infinite series are assumed: one exceeding the other by a finite number. This setup is crucial for the proof.
The series can be ascending (causes) or descending (effects).
3
Follow the correlation method
Correlate the two series from a common starting point: match first with first, second with second, and so on.
4
Examine the two cases
Case 1: One-to-one correspondence leads to equality, contradicting excess. Case 2: An unmatched part causes the deficient series to cut off, making it finite.
Either way, the assumption of infinitude fails.
5
Note the scope of the proof
The proof applies to successive and simultaneous, ordered and unordered series. That makes it comprehensive.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه (الثاني) من وجوه إبطال التسلسل (أنا نفرض من معلول (ما ) بطريق التصاعد ) إلى غير النهاية جملة ومما قبله بمتناه إلى غير النهاية جملة أخرى، هذا إذا كان التسلسل في جانب العلل، وإذا كان في جانب المعلولات فرضنا من علة معينة بطريق التنازل إلى غير النهاية جملة ومما بعدها بمتناه إلى غير النهاية جملة أخرى فيحصل هناك جملتان غير متناهيتين إحداهما زائدة على الأخرى بعدد متناه. (ثم نطبق الجملتين ) أي إحداهما على الأخرى ( من ذلك المبدأ أي من ذلك الجانب الذي لكل واحدة منهما فيه مبدأ، فالأول) من إحداهما ( بالأول ) أي بإزاء الأول من الأخرى ) والثاني بالثاني، وهلم جراً. فإن كان بإزاء كل واحد من الجملة ( الزائدة واحد من الجملة ( الناقصة ) في عدة الآحاد كانت الناقصة كالزائدة ) ، أي مساوية لها في عدة الآحاد، ( وهذا خلف . وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن بإزاء كل واحد من الزائدة واحد من الناقصة، ( وجد في الزائدة جزء لا يوجد بإزائه في الناقصة شيء، وعنده ) أي عند الجزء الذي لا يوجد بإزائه شيء من ) تنقطع الناقصة ) بالضرورة فتكون الناقصة (متناهية)، لانقطاعها . ( والزائدة لا تزيد عليها إلا بمتناه كما صورناه ( والزائد على المتناهي بمتناه متناه) بلا شبهة. ( فيلزم انقطاعهما وتناهيهما في الجهة التي فرضناهما غير متناهيتين وغير منقطعتين فيها، هذا خلف. وهذا الدليل هو المسمى ببرهان التطبيق، وهو ( العمدة ) في إبطال التسلسل لجريانه في الأمور المتعاقبة في الوجود، كالحركات الفلكية، وفي الأمور المجتمعة، سواء كان بينها ترتب طبيعي، كالعلل والمعلولات، أو وضعي كالأبعاد أو لا يكون هناك ترتب أصلاً، كالنفوس الناطقة المفارقة.
The second aspect of the ways of refuting infinite regress: (We suppose from a certain effect by way of ascent) ad infinitum a series, and from what is before it, finite, ad infinitum another series – this is when the regress is on the side of causes. And if it is on the side of effects, we suppose from a specific cause by way of descent ad infinitum a series, and from what is after it, finite, ad infinitum another series. Then there result two infinite series, one of them exceeding the other by a finite number. (Then we correlate the two series), i.e., one of them with the other, (from that starting point, i.e., from that side which each of them has a starting point). So (the first) of one series (with the first) i.e., opposite the first of the other, and the second with the second, and so on. If (opposite each one of the exceeding series there is one of the deficient series) in the number of units, then the deficient series (is like the exceeding series), i.e., equal to it in the number of units, (and this is a contradiction). Otherwise, i.e., if opposite each one of the exceeding series there is not one of the deficient series, (then there is found in the exceeding series a part opposite which there is nothing in the deficient series. At that point) i.e., at that part which has nothing opposite it from (the deficient series cuts off) necessarily, so the deficient series is (finite) because of its cutting off. (And the exceeding series does not exceed it except by a finite amount as we have depicted it. (And that which exceeds the finite by a finite amount is finite) without doubt. (So it follows that both cut off and are finite in the direction in which we supposed them infinite and not cutting off – this is a contradiction). And this proof is called the Burhan al-Tatbiq (Proof of Correlation), and it is (the mainstay) in refuting infinite regress because it applies to things that are successive in existence, like celestial motions, and to things that are simultaneous, whether there is between them a natural order, like causes and effects, or a positional order like dimensions, or there is no order at all, like separate rational souls.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage introduces the second proof (al-wajh al-thani) against infinite regress, known as Burhan al-Tatbiq (Proof of Correlation). The method is as follows: For an ascending chain of causes, suppose from a given effect by going upwards ad infinitum we have an infinite series. Then consider another series consisting of everything before that effect (which is finite) but extending to infinity in the opposite direction. This yields two infinite series, one of which exceeds the other by a finite number. Then correlate the two series starting from a common endpoint: match the first of one with the first of the other, second with second, and so on. Two cases arise: either every element of the exceeding series has a counterpart in the deficient series, which would make the deficient series equal in number, contradicting the excess; or there is an element in the exceeding series with no counterpart, at which point the deficient series must cut off and become finite, making the excess finite as well, contradicting the assumption that both are infinite. Thus the original series cannot be infinite. This proof is called the Burhan al-Tatbiq and is considered the chief argument against infinite regress because it applies to both successive entities (like motions) and simultaneous entities (like dimensions or souls), regardless of whether they have a natural order, positional order, or no order at all.

Key Terms
برهان التطبيق
التسلسل
القلة والكثرة
اللامتناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Burhan al-Tatbiq only works for series with a natural order, like causes and effects.
Correction: The proof applies to both successive and simultaneous things, with or without order, as the text explicitly mentions celestial motions, dimensions, and separate souls.

Checkpoint
What is the key feature of Burhan al-Tatbiq that makes it comprehensive?
A) It only works for causes and effects.
B) It applies to both successive and simultaneous things, ordered or unordered.
C) It requires the series to be physically real.
D) It depends on the impossibility of self-causation.

Key Takeaway
Burhan al-Tatbiq refutes infinite regress by correlating two series and showing that either they become equal or one becomes finite, both contradicting the assumption of infinitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just followed a logical proof that infinite regress of causes is impossible. Does your daily life reflect a reliance on a First Cause, or have you been living as if things 'just go back forever'? What would change if you truly internalized that your existence depends on a starting point outside this chain?
?
The proof uses a correlation between two series to expose a contradiction. Consider your own beliefs: are there any assumptions you hold that, if systematically examined, would lead to a similar contradiction? Are you willing to subject your most comfortable ideas to such a test?
?
If an infinite regress is impossible, then the chain of causes that led to your reading this sentence must have a first cause. Does that First Cause have a claim on your life? What would it mean for you to acknowledge that the ultimate starting point is not a thing but the Creator? How would your priorities shift?
